THE RED PAGE.
HIS CHILDHOOD.
I dare not sing of childhood’s days
But that I feel iu their far ways
Old age’s fancy fondly strays
Its joys to reckon ;
And so I make for him who knows
The fragrance purer than of rose
Scenting that time, and ever goes
With farewell beckon.
There wonder met my waking eyes
In suns that always seemed to rise,
Sweet angel-gold, on richest dyes
Of patch-work hoary ;
And though the silk was frayed and torn.
Showing beneath dim words forlorn.
Yet still it gleamed bright with the morn,
And edged with glory !
I heaped my playthings on my bed :
My horseman’s plume of white and red,
The fan for beauty long since dead,
The rose and azure
Of quaintest china—sure the lad
Lonely with these old heirlooms had
Dim murmurings that made him sad
For Time’s erazure.
For Sabbath eventide alone
The child and mother made mild moan,
Close clasped, for grandeurs past and gone,
And cold to-morrow ;
And in her lap the dreamer sighed
. To hear how gentle Jesus died,
With blood-drenched nails and streaming side,
To save us.sorrow.
But, O, that morn at Trinity—
The golden glass where Heaven must be.
The organ-thunders rolling free.
That made him shiver ;
Delight of pricking blistered paint,
Of southernwood the odour faint.
The face of mother, sweetest saint,
And sleep’s full river !
Joyful it was with parchments old,
Last testaments of those ininould.
To play upstairs in sunny gold
From noon to fading ;
But when the candlelight was clear,
The staircase was a place of fear,
Giving on regions waste and drear,
Life’s glory shading.
But sunrise bathed in amber flame
The old park gate, whose globes of fame
Were set for climbing, at the game
Our young strength tasking;
And through the endless summer day
We flew our kites, or joined in play
At marbles, whipt our tops, or lay
On hot flags basking.
What serious businesses we made
Of reeded waters ! Unafraid
We chased the minnows, loved to wade
The crisping shallows :
The wild deserted garden near
Old Roger’s lodge was still more dear,
Though Jenny Greenteeth —mother’s fear—
Lurked in the mallows !
A thousand secrets, sad and sweet,
Mournful as dim enchantments, meet
In one dear name that I repeat
In sacred dreaming:—
Old Penwortham with trees so talk
Grey church and graves, and low worn wall,
And neighbour well witli soft low fall,
To Mary streaming.
But winter, too, had its delight
With hide and seek at close of night,
And scrap-book, screen, and hearth red-bright,
With winds a-blowing;
And druggists’ bottles green and gold,
And crimson, yellow—to behold
Unfailing joy ; and stories told
While skies were snowing !
And through the streets so wild with galesj
The lamplighter left golden trails
Of stars and dreams and haunting tales
For graver-lover ;
Who, as he saw the curtain wave,
Painted his pantomime, or brave
Dark band of pirates in a cave,
At heart a rover.
O, the deep stain of Prussian blue,
Of crimson lake the luscious hue !
My lips with rainbow splashes too
Were thickly tinted :
Both clown and pantaloon were mine,
I felt, and daubed them rich and fine,
But harlequin and columbine
Of something hinted. -
Ah, could I light upon some spell
To summon from each memoried cell
The fairest image I could tell
Of primal pleasure,
Old faces still should live awhile.
Again I’d climb the meadow stile
And yearn-for home, the twilight mile,
And heart’s pure treasure !
On earth’s green lap I used to lie
When buttercups were waving high
And summer flies went floating by
My leafy cover;
And when this round of life is o’er
And I approach that lap once more,
I’ll lay me down as once before,
Sweet earth’s true lover !
T.
J.H.
THE OTHER POET
EOBERT BRIDGES puts forth volume
after volume steadily. The new one,
his sixth of Poetical Works (Smith,
Elder ; 65.), holds two plays—one
“ The Feast of Bacchus”—a pleasant comedy
adapted from Menander through Terence.
Maybe the content is less significant than
the manner. Bridges, who is a learned
authority on English versification, has
adopted “aline of six stresses”—which he
commends as a comic metre that will admit
colloquial speech without torturing it.
Whether the metre is verse or prose is, as
he suggests, not precisely clear. A line or
two may show :
Chr. Good ! son , for though a wife
Is an evil, she is a necessary evil, and one to
which
You will <?et accustomed in time. ’Tis more
respectable too
To he married ; and the Only cure for a temper
such as yours.
With a rest at. “time” and “cure’’the
last two lines come fairly, but the second
seems problematic. However, thus is
raised a new point of Art : which (if one
cease to meditate seriously Life and Death)
is still the only .thing worth thinking about.
The second play is a second part of
“ Nero,” a drama with noble passages that
needs only to be dated from Elizabeth to
earn classic reverence. The plot turns
round the conspiracy of Piso, and exhibits
Nero and Seneca as characteristically as one
inky conceive them, closing with the latter’s
death. Here the metre is traditional, and
Bridges gives it a stately gait. He has the
English heat, and finds a warm body for his
dream. Lines like these ai’e said with an
air :
I ask you, Quintian, as a man of culture and
erudition, what do you make of it ?
The pale Parnassian reptile, that hath ne’er
Moistened his leathery tongue in Hippocrene...
, Nero. —Tell me, Seneca,
Can a good man be happy on the rack ?
Vat. —Not if the rack be good.
Nero’s dream of a new Rome,
Beautiful, incombustible, Neronic !
The ideas of “ Petrouius, a gentleman of
Rome ” (or of any daily paper’s sub-editor) :
... You little think
\\ hat charm the witching night hath for her
lovers;
How her solemnity doth deepen thought,
And bring again the lost Hellenic Muse
To sing from heaven : or on moonlit swards
Of fancy shadows in transfigured scene
The history of man.—Thus, like a god,
I dwell; and take the early morning cries
For calls to sleep; and from divinity
Fall to forgetfulness, while hustling day
Ravages life ; and know no more of it,—
Your riot and din, the plots and crimes of Rome—
Than doth a diver in Arabian seas,
Plunging for pearls beneath the lonely blue :
But o’er my slumbering head soft airs of dream-
land
Rock their wild honey-blooms, till the shy stars
Once more are venturing forth, and I awake.
Bridges, by the way, drops the capital for
adjectives of races : english, latln, Christian.

The Golden 1 Vest is title of a well-illustrated,
badlv-printed account of Westralian mines, men,
and odd matters : nearly 100 pp. folio (R. C. Spear,
Perth, 1,9.) Much labour has been spent upon the
pamphlet, yet much that Interests is hidden
rather than displayed. The illustrations include
portraits of Arthur Bayley,' the Forrest exploring
party of 1869, and others less notable. J
No. 1 of The Gadfly, a 28-pp. illustrated weekly,
appeared in Adelaide on Feb. 14, and delighted
its proprietary syndicate of young journalists by
selling the first edition in the first hour. There
are plenty of pictures and paragraphs in the new
paper ; and certainly Adelaide has room for it if
the advertisers will do their manifest duty.
A second book of verses by Will Lawson, of
Maoriland—with the title Between The Lights— is
announced for publication presently.
Thomas E. Spencer, the last recruit, is a
Londoner just turned sixty, and king of the poets
of “free and accepted masons.” Their odes are
chanted to his inspiring words—their grandmasters
are installed with his exhilarating anthems.
Easily seen how he nourished his humour.
In Feb. Smart Set J. L. Williams reprints and
extends his “Stolen Storydoubtless “the
finest ” newspaper “ story in the world.” Though
written and printed as narrative, it is dramatic
rather than literary : the play it holds should run
a hundred nights in any sensible city. This
character appears more and more in Ameri-
can fiction : which attempts rather to be
life than literature—endeavours to find words
that will give direct contact with the fact, rather
than those that will mirror it beautifully or
fully. In the effort style goes, and...style comes.
Lynch heaps and hurries his words at the crisis as
■ if they were soldiers he was bringing to attack the
reader’s mind. And since he conquers, he must
be conceded military art at least.
R. S. Ross, first editor of Barrier Truth, who
leaves after three years’ service, is son of R. M.
Ross, an old Q. journalist. He made his little
Labour paper one of the most readable in N.S.
Wales, by dint of shining sincerity. 1
The late G. J. Holyoake’s encounter with the
British newspaper stamp laws Was memorable.
By sending out in 1854 30,000 copies of an un-
stamped penny paper called The War Chronicle, he
made himself liable to fines amounting to 600,000 k
Summoned to the Court of Exchequer, he sug-
gested that he should be allowed to clear off the
penalty in “ weekly instalments” ! In the follow-
ing year the stamp duty was repealed. Ilolyoake,
by the way, was the last person tried before an
Eng. jury for blasphemy (1842).

death of M. Grant Duff sets afloat a
I new cargo of the anecdotes that he col-
JL lected for the entertainment of nations.
One :
When Lord Ampthill was 3 r oung he kept a collection of
much-cherished serpents in a room which opened into
his mother’s. “ But don’t you,” someone said to Lady
William, “find that very disagreeable?” “Oh, yes,”
she replied, “ very disagreeable indeed ; but I like dear
Odo to have home ties ! ”
The speaker’s relationship separates this from
the type of blunder or gaffe (such as the Greek
master’s remark to his class :
-Next louo, I wash ; middle, Imsom-ai, I wash my rself—
but this is very rare . . .).
,si3aid by another, it might be sarcasm as neat as
Porson’s for Gibbon :
His industry is indefatigable ; his accuracy scrupulous ;
his reading (which is, indeed, sometimes ostentatiously
displayed) immense ; his attention alwa.ys awake ; his
memory retentive; his st.vle emphatic and expressive;
his periods harmonious. He pleads emphatically for the
rights of mankind, and the duty of toleration, nor does
his humanity ever slumber, unless when women are
ravished, or the Christians persecuted.
Porson, however, was rather heavy than keen.

Scotland in that treasury of good things, The
Harleian Miscellany:
Some are of opinion that, when the Devil shewed our
Saviour the Kingdoms of the Ear.th, he laid his Thumb
upon Scotland, and that for a Twofold Reason : o’irst
Because it was not likely to be an,y Temptation. Next
Being part of his Mother’s Jointure, he could not dispose
of it during her Life.

“JVlay I have your vote?” “I’d sooner give it to the

Another of Grant Duff's stories reminds of
O’Dowd’s verses “God in History,” in which he
tells the fate that has befallen Egypt, Greece,
Rome. Tyre, Carthage, Holland, Spain, for doing
the deeds tha.t Britain does—and tells no more.
What is the name for that figure of omission ?
O’Dowd knew none. It comes again here :
Disraeli went with Dean Stanley-tcta service at West-
minister Abbey. The crowd was great; but the Dean was
recognised, and managed to get his companion a seat
upon the top of a tomb. — : — was preaching. After
listening for a while, Disraeli said, “ It seems to me to be
a very remarkable discourse, but do you think I could
get away?” He was assisted down. “Good-bye,” he
said, “the multitude, the lights, the darkness, the
courtesy were most interesting. Good-bye.”

The following story came years ago as a
Bulletin paragraph, and was subsequently written
by W. T. Goodge into a rhyme for Attorney
Magee, of Millagadee, who would n’t give a
receipt to Chinaman Lee :
Grant Duff’s version refers the yarn to J. R.
Lowell, so probably the original parentage was
American :
Lowell told this morning a story of an American who
was fined for being drunk. When he had paid his money
he asked for a receipt, which was refused as being quite
unnecessary. Again he asked, and it was carefully ex-
plained to him that the amount he had paid was entered
by the judge’s clerk in the court books, and that he would
never hear anything more about the matter.
“ Judge,” said the man, “do 3-0 u believe in a day of
judgment ? ”
“ Yes,” replied the judge ; “we all believe in that.”
“ Well,” rejoined the other, “ on that day it will be
said to me, ‘Jahez Smith, you got drunk.’ ‘Wes, Lord,’
I will answer, ‘ and 1 paid my fine. ‘ Where’s your
receipt?’ it will be said ; and do 3'ou think it reasonable,
judge, under these circumstances and on such a day that
I should be obliged to lose my time by going down to hell
to look for 3 T ou and your clerk?” -

An Athenceum writer says the new Oxford
edition of Blake is the first correct text to be
printed. Yeats’s version is alleged more faithful
than Rossetti’s, “but it rearranges the material
with freedom, omits and emends many poems, and
contains numerous inaccuracies.” It will be seen,
says the Oxford editor, “that scarcely a single
poem or even epigram has been suffered to remain
as Blake wrote, it.”
“Mrs. Flora Annie Steel, having considered
the beasts that perish, has come to the conclusion
that all dogs are better than any man, and that
most men are worse than any pig.”
According to George Braudes, in his last volume
of Main Currents, “ Heine was at one and the
same time a passionate lover of liberty and an
out-and-out aristocrat. He had the freedom-
loving nature’s thirst for liberty, pined and
languished for it, and loved it with his whole soul;
but he had also the great nature’s admiration for
human greatness and the refined nature’s nervous
horror of the rule of mediocrity.”
T.H.A. requests a reference to verses called
“ After Years,” published “five or six years ago,” com-
mencing, “ I met her in a ball-room, all arrayed in silk
attire.”
They tell it of Browning — lbsen—Henry
James; but it has a germ of truth for whomever (of
those kinds) it is told ; and indignant remonstrances on
behalf of Henry-James will not be printed. As Ibsen is
sick and cannot retaliate, it will do to tell of Ibsen—and
Tolstoy. Tolstoy, then, was asked if he understood
Ibsen. “ No,” he didn’t say-, “not his plays ; but then
Ibsen does n’t understand his plays himself. They are
written—yes, it is clear that they are written—and then
Ibsen sits down and waits. Presently come his disciples,
and his commentators, and his critics, and argue about
the meaning. After a while they settle, at least, what
the plays do not mean, and they tell it to Ibsen. Then
he knows.”
In a study just published by Blackwood (6s.
net.) Charles Whibley pays eloquent tribute to
William Pitt the younger. He takes the Tory
side, as Macaulay took the Whig side ; and makes
good his advocacy. His final chapter, “The
Statesman and the Man,” is a summary finely
written. To Pitt’s eloquence and high ability
others have testified ; but one does not remember
seeing Pitt’s practical statesmanship urged as
strongly as Whibley urges it. In truth, he urges
so well that the defence might read like an
attack. “The measures that he passed were
always opportune...” “He even surrendered his
principles, ..69 the exigence of government.” “No
minister has ever employed spies on so vast a
scale or to so good a purpose.’ And in the fol-
lowing passage there is a historical contrast
between writer and statesman that is of perma-
nent application—even in Australia.
But there is another trait in Pitt's character which his
enemies could not forget or forgive. lie lived in a meta-
physical age, and he did not confuse the craft of govern-
ment with metaphysics. There exists a sketch of Pitt,
the joint production of Coleridge and Hazlitt, which
reveals, in its own despite, the strength and restraint of
the great Minister. These two philosophers complain that
Pitt has not left behind him “ a single memorable say-
ing—not one profound maxim—one solid observation-
one forcible description—one beautiful thought—one
humorous picture—one affecting sentiment.’’ They
declare that “ he made no addition whatever to the stock
of human knowledge.” Even if this were’true, it would
convey no reproach to Pitt. It was not his business to
increase the stock of human knowledge, but to govern
the empire. It is not of a statesman that we should
demand “ humorous pictures ” or “ aftetiflg sentiments.”
And Coleridge and Hazlitt plainly showed the bias of
their mind. They regarded him " exclusively from a
literary point of view. They pondered his speeches, and
they found in them no general ideas, no vague plati-
tudes, no kindling phrases, no cheaply made epigrams.
And so confusing a facile management of words witli
policy, they contrasted him to his discredit with tire
leaders of the Whigs. But they overlooked, in their
anxiety to belittle a great man, the true purpose of
government. In their eyes it seemed Pitt’s disgrace that
“ his talents were exactly fitted for the situation in
which he was placed.” Missing in his speeches the “ high-
wrought imagination ” of Burke, the “ vehemence ” of Fox,
“ ease, brilliancy, and acuteness ” of Sheridan, they argued
erroneously that he was deficient in the qualities of true
statesmanship, wh.ereas his modesty of speech proved his
capacity. Fox and Burke and Sheridan could beguile an
audience more cunningly than any of their contempora-
ries. But-had they been placed at the bead of the state
in a time of danger, all their exquisite verbosity would
not have helped them to save the country.
Pitt, on the other hand, was a man of action. Words
were his servants, not his masters. He used them when
they helped him to gain his end ; he did not submit to
their domination in idle vanity. And he was acutely con-
scious of the limits which Coleridge deplored, and which
he deliberately imposed upon himself. He refrained from
“ general ideas,” not because they were beyond his reach,
but because they were not to his purpose. What the
philosophers deemed Iris weakness was, in truth, strength.
His mind preferred induction to deduction. “The
merit of the British Constitution,” said he in a character-
istic phrase, “ is to be estimated not by metaphysical
ideas, not by vague theories, but by analysing it in prac-
tice.” That Coleridge should have thus misunderstood
the genius of Pitt is not surprising. He lived in a dream-
land of speculation, and at the outset was more than half
a Jacobin. But Hazlitt, despite his Jacobinism, knew
the prize-ring and the tennis-court, and ho might have
remembered that statesmanship, like sport, has more
need of deeds than of words.
(Follows one of Baudelaire’s “ Little Poems in Prose,”
trails, by Arthur Symons.]
Be always drunken. Nothing else matters :
that is the only question. If you would not feel
the horrible burden of Time weighing on your
shoulders and crushing you to the earth, be
drunken continually.
Drunken with what? With wine, with poetry,
with virtue, as you will. But be drunken.
And if sometimes, on the stairs of a palace, or
on the green side of a ditch, or in the dreary soli-
tude of your own room, you should awaken and
the drunkenness be half or wholly slipped away
from you, ask of the wind, or of the wave, or of
the star, or of the bird, or of the clock, of what-
ever flies* or sighs, or rooks, or sings, or speaks,
ask what hour it is; and the wind, wave, star,
bird, clock, will answer you: “It is the hour to
be drunken ! Be drunken, if you would not be
martyred slaves of Time; be drunken continu-
ally ! With wine, with poetry, or with virtue, as
you will.”
A.G.S.

HIS SEPULCHRE.
With book and friend let me grow old,
And draw a quiet breath,
As shadows lengthen and the slope
Runs down to dusty death,
Witli open eyes for those pure forms
Haunting the heart of things,
And ears for secret charities
That float on angel wings.
(So pass my life until there come
From far the last sad call,
And understanding I will go
From library and stall,
From sunny pavement written o’er
With names of friends at rest,
To watch through woodland glades the deer
Speed to the mountain breast.
Then after long farewell—the last!
And parting with the sun,
With bird and flower and noble tree,
In calm of labour done,
Within the solitary hills,
By lonely churchyard gate,
And low embattled tower and wall,
God’s kindly time I’ll wait...
There sheep would feed around my grave,
The scythe flame in the corn,
The lark rise singing to the blue,
The peewit call forlorn :
O, nameless there would I be laid
In that green quiet place,
Not without tears —and so farewell
Mute unresisting face.
T.
J.H.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Tasmanian Idyll.
O, Apple-land o’ leisure,
Here’s a patch o’ rustic pleasure
That my fancy deems unequalled in this Garden
o’ the Seas;
And Contentment is the maiden
Who owns the orchard laden
With the blossoms that Pomona stole from Eden’s
apple trees.
Sweet Phyllis, for your buckle
There’s the quaint old honeysuckle ;
I stole it for your girdle from the self-assertive
bee;
O, and marigolds for asking—
In scented sunbeams basking—
’Mid infant grasses tesselate a ruddy path for me.
O, it’s sweet and isolated,
And with perfume it is mated;
Each butterfly is drunken with the od’rous nectar
brew ;
While zephyrs light are skimming
The cream just over-brimining
Those cups Perfection chisell’d when old Paradise
was new.
O, Apple-land o’ leisure,
To this patch o’ rustic pleasure
Would the Lotus-eater, blase of Contentment, ask
the keys;
Within her bower o’ dreaming
He’d see thro’ blossoms gleaming
Elysium located here among the apple trees.
tas.
M. LEGRAND.
The Laggard.
To-morrow I will comfort him ;
To-morrow I will soothe her pain ;
Then on the tide I idly swim
And do not think of them again.
His sorrow goes uncomforted,
Undried the sad tears that she shed.
To-morrow I will rule the world ;
To-morrow I will conquer Fate ;
Then I forget the challenge hurled
And dally idly at the gate ;
And so the world knows not my name
Nor will Fate recognise my claim.
To-morrow I will turn to God ;
To-morrow I will sin atone ;
So do I dream until the sod
Hides vain resolves and deeds undone,
Ami jtiea' . and Earth withhold their grace
From me, the la. "ard in the race.
N.s.w.
DOROTHY M'CRAE.
A Complaint.
I lounged beneath the wilga’s shade
And judged the poet’s stock-in-trade—
The emerald glade, the gay cascade,
The crumbling shrine, and hedges green.
Antique and sere, their metres lack
The dash of winds across the track
Of vanished tides, whose saline wrack
Still marks the ebb of errant seas.
Unknown to them, the sparkling hue
Of Southern skies, whose circling blue
Seems striving e’er to gird anew
The far-flung plain’s infinity.
The brolga’s call at break of day,
The subtle scent, the gentle sway
Of eucalypts when breezes play,
Await a newer minstrelsy.
Our world is new; no mouldering shrine
Nor ruined fane our ways align ;
No sated vaults do undermine
Our faith in human potency.
Our world is young ; the winds of God
Untainted, with the sunlight shod,
O’er pathless wild and plains untied
Bring messages of mystery.
On lands of moor and fen and wold,
Where earth is nought but carcase-mould,
The shaft that fairly strikes the gold,
With us, has sped the bow in vain.
Alas ! the poet’s stock in trade—
The hedges green, the bosky glade—
Reviewed beneath the wilga’s shade
Suggests a mild futility.
n.s.w.
A.P. ROGERS.
Respite.
Here, where the night is cool and gracious,
The earth-scent keen on the dewy air,
One may dream that the world is spacious,
All tracks rose-bordered and smooth and fair.
A surge takes hold of his blood and marrow,
And thrills his pulses and charms his brain,
And bids him forth from the byways narrow,
Where men and women grow old in pain.
He knows the call that the birds have chorused,
He understands what the winds would say ;
He sees far off in an ancient forest
A hand that summons his soul for aye.
He turns once more (as a stream diverted
May turn again to the course it ran)
To God-made ways that his feet deserted
To tread ways made by the hand of man.
He dreams of a camp-fire red and lonely,
Where, head on arm, he awoke to hear
A low cry sound, and a low cry only,
Of some wild lion that prowled a-near.
A far clock strikes, and the dreamer’s vision
Returns to earth at the warning sound ;
The forest fades, and he seeks his prison—
A toiler tired of his daily round.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.

llotv to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager, 'l’o send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY. MARCH 8, 1906.
FEDERAL LAND TAX, OLD AGE
PENSIONS, &c.
Next session the Australian Parliament is ex-
pected to commence dealing with some financial
problems of considerable tonnage. Two of them,
at least —the Federal Land Tax and the Federal
Old Age Pension system—are matters in which
the Democratic party is deeply interested, and if
that party wants to make a good showing it requires
to glue its eye hard and early on the ways and
means of the case. The Conservatives of the Aus-
tralian Parliament are under no such necessity.
They don’t advocate a Federal graduated land-
tax, therefore they don’t require to consider how
it would interfere with existing State land-taxes
or other branches of State finance; and they don’t
seriously propose any system of Australian old
age pensions, therefore they are under no obliga-
tion to show how the money for these pensions
can be raised. The Conservatives are in the easy
position of being able to sit back and criticise,
and—should the chance be allowed them—scoff a
proud, superior scoff about the financial ignorance
and incapacity of their opponents. The Demo-
crats, who advocate both the Federal old age
pension system and the Federal graduated land-
tax, have on their hands the heavy job of con-
structing a financial scheme into which these two
things will fit.
* * # *
A Federal old age pension system, run on fairly
generous lines, will certainly cost more than would
a State system run on equally generous lines. The
Federal system will make so many years residence
in the Commonwealth an essential condition,
therefore it will take in thousands of nomads who
have not qualified for a State pension by the
required length of residence in any one State. A
Federal old age pension system will probably in-
crease the financial load a little as regards N.S.W.,
though that State has the most generous pension
system in the Commonwealth. It will greatly
increase the burden as regards Victoria, for that
State has an impossibly mean system, under which
an old age pension is rather an insult than an
assistance. And it will impose a completely new
burlen as regards the other States. Two of
these—Queensland and S. Australia —have no old
age pension system because they are the most
deeply indebted States, per inhabitant, on this
earth, and are too “stony” to consider any new
expenditure at present. Tasmania has none
because it is rather poor, very much mismanaged,
and doesn’t want to lay any adequate tax burden
on its big landlords. Westralia has none because,
though it is rich, its incredible extravagance
leaves it in a state of constant deficit. The
Bulletin offers these figures as a rough estimate :
Cost of Probable Cost
Present Old of Federal Old In-
Age Pensions. Age Pensions. crease.
N.S. Wales £508,000 £600,000 £92,000
Victoria.. .. 205,000 480,000 275,000
Queensland .. Nil 210,000 210,000
S. Australia .. Nil 149,000 149,000
Westralia .. Nil 98,000 98,000
Tasmania .. Nil 72,000 72,000
£713,000 £1,609,000 £896,000
These figures are arrived at by making what is
thought to be a reasonable allowance for the in-
crease in the N.S.W. old age pension expendi-
ture, through the inclusion of the old nomads who
would be qualified by residence in the Common-
wealth, but who are not qualified for the State
pension by sufficiently lengthened residence in
the State; and then charging the other States
according to population on the same scale. The
figures show the folly of the easy-going assumption
put forward as one way of shirking the financial
difficulty, that there is no serious need to provide
funds for Federal old age pensions, because it is
merely a question of turning a State liability into
a Federal one.
« * * *
The proceeds of a Federal graduated land-tax
are much relied upon to provide the means for
paying old age pensions, but even this source of
income under present conditions isn’t all it seems.
Assuming that the Australian Parliament does its
work properly, it will impose a tax that will cover
the whole ground—not a mere trifle that can be car-
ried as an addition to existing State imposts, but
a tax which will compel the States to repeal their
existing imposts. It would be mere waste of men
and office expenses to have two separate land-
taxes collected by two separate authorities. That
tax will yield a large income to begin with, but if
it is properly devised, and if it does the work for
which it is chiefly intended by breaking up the big
estates, it will be a declining income, and will be-
come small by degrees and beautifully less. It
will even tend to become beautifully nil. Mean-
while a question will arise about recompensing
certain of the States for the loss of their State
land-taxes, and if so much of the land-tax derived
from any one State is returned to that State, then
a similar refund will be demanded by all the other
States. Let it be supposed that a properly gradu-
ated land-tax, with something extra on absentees,
and with a fair exemption as regards the small
owner, yields £625,000 a year in N.S.W. ; this is
probably all—perhaps much more than all—it will
yield after the breaking-up process has fairly set in.
And let it be supposed that it yields, in proportion
to population, a similar amount in the other
States —a very rough mode of calculation, but one
which may prove reasonably accurate after all—
these would, be about the results, based on Cogh-
lan’s figures for 1903-4:
Present
- Land-tax Federal
(about). Land-tax. Increase.
N.S. Wales .. £322,000 £625,000 £303,000
Victoria .. .. 106,000 500,000 394,000
Queensland .. Nil 220,000 220,000
S. Australia .. 77,000 155,000 78,000
Westralia .. .. Nil 102,000 102,000
Tasmania .. .. 50,000 75,000 25,000
£555,000 £1,677,000 £1,122,000
These figures probably show things in too favor-
able a light, but taking them as they stand they
produce some curious results. S. Australia,
for instance, is the most beggared, the most
barren, the most involved, and the most drought-
cursed of the States. Its present State revenue
leaves no margin for old age pensions or anything
else, and it has a right to claim that the Austra-
lian Government, if it insists on paying the pen-
sions, shall also find the money. Also, its State
Treasury can’t do without the proceeds of the pre-
sent land-tax—in fact, it requires a great deal
more land-tax to keep it solvent. Yet if S.A. is
Compensated out of the Federal land tax, the other
States will demand a similar allowance, and will
doubtless get it; Tasmania, which is in the same
financial hole as S.A., will coibunly make its
demand very strongly. And if S.A. gets bade
half its share of the Federal land-tax to save it
from bankruptcy, and the other Slaics receive a
similar allowance, then the biggest land-tax that
there appears to be any hope of collecting isn’t
likely to more than half cover the cost of old age
pensions. Yet if the appropriation for old age
pension purposes of the entire proceeds of land-
taxation by the Federal authority left S.A. and
Tasmania insolvent, Sheol itself wouldn’t hold the
row that would ensue.
* * * *
Very little can be done at present in the way of
finding money for old age pensions out of Customs
and Excise duties, for, while the Braddon clause
lasts—and it lasts a good while yet—to raise even
£500,000 in this way to aid the pension fund
would necessitate raising £2,000,000, and giving
£1,500,000 back to the States—both those that
need it and those that do not. And yet, in deal-
ing with the difficulties in the way of raising the
money for old age pensions, only one difficulty has
been touched upon. It is just as necessary to
raise at least another £1,000,000 or more per
annum for defence as to raise £1,600,000 a year
for pensions, and the raising of £250,000 or
£500,000 a year in reduction of the public
debt is as essential as any of them.
The trouble is that every time the Aus-
tralian Government tries to do anything large
and urgent the shadow of the Poorest States
(which are also the Most Indebted States) falls
upon it—to say nothing of the shadow of the
Richest and most Cantankerous State. And even
when the Braddon clause expires, and the Aus-
tralian Government is no longer legally bound to
keep the Poorest States afloat by returning three-
fourths of the tariff revenue, there will be a great
moral necessity to keep them out of bankruptcy,
because if one State ever gets bankrupt the con-
sequent panic in London, and the consequent flat
refusal to renew any more Australian loans, will
mean general insolvency. The Poorest States,
mostly through their own past and present bad
management, can’t easily afford to pay their share
of old age pensions or of a proper defence system.
Yet if part of their share of these outlays is re-
mitted and returned to them to keep them out of
bankruptcy, there wili be corresponding demands
from other States, including the Most Cantanker-
ous State, which don’t really require the money,
but which are agitated about their State Rights.
These things are calculated to paralyse any
attempt at doing something large and energetic
for the good of Australia —at least while there is
a painfully affable Government in office.
* * * *
The short way oat of the difficulty is to cut all
connection between Federal and State finance.
That could best be done by an amendment of the
Constitution authorising the Commonwealth to
take over all the State debts bodily as they stand.
Such an act would end all refunds of Customs and
Excise revenue. It would enable the Common-
wealth to retain the whole revenue raised by new
tariff imposts, and to turn a deaf ear to all demands
by the State Governments for a share of the Federal
land-tax or absentee-tax or any other tax. At
present the Commonwealth is always liable to be
bailed up by the Poorest States with the cry : “If
you do this or that the reduction in our
State revenue is liable to make us insol-
vent. If we are even seriously threatened
with insolvency the chances are that London, in
its panic, will refuse to subscribe the money to
renew the loans of any Australian State ; and
even the most solvent of them—even N.S.W. and
Victoria—will go down like a stone if its loans
are not renewed in London.” Of course, the fact
has always existed that the default of one Austra-
lian State would pull down the rest, but until re-
cently there was no Australian Government which
could be blamed for this State muddlement, or on
which the responsibility could be shifted. Now
there is an Australian Government, and every
time it attempts to do anything large and neces-
sary it is liable to stumble over the corpse of the
Poorest or the Most Muddled State, which thinks
it can’t afford to pay its share of the defence ex-
penditure or the old age pension expenditure,
which wants its share of the money they will cost
to keep its debt and its incapacity afloat, which
is liable to burst if it doesn’t get the money,
and which will pull down all the other States if it
does burst. Of course, even if the Commonwealth
took over the existing State debts, that wouldn’t
pre vent the States contracting new debts, but for
these the Australian Government need take no
responsibility, and their renewal wouldn’t affect
this generation, at all events.
* # * #
If the Constitution were amended to allow the
State debts to be taken ove. 1 bodily on a date to
be fixed by proclamation, N.S.W., where the debt
per head is just about the s&ne as the average
debt per head of the Commonwealth, would hardly
gain or lose anything. Queensland, S. Australia,
and Westralia would gain by the general levelling
up; Victoria and Tasmania would lose. (The
Northern Territory has such an incredible debt—
several hundred pounds per head —that it is only
included in this proposition on the assumption
that the whole Territory would be handed over
along with its liabilities, as an estate in liquidation.)
There is a fair chance that a majority of the States
and a majority of the total electors would assent
to the required alteration of the Constitution.
And, as a matter of fact, it is probable that very
little injustice would be done, despite the
apparent discrepancies. Tasmania, for instance,
owes only £53 per inhabitant against Queensland’s
£79, and just now a combination of the two debts
would make Tasmania a loser. But Queensland's
population is growing fast, and that of Tasmania
is not, and by the time the increase of population
had , educed Tasmania’s federated debt to £4O a
hea i that of Queensland might easily be down to
£3O. And if the Australian Parliament chose to
adopt the Canadian banking system, and raise
£8,000,000 or £10,000,000 in gold, free of interest, by
compelling the banks to hold at least 40 per cent,
of their cash reserves in Federal notes, it could
found, with the proceeds, a sinking fund which
would steadily reduce the debts till they vanished
altogether. Even the State which lost most at the
moment by the consolidation of the debts, and by
sharing the burdens of its poorer neighbors, would
be more than compensated by getting its debts
ultimately paid off at no expense whatever.
* * * *
The consolidation of the State liabilities is
really the first essential in Australian politics. It
comes before the Federal old age pension law and
before the Federal graduated land-tax. In a
sense it even comes before the defence question.
Australia should certainly make a very early com-
mencement at founding its arms and ammunition
factories, training its men, creating its ship-build-
ing yards, building its fleet, and establishing its
iron-works, but it should also set about the con-
solidation of the State debts and the establish-
ment of a sinking fund before the bills for new
defence works begin to come in heavily. Other-
wise it will be hampered at every turn by the
cry of the Poorest States, which are also
the Most Indebted States, and generally the
Most Muddled and Incompetent States. It has
to consider their poverty and incompetence lest
they become bankrupt, and create a financial
panic which will pull down their neighbors.
When it considers them it has to consider the
other States equally, or else the spectre of State
Rights will arise an,' glare at it like a buggy lamp
on a dark night. L. has to go slow in all its
financial dealings, so , hat the lamest member of
the expedition can keep up. If it cut off all
financial connection with the State Governments,
and made it impossible for them to raise the
bogey of insolvency by leaving them no State
debt to go insolvent on, the Commonwealth would
have to face an interest bill which would at first
absorb most of its Customs and Excise revenue.
But it could always raise what money it wanted
by increased duties, by graduated land-tax,
income-tax, absencee-tax, or any other means.
The increased strength and prestige of the Com-
monwealth when it was freed from the irritating
and aggravating financial dictation of the State
Governments would, in itself, be worth paying a
great deal for. And the State Governments,
with their railway and land revenues to depend
upon, could potter away peacefully in the back-
ground and say nothing. Hitherto they have
been saying a great deal too much.
THE TRAGIC N.S.W. LANDS MUDDLE.
For a few brief moments during its recent sittings
the N.S.W. Lands Commission got away from the
squalid atmosphere of bribes and corrupt conces-
sions to take a glimpse of the tragedy be-
hind all the minor incidents—the great
tragedy of the muddle which has made the
land history of Australia, and of N.S.W. in par-
ticular, so deplorable. When N.S.W. was first
settled there was ample knowledge available to
guide the original colonists to a sensible land sys-
tem. Without presuming that they should have
had a knowledge of the land problems which had
vexed Jerusalem, Athens and Rome, they at least
knew—or should have known —of the evils which
had afflicted Britain through landlordism, the
consequences of which were just at that time so
apparent. In France the “single tax” had been
already long discussed, and the Revolution had
swept the land titles of the nobility into the
dust-bin. All Europe was seething with discus-
sion of the land question. But even dim echoes
of the discussion do not seem to have reached to
the far-off convict colony in Australia, where
flogged and floggers, prisoners and their warders
festered in a common sink of brutality. As
soon as civilisation in any . real sense
began to dawn the Government of the new
country began to perpetuate the worst evils of the
landlordism which nad afflicted Europe, and
which had but recently drenched that continent
in blood. Any land in Australia for which there
was a demand was parcelled out among the friends
of the administration. With pathetic fidelity to
the old kingly traditions of William the Con-
queror and other such royal freebooters, one area
was given to a vigorous fighter, another to a
skilful flatterer, this to an importunate priest,
that to a disgraceful woman. Convicts shared
in the division; those who had shown
special slavishness to their warders, or
who had displayed skill and industry as
hangers and floggers rejoiced in grants. Thus
early a grotesque band of landed proprietors was
formed (soon to seek, through one of their number,
official recognition of their land-wealth by being
dubbed peers !), and the quaint rabble quickly
added to its gross .Rabelaisian humorousness by
the recruits it attracted. Many soldier or
convict grantees disposed of their broad acres
to publicans for enough rum to see them over
the Styx, and the publicans joined the ranks
of “landed gentry.” Other worthy squires
found their titles in marrying the leman of
some person in authority, and the complaisant
husband of a “Red Rover ’’female “founded a
family.”
• * * *
Begun thus worthily, the N.S.W. Lands
administration has never lost a savor of disreput-
ableness. But as the free population increased
and the old convict stain was rubbed out, many
honest settlers crowded to the rich acres of the
new continent. Along the river flats the ploughs
of honest farmers began to cut the soil. Across
the inland plains crept the flocks of the pioneer
pastoralists, “squatting” where they listed. Soon
the first call came of lack of land. Would-be
farmers found their way blocked by the “squat-
ters,” and cried out for land reform. Had there
been a statesman in N.S. Wales then, the bold
reform of undoing the mischief which had been al-
ready done, and nationalising all the land of the
State to hold it for the benefit of the people, would
have suggested itself. Resumption of what had
been already alienated would then have been a very
easy matter, and would have made possible an or-
ganised development of the agricultural and pas-
toral possibilities of the country. But folly and
the desire to pander to popular clamor swung the
politicians in exactly the opposite direction. Far-
mers were clamoring for land. The squatters,
holding huge areas on lease, would not open up to
the small settler. So the whole State was practi-
cally thrown open for “ free selection.” On a
leasehold area any person might select a farm.
* * * *
The effect of this disastrous bit of politics was,
in truth, far worse than that of the early gifts—■
disgraceful as they were. The great bulk of the
State was at the time only suitable for the grazier.
But every grazier was put at the mercy of the
selector, who might come and take away from him
the vital areas of his run, thus making the rest
useless. In self-defence the grazier employed
“dummy selectors ” to take up land on his behalf.
Thus began the sordid tragedy of land dishonesty.
With each succeeding Lands Act the old iniquity
has been patched a little, reformed a little, but
always left in the main intact, because vested
interests had so grown under it that a giant reso-
lution was needed to grapple with the problem on
really honest lines. The effect'of the “ free selec-
tion ” legislation may be accurately represented by
a typical concrete example. Squatter A. had origi-
nally 100,000 acres on lease, for which a fair rent was
£2OOO a year. After a degrading struggle with
selectors, dummies, and Crown land agents, he
emerges with h’s 100,000 acres freehold and a
debt of £BO,OOO. Some of the money has gone to
the State in “free selection” instalments, and
has been spent on riotous living; much of it
has gone to blackmailers, dummies, corrupt poli-
ticians and civil servants, and in interest to
money-lenders. Now Squatter A., with his land
secured at a price which is really above its true
value, is practically only a caretaker for some finan-
cial institution. All that he has got out of the
struggle is his bread and butter and a nasty taste
in the mouth as he thinks of the maze of perju-
ries and corruptions through which he has
wandered.
* * » *
Since that first “free selection” Act there has
been much land legislation in New South Wales
—most of it designed to ameliorate the condition
of the man on the soil, or the man wishing to
get on the soil, but all having practically the
same effect of making the supply of land to
the intending agriculturist mostly a gamble,
and of forcing the pastoralist into a fresh
series of briberies and evasions. What has
never been recog uised is that there is plenty of
room in the Sta< j—and will be many generations
hence —for botii grazier and agriculturist. The
politicians have always persisted in representing
the two as mutually hostile and the blackmailer
has fleeced both in turn. The position calls for
sympathy for the squatter, deeper sympathy for
the agriculturist forced out into the droughty
country to grovel for a living in the dust, for the
unhappy land-hungry man who wanders from
ballot to ballot in search of a farm, and for this
misfortunate country which finds its land wealth so
woefully mismanaged.
* * * #
Imagine if, at the time of the John Robertson
Act, the sensible, instead of the most absolutely
senseless, action had been taken. The bulk of
the State’s area was then on lease. The trouble
was that most of the known good land on the
coastal belt had been grabbed by monopolists,
who were holding “for a rise,” and thus blocking
settlement. If the small area then in private
hands had been resumed—at a fair price—and
room found for the farmer on the coast without
disturbing the pastoralist in the interior ; if, then,
the area of agricultural settlement had been
slowly extended as the demand grew, county after
county being devoted to the farmer as need
arose—the land position would be now this.
The pastoralists would hold their areas on
lease, not freehold ; would have been spared the
enormous expenditure in “ dummying,” and con-
sequently would have been able to spend more
on improvements. Agricultural settlement would
be thick in a few districts instead of thin in many,
and in the bulk would be enormously greater than
now, for the prospect to the young farmer in a
thickly-settled, fairly-fertile district is vastly dif-
ferent from the prospect of holding an outpost in the
waterless interior. The administrative expenses
of the State would be very much less, since there
would be no remote settler to follow up with a
public school, and a road, and a policeman. What
mighfc- have been done then could be still done
now, though with enormously greater expense
and trouble ; and, probably, will have to he
done one day, when private ownership of land
has been proved a failure. Certainly with every
effort to make things better with a new Land
Act, the situation is made worse. Free selection,
“classification,” the opening of half the pastoral
areas to selection, exchanges, repurchase for closer
settlement, improvement leases, all the great
“reforms” have led only to new swindles. The
man on the land is still harassed for backsheesh.
The man who wishes to go on the land still finds
his path blocked.


THE MORT’S DOCK PROBLEM.
The Mort’s Dock Co., Sydney’s biggest ship-
repairing concern, and also its biggest concern in
many other lines, recently made, through its
manager, a complaint that should set the public
thinking considerably. The co. builds little
vessels—vessels that are too small to be sent out
here ready-made—and builds them as well as any
shipbuilding firm in Europe or America. It has
Protection in that line of industry; Cape Horn,
and the Cape of Good Hope, and the Bay of Bis-
cay, and the Great Australian Bight are each
equal to 500 per cent, duty on small vessels. It
does a good deal of repairing ; if a ship is too
badly battered to do the voyage to Europe or
America, it has to be repaired here, for repairs
can’t be imported. As regards the building of
large-sized vessels, it finds it can do nothing at
all. There is a miserable per cent. Protec-
tion on some machinery, but in view of the fact
that the labor employed in making machinery
.costs about 87 per cent, more in Australia
than it does in England, it is only in special cases
that there is anything to be done in the business. As
regards the ships themselves, the imported vessel,
Ibuilt in Britain or Germany or France or Japan
or America, and purchased for service on the
Australian coast, comes in free. Therefore, while
skilled labor employed in building ships costs
Bd. an hour in England, and Is. 3d. an hour in
Australia, the prospects of this industry are abso-
lutely nil. In this respect it is like a good many
other industries.
* * * *
Under these circumstances it is reasonable to
ask the Freetrade section of the Labor party if it
really wants to prevent new industries arising in
Australia. There are three political parties in-
volved in the discussion, and anyone who con-
siders them can see at once why the Freetrade
Laborists are singled out as the people to be ques-
tioned in this fashion. The parties are
(1) The Tory Freetraders who want to create manufac-
tures without Protection by cutting down wages.
(2) The Protectionist Labor men and other Protectionist
Democrats who want to keep wages up, and to make
manufactures possible by levying sufficiently heavy duties
on products imported from low-wage countries.
(3) The Freetrade Labor men who want to keep wages
up, and who yet demand that this country shall compete
on even terms with imports from low-wage countries ; in
-other words, the men who demand an impossibility.
There are therefore two sections of the com-
munity which propose a remedy for the pre-
sent industrial stagnation. One proposes to
find a remedy at the cost of our own people ;
the other at the cost of the foreigner. The
Labor Freetrader alone doesn’t propose to
find any remedy, and thereby he brands himself
as one of two things —a fool or a public enemy.
Also he has already branded himself as a shuffler
of a very bad order. There was a time, under the
old State regime , when the mostly-Freetrade
Labor party held the balance of power in N.S.W.
Parliament, and dictated to a professedly Free-
trade Government in office, and kept that
professedly Freetrade Government in office.
In those days it affected to believe, just
as it does now, that high wages, short
hours, and Freetrade are compatible things,
and that it is not only possible for the
country to exist under them, but that they will
lead the country to prosperity. But if it had de-
manded, and secured, the abolition of the Protec-
tion granted to the great sugar, wine, beer, and
-tobacco industries they would simply have disap-
peared, and it apparently knew this so well that it
•didn’t attempt to apply its doctrine of Freetrade,
.high wages and short hours to these industries.
And it apparently knew that if it had succeeded
in applying its doctrine of fair wages and reason-
able hours to the N.S.W. farming industry, and
.had also secured the abolition of the huge bonuses
•or rebates or surreptitious Protection granted to
'that industry by the carriage of its products on
'the railways at far less than cost, the agricultural
industry would mostly have crumpled up; so it
left the agricultural industry pretty much alone.
It kept things going in certain industries by
winking at high Protection with one eye, and at
long hours and poor wages with the other. That
kept both its eyes busy winking.
* * * *
It is a reasonable thing to ask the Freetrade
Labor section to do definitely one of two things.
If it really believes that Freetrade, respectable
wages and fair hours can go together, it should
act accordingly. It should go solidly in Federal
politics for the removal of all duties that can be
spared, and demand that those which are neces-
sary for revenue purposes shall be balanced by
equal excise duties on local products, so that they
shall cease to be protective. In State politics it
should demand that the surreptitious Protection
given to the N.S.W. farmer by carrying his pro-
duce on the railways at a mere fraction of the
rates charged on most other goods, and at much
below the real cost of carriage, shall be abol-
ished. and that agriculture shall no longer be
subsidised at the public expense. And it should
try to find out some way of securing for the farm
worker—the worker in an unprotected agricul-
tural industry—something approaching as good
wages and hours as the city artisan enjoys. Also it
should put some restriction on the enslaving of the
cocky’s small son as an unpaid toiler on his
father’s farm, seeing that there are severe restric-
tions on the employment of child labor in trades.
If it succeeds in doing these things, and the in-
dustries involved keep progressing, it will have
proved its case to a very fair extent. But if it
hasn’t the courage to attempt these things, then
it should either join the Tory Freetraders, and let
city industries progress, as agriculture does, by
long hours, low pay, and a little surreptitious
Protection; or it should join the Protectionist
Democrats and admit that a high-wage country
can’t progress unless it shuts out the competing
products of low-wage countries.
* * * *
The Freetrade Tory party is, in this matter,
but in another direction, j ust as great a fool as
the Freetrade Labor party. The latter professes
to think that free imports, high wages and pro-
gress can go together. The former fancies that
free imports, low wages and high rents can
go together. If the Freetrade Tory got the
low wages he craves after, the low wage artisan
would begin to live with his family in one, or at most
two rooms, as his luckless brethren have to do in the
-British Isles. The four-roomed cottage would
R’ 0 li t u° ° r tliree or f° ur families instead of one.
I ully half of each Australian city would cease to
be wanted, and rents and valuations would slump
oO per cent., and property-owners and mortgagees
ruld have to write off half the value of their
holdings as a bad debt. And the slump would be
permanent, not temporary, for when new build-
ings could be put up by the new cheap artisan at
4s. a day the old buildings, put up by the artisan
at 10s. or more per day, would have to be written
fi? W m ln va^ue to the new level. But the folly of
the lory Freetrade cheap labor individual is a
side issue. He is an incurable case, and has to be
.treated as such. The Freetrade Labor person,
it is hoped, is not incurable. Still, is is up to
him to answer four questions :
(1) Is he prepared to apply his doctrine of decent
wages, fair hours, and Freetrade —real Freetrade, that
is—to all industries, with a firm belief that it will lead
to progress and prosperity ?
(2) Or, is he willing to give to every industry the same
liberal Protection that he has winked at so persistently
in the case of wine, beer and tobacco, and to give the
Australian manufacturer the same surreptitious aid in
the way o' "heap railway freights that he has not objected
to in the case of the farmer?
(3) If he is going to continue winking at long hours,
low wages, ai.d cheap child labor in the agricultural
industry, will ne wink equally hard in the case of manu-
factures ?
(4) If for some reason he imagines that nc should strain
his Freetrade and Labor principles to keep up the wine,
beer, sugar and tobacco trades and the agricultural
industry', yet that he should not strain them to found
shipbuilding and other industries, then will he explain the
reason of the different - > A difference of that size calls
for explanation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Where the White Can’t Live.
John See, E. W. Cole, Bruce Smith, and their
friends of the White-men-can’t-live-in-tlie-North
party, ought to study a book, lately issued in a
hard, official blue cover, and branded “ Vital
Statistics, 1904: Queensland,” much, if not most,
of Queensland being in that north where the poor
white man can’t live. In 1904, says the Govern-
ment Statist, the death-rate was very satisfac-
tory ; and it would have been a lot more satis-
factory if Cole’s colored friends, who are supposed
to simply revel in that climate, had not insisted
upon dying in rather startling numbers. Dividing
the population into the streaks of which it is com-
posed, this was the death-rate: —
Kanakas .. .. 20’49 per 1000
Chinese .. .. 10 ‘l4 ~
Other Asiatics .. 10'03 ~
White people .. 9'84 ~
So the white people who can’t live in the north
have a mortality experience three times more
favorable than the Kanaka, who is represented
as being the very man for the place and the job.
Put in another way, it means that in 1904
one in every 38 Kanakas died; while among
white people only one out of every 102
died. And even that doesn’t represent the
full extent of the superior suitability of the white
man for life in Queensland. The white popula-
tion there is, to the usual extent, an infant crying
out for the moon and milk ; and everywhere the
infant’s hold on life is feeble. But the Kanaka
birth-rate is small; in 1904 oidy 43 infants were
born, or 5‘2 per 1000 of the Kanaka population,
while the white race birth-rate was 28'12
per 1000. The “white” death statistics
should, therefore, be much worse than the
kanaka rate, because of this vastly larger
proportion of white infants hanging on to life by a
thin thread that a curd of sour milk may break
yet, as a matter of fact, the “white” death statistics
are nearly three times better than the Kanaka
statistics. The same rule ought to apply to the
Chinese and other Asiatic figures; the “white”
figures, because they include the large infant mor-
tality, should be worse in both cases. And yet
they are better; given the white infant in-
remembering how handicapped it often is by the
remoteness of its parents’ homo from medical and
nursing skill—the white population has three or
four times a better and firmer hold upon life in.
Queensland than the Kanaka or the Asiatic.
Which looks like a pretty strong tip from Nature
that not the yellow or the black but the white is
the man for that country, unless it is Nature’s
idea that the right man for a place is the man who-
has the best chance of dying there.

The New Australian Plague.
A Melbourne hair-worker died last week from-,
anthrax. He was handling some bullocks’ tails,
and feeling some irritation from a scratch on his-
chin, he rubbed it with his hand and was inocu-
lated with anthrax. A malignant pustule deyel-
bped. He was taken to the Melbourne Hospital
on a Saturday morning and died the same evening.
That form of the disease—the malignant pustule
form —is comparatively rare in humans. The
other form, known as wool-sorters’ complaint, is
much more common. There the spores of
the bacilli are inhaled and the lungs are
attacked. Anthrax is a dreadful disease, and
is incurable in the lower animals and in human
beings alike. The cause of it reaching human
beings is mainly the carelessness and avarice of
the man who grows sheep and cattle. All
animals that die from anthrax should bo carefully
cremated. This is the only way to destroy the
germs, which are most resistant, and will live in
damp ground for years, 4’naturalists and far-
mers, however, cannot bear to burn a good skin ;
so diseased anrials are skinned and the hides
sold. The disease is thus carried round. Every-
body who handles the hide is liable to be infected.
Even when the hide is made into leather infection
clings round it. These facts have only been dis-
covered after exhaustive experiments, but no care
has been taken to make them public. The mouse
and the rat are particularly liable to infection,
and are splendid mediums for the propagation of
the disease. There have been several out-
breaks in Australia. At Corowa (N.8.W.)
two or three years ago some men were in-
fected ; it was shown that they had been
inoculated by flies, which luA carried the
bacillus from diseased cattle. The matter is
one of supreme importance. It is, in a way, a
greater question thaa that of the bubonic plague,
for anthrax, if it once gets hold of a country, will
devastate it and carry off man and beast alike.
It is claimed that the sixth plague with which
Moses is alleged to have bounced Phabaoh was
anthrax, and it see s. s probable enough. Anyhow,
this country ougbo to wake up and say that it
isn’t going to have any anthrax. The importation
of foreign bones and bone meal should be rigor-
ously prohibited unless it can be shown that they
were sterilised before f »port. The beasts, skins,
leather, hair, wool and ..orns of every country in
which anthrax is known to exist should be pro-
hibited from importation. Then heavy penalties
should be imposed for not cremating bodies of
animals that have died from the disease; or for
selling, or allowing to be sold, any portion
of any diseased animal. Plenty of farmers, when
they see a cow or sheep sickening, kill it, and sell
flesh, hide, hair, bones, and everything else.
Anthrax should be made a reportable disease.
At present, in Victoria at least, doctors are not
required to report cases of anthrax, and patients
die in their homes, which are not fumigated, and
retain the infection virtually for ever. So little
attention has been paid to the disease in human
beings that the ordinary medical practitioner
seldom diagnoses it. There are probably hundreds
of deaths set down to tuberculosis and other affec-
tions of the lungs and heart that are really due to
anthrax. The reportable diseases are for ever in
the medical practitioner’s mind. He looks for
them first, and they overshadow everything else.
If he were required to report every case of anthrax,
and such cases were at once isolated and proper
plague precautions taken, the risk of wholesale
infection would be reduced to a minimum.

An Australian Hat Invites Attention.
A letteb from Melbourne:—
To a sincere advocate of local manufactures there is
nothing more irritating than the fact that Australian-
made goods are freely purchased by an unpatriotic public
not for what they are, but for what they are thought not
to be. It is a common occurrence for a native-born Aus-
tralian—the pupil of an unteachable imported father—to
ridicule the pretension? of local industries, boasting
openly of his faith in Eng u«U and American material and
workmanship what time be is wearing a suit of tweed
loomed at Geelong or Marrickville, a hat from one of the
Melbourne nulls, and boots that are even more completely
Australian than the hat. Both his prejudice and his
ignorance have been forced upon him. If he attributes
his smart “Johnnie” appearance to the foreign origin of
his clothes, it is because those local products were sold to
him under false pretences. Thus far, in the history of
all, or nearly all, Australian manufactures the
retail traders have done their best to discour-
age them by invariably truckling to customers
for whom the term “ imported ” carries a
charm. Manufacturers’ have hitherto been mostly
content to feed the very prejudices against which they
were fighting. Instead of branding their goods as Aus-
tralian they have given them any non-committal trade
mark that would enable the shopkeeper to introduce
them to the public as “ Best imported,” &c. The effect
of this trickery as regards the trade in boxer hats has
been very advantageous to the actual importers of Eng-
lish makes, for it stands to reason that every mock-
imported but vaguely branded hat which, on its merits,
gives satisfaction to the wearer must serve to strengthen
the old prejudice in favor of imported hats.
With the hope otenlisting Australian sympathy for his
lighting policy the manager of the Austral Hat Mills, Mel-
bourne, has decided to stamp the name of its birthplace
upon every hat manufactured at the mills. There can be
no deception about the nationality of a “ boxer” or a soft
felt “sombrero,” branded “Austral Hat Mills” on the
lining. The retailer can’t enlarge upon the glossiness of
its surface, and the smartness of its binding and
get-up, for the purpose of declaring that “you
never get the same finish in an Australian
hat.” The “ Austral ” hats, hitherto sold under various
disguises, will now challenge comparison between the
local and imported article, and inasmuch as the mills are
controlled by an independent co., not by a ring of whole-
sale houses, the hats can be supplied direct to retailers at
prices which will make the comparison fair. Hitherto
the Melbourne manufactured hat has been burdened with
the profits of two middlemen.
Was there ever any reason for the local prejudice
against the local product? Several of the struggling
Victorian manufacturers may have been open to
scornful criticism from unthinking people 20 or 30
years ago. Prejudice against the word “Australian,”
as applied to a felt hat, was possibly founded on the
demerits of the goods offered to the fathers and grand-
fathers of the present generation. The young man of
to-day cherishes, without reason, the prejudice for which
his ancestor may have had some reason in the dim past.
The early manufactures were necessarily crude. The up-
to-date manufactures are first-class, and all they need is
honest recognition from the public and sufficient Protec-
tion to safeguard them from the onslaughts of the foreign
sweater, if the price of European labor were suddenly
raised to the Melbourne standard the Austral Hat
Mills would not say another word about the tariff.
Felt hats are made of rabbit fur in the better qualities,
and wool in the lower qualities. This country is full of
rabbits and sheep, and its hat factories use good raw
material, where an Italian manufacturer is disposed to
use shoddy. But the Italian worker toils 70 hours a week
for a wage of £l, whereas the unionists at the Austral Hat
Mills earn from £3 to £5. And any imperial-minded
supporters of good old English articles for good old Eng-
land’s sake will be interested to know that the imported
soft felt hats are coming more and more from Italy.
Sweated-labor conditions in Italy being much more aw-
fully pestilential than in England, a new danger threatens
misguided persons who pay as much as 15s. for a large,
limp specimen of foreign headgear.
Fully £200,000 worth of hats, a large percentage of
them being felt, will come to the Commonwealth this year
from abroad. Locally manufactured hats, to a large
amount, will be sold as imported goods. The idea is to
convert this stupid unpatriotic prejudice into a just
appreciation of an Australian brand as a guarantee of
merit. Therefore good luck to the Australian brand 1

The High Commissioner.
The Deakin Government lately contrived, in five
sadden days, to appoint Captain Collins to an
mnnecessary post in London —a post for which he
can’t be said to have any special fitness, because
the job is so vague and uncertain that it is impos-
sible to say what kind of fitness is required—but
is still unable to make up the vast recesses of its
mind about the appointment of a Federal High
’Commissioner. The billet is kept open, and
serves, so long as ib is _ open, to keep
about a dozen aspirants in a respectful
attitude on the Ministerial doorstep. If it
were filled the man who got it might cease to be
respectful, for the world is full of men who are
not nearly so civil after they have gob a thing as
when they are applying for it; and the eleven
men who didn’t get it would almost certainly be
much less civil than they are now. Either for the
sake of stringing on all the large number of pos-
sible Commissioners, or because the Ministry can’t
make up its mind, or bocause the different minds
of the Ministry won’t make up the same way, op
through sheer laziness and procrastination, nothing
is done. Meanwhile the press amuses itself on
slack days by inventing imaginary Commissioners.
It invented J. C. Watson the other day, ami
then filled an '••rherwise blank space in the paper
by printing his assurance that he had never heard
even a rumor about the matter. No doubt J. C.
"Watson would make a first-class High Commis-
i.jner, but there are not so many straight and
capable men in Australian politics that he could
be spared, and he certainly doesn’t want the
position. The High Commissioner will probably
be appointed with a rush when Parliament is
about to expire, and “bait” is no longer wanted,
and when the twelve applicants on the Ministerial
doorstep grow angry and unmanageable. Mean-
while Captain Collins goes to England as a sort
of temporary apology for a Federal representative,
with no powers in particular, and a status which
isn’t anything definite.

The Westralian Government has already squan-
dered over a quarter of a million on rabbit fences,
but the money might just as well have been
thrown into the Indian Ocean. The first attempt
to check the long-threatened rabbit invasion was
made after bunny had got a firm grip on the grass
lands west of the goldfields. He had successfully
negotiated the wide strip of sandy desert between
the goldfields and the South Australian border,
and had found a land of plenty. Outback pros-
pectors and miners welcomed him, and tinned
dog was supplanted by savory rabbit with onions.
Then the Lands Department lazily started to
keep bunny back, but it was too late. A fence
was run right up from the south coast for several
degrees, but it either broke down or was covered
with drift sand, and the rabbits scampered across
it with the greatest ease. Then a second fence,
near the coast, running parallel with the first one,
was erected, and now the rabbits are on both
sides of it also. Squatters in the far North —on
the De Grey and Fitzroy rivers, and even on
Sturt Creek —are already threatened, and
they blame the Lands Department for the clumsy,
half-hearted measures adopted. Although useless
for checking the rabbit invasion, the fences have
brought abo”t a totally unexpected result in the
wholesale destruction of the emu. During the
winter months emus flock in tremendous num-
bers to the wide, open plains east of the gold
fields, but as summer comes on they are driven
back again, following up the drying soaks and
waterholes. They are silly birds, and have not
the cunning of the rabbit, so when they bump up
against any section of the fence that may be erect,
they retire to a little distance and wait for it to
fall down. A boundary rider recently found 300
dead emus along one section of 60 miles, so the
annual destruction of these typical Australian
birds in the course of a year must be very great.

“S.R.” on Australia’s Gun :
Australia must at once lay the foundation-stone of
national defence by setting up its own gun factories.
The obstacle in the way, says Lieutenant-Colonel Irvink,
is that the number required is too small to warrant the
outlay. Well, better men than Colonel Irvine, in the
year 1791, set up a gun factory at Springfield (U.S. A.). At
that period the population of the States did not exceed
Australia’s. To-day America possesses one of the best
arsenals in the world. Time Australia began. The state-
ment that the number required is so small is bosh. At
the last extremity Australia should be able to find at least
1,000,000 men capable of carrying rifles. .So Australia re-
quires to have on hand 1,000,000 up-to-date rifles. I’ll
warrant many an up-to-date small arms factory in Great
Britain wouldn’t make good the deficiency in a year or
two. The Yank thinks it good policy to give his army
a new weapon every eight years, and Australia
would also require to keep its rifles up-to-date.
As for artillery, Australia has, for all practical purposes,
none whatever. If you swept up all the position guns on
our oo.ist defences, from Thursday Island to Fremantle,
you couldn’t arm one decc.it oruiser. Thursday Island,
Russia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fourteen hundred persons have been executed without
trial.— Russian cable.
The sword of tyranny is raised,
Reeking, in Russian air ;
The revolution burned and blazed,
Its ashes smoulder there.
The Czar rides safe ; in every street
His panoply is spread;
But pale ghosts whimper round his feet
Of fourteen hundred dead.
His judges march in gory tracks,
More cruel hour by hour ;
The hangman’s noose, the headsman’s axe,
Their instruments of power.
And Russia’s lips have ceased to sing,
And Russia’s eyes are red ;
But they await their reckoning,
Those fourteen hundred dead.
A phantom army, they assail
The Czar’s embattled corps ;
They burst the locks of fort and gaol
And steel-bound palace door ;
From their still corpses hatreds bud,
And blossoms overhead
The flower of vengeance from their blood—•
The fourteen hundred dead.
The great Red Army, uncontrolled.
Shall march through Russia’s land,
As over France, in days of old,
It swept its crimson hand ;
Then Czar and prince shall shrieking fall,
And in a mist blood-red
Shall see the ghastly mouths of all
Those fourteen hundred dead.

A Waggon and a Star.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Australia has hitched its waggon to the Star of
Unity.” —Prime Minister Deakin.
Yes, we’ve hitched it to a star,
This, our old Australia,
And we’re rushing thro’ the inky realms of space ;
But our craft is steered by Cohen,
And we don’o know where we’re goin’—
On to glory, or to ruin and disgrace !
And another says : “ We’ll drift
Till the shades of Isaac lift,
And our leader’s dazzling lights displace the gloom;
But till then we well might shudder,
For there’s no one at the rudder,
And we’re drifting slowly, surely t; our doom ! ”
But I’d venture to surmise
That we’ll never reach the skies,
Nor along the Milky Way aspire to soar;
For our hopes are cold and cankered,
We are braked, and sprayged, and anchored
To a luring th, ee-ball sign at Coesn’s door ?
N.S.W.

Townsville, Brisbane, Sydney, Newcastle, Melbourne,
Hobart, Adelaide, Fremantle, all require big 12in. guns
that can keep off warships. And of Tin. and 4in. guns and
torpedo guns we require stacks. Modern warfare is
largely an artillery struggle. Note the great masses of
artillery which Japan and Russia put into the field.
Time Australia set the furnaces roaring and the anvils
ringing. Thus we will get more guns, better guns, and
more ammunition to practise with. And the fact of these
articles being made in Australia will be an object lesson
to the young Australian. He will realise more readily
that he has a duty to perform, the defence of his country.
Make a rifle for every man, teach every man how to use
it, and compel every man to fit himself for the task of
holding Australia against the world. Then may come the
four corners of the world in arms, and we shall withstand
them—or make a good attempt at it.
The daily press found it easier to shove the Boy
on to the stage as evidence of the tyrannous
selfishness of Labor unions than to keep him
there. The Boy simply bolted when the lime-
light of fact was turned on him. It is not the
Labor unions that keep him out of apprenticeship,
but certain employers who find the unions insist
on the Boy getting fair play. The trades
in which the Arbitration Court has fixed the
ratio of apprentices to journeymen see that the
Boy receives wages and is taught his trade. To
get out of doing both has been the study of many
employers. The dailies no sooner posted the Boy
on the stage as a victim of Labor tyranny than a
father instanced the case of his son, who agreed,
in the old pre-Arbitration Court way, with a firm
for five years’ service, the first year without wages.
Next year, when wages were supposed to com-
mence, the son was knocked off, and there the
agreement, under various spurious pretexts, was
ended. The unions have brought about a
schedule of rates, which must be paid,
and a term of apprenticeship, which must
be completed, with adequate teaching through-
out. Bosses who object to the ratio simply wish
to have more boys than men for wages sheet
purposes alone. Boys kept at special lines of a
trade may become fairly proficient in those lines
without knowing the trade as a whole, and an
employer with a shop full of such boys does not
want men at all. It is for the sake of the men of
the present, and equally for the sake of the men
of the future, that the unions insist on their
conditions.
“Locust” makes some remarks up in his tree :
The Britishers have' again gone bald-headed for their
old fetish, Freetrade, at the elections. This illustrates
a good trait in their character, honorable to their ’earts,
but discreditable to their ’eads. Apropos —did you ever
go into that queer place, the Savage Club, somewhere in
Arundel-street, London, near the Embankment, on one of
their festive evenings, and hear the old favorites of the
club get up one by one, and by unanimous request sing a
song? For 50years, at precisely half-past ten, it has been
the custom to call for Blank or Wash to sing “ The Old
Battered Muffin,” or “ The Old Mill Wheel.” He gets up and
sings it. His voice is a trifle cracked, the song is of the old,
rugged, sentimental order of a bygone day; but the
singer and the song are received with deafening applause.
It may be that, tortured by conscience, this old and
popular Englishman determines to try something new.
But the moment the pianist strikes up the innovating
tune, shouts of protest are raised —“ Give us the ‘ Old
Fire Shovel ’ Blank ! ” until he has to hark back to his old
song. So it is with politics. For over 50years the British
public have been accustomed to have the “ Old Fire
Shovel ” of Freetrade sung to them, preached to them,
and printed for them. When any political leader gets up
and tries to sing a new economic song he is received with
amazement, and requested to sing the ballads of yore.
If he refuses he is regarded as an innovator, and there-
fore dangerous. Balfour’s mistake was that he tried
to sing the new song of Protection to the old Freetrade
accompaniment in a different key, and the two tunes did n't
harmonise. He is at last beginning to sing Protection,
but in a plaintive falsetto in minor modes. Joe Cham
berlain’s deep bass will make itself heard eventually.
Either that, or the nation must wait till the generation
which “ has been Freetrade, man and boy, fur fifty year
as me fayther was afore me,” has died out, and a new
generation will be convinced either by its brains or its
stomach that Protection is the only policy which leads to
economic salvation.
