After an interval, more deaths in Sydney
are reported as the result of eating oysters pur-
chased from hawkers at the door, and some of
these oysters have been traced to the rocks of
Middle Harbor. At Long Bay, near Folly Point,
there has been for some time a scandal which
probably no other civilised community would
tolerate. At the head of the bay the Water and
Sewerage Board has placed septic tanks, and
these, through inefficient working, are a public
danger. On close summer evenings the in-
habitants about have to close doors and
windows in order to shut out the stench,
and the waters of the harbor thereabouts
are polluted by the effluent from the tanks. The
Water and Sewerage Board airily denies that there
is anything the matter; but, one afternoon, a
couple of years ago, the members of the Fisheries
Commission took a trip to the place on a launch.
They saw and smelt, and, before the boat was
back at Circular Quay, an order had been drawn
up closing the waters of that part of Middle
Harbor against fishing. That order still stands,
but the Water and Sewerage Board, in spite of
numerous reminders, keeps to its policy of inac-
tivity, and, as a result, typhoid germs are carted
in bottles about the streets.

The Bent Government Court of Inquiry into the
cause of a recent accident on the Bent Govern-
ment’s railway line turns out to be an even more
audacious job than was expected. The Court con-
sists of Judge Box and two other inquirers, who
will add to the expenses. Bent’s Railway Com-
missioners are represented by a barrister, who gets
his instructions from a Mr. Clarke, of the Crown
Solicitor’s office, and on the other hand there is
barrister W. H. Williams (ex-M.L.A.) instructed
by Bent’s friend, W. H. Croker, who at first
were said to “represent the public,” but
are now described as “assistants” to the Board.
These legal persons will get their fees from
the Treasury, of course, for conducting what is
practically a mock trial of the Government Rail-
way Department, the finding of the court all the
time being a foregone conclusion. Before the cur-
tain was raised on the farcical performance, Bent
gave his word that lawyer Woolf, representing a
number of upset passengers who have put in
claims for compensation, would be permitted to
appear on their behalf and elicit evidence re the
cause of the accident. Woolf would have cost
the Treasury nothing, whereas Williams and
Croker will cost a lot. First thing the court did
was to disallow the appearance of Woolf on the
ground that he wasn’t needed in the public interest.
The engine-driver and fireman have a lawyer on
the job, likewise the Transportation Society of
the Railways, and these are the only two legal
persons employed on the inquiry who are not
getting paid by the Treasury. As to the length
of the Bent proceedings there are various conjec-
tures. The Inquiry is the softest of fat things for
the participants employed by Bent. They can
sit and sit, and talk and talk, with the fullest
assurance that their fees will be paid. And the
result, as aforestated, is a foregone conclusion.
Last Friday, in an article entitled “ Small Settle-
ment and Large Owners, ” Sydney Telegraph set
out to define its cheerless attitude towards the
man who is to go on the land. This was the atti-
tude :
(1) To break up the large estates which now block
settlement by means of a graduated land-tax is sinful
robbery. It “would have a confiscatory effect by com-
pelling owners to either over-supply the land market to
escape the tax, or pay the value of tlieir holdings in taxes
while waiting- for fair prices.”
(2) “There is only one legitimate way of getting land
for settlement, which is to buy it. ... If the era of
deficits should return, or exceptional expenditure crop up,
there will he no money to spend in buying estates for
close settlement. Even if there was plenty, experience
of this method is not wholly encouraging.”
Put shortly, the ’Graph holds: (1) That people
should go on the land. (2) That the big estates
shouldn’t be broken up by a graduated land-tax,
to supply them with land to go on. (3) That the
repurchase of big estates to furnish the people
with small farms to go on is not advisable. (4)
That there should be no graduated land-tax to
prevent such farms as now exist being bought up
and consolidated into more big estates, whereby
people woidd be driven off the land instead of put
on it, because the graduated land-tax has a “ con-
fiscatory effect.” (5) All the same, people should
be settled on the land. Is it worth while running
a large, damp, inky paper in order that the edito-
rial staff may wander round in a circle like this,
and arrive nowhere, and then charge a penny for
its wanderings ?

Beatrice Grimshaw, who is “doing” the New.
Hebrides for Sydney Herald, thinks that there
is trouble ahead for the whites in Tanna, and
that this year will probably see its arrival. She
writes :
The bush tribes are absolutely reckless as to the de-
struction of white men’s property when they start out to
burn each other’s gardens or villages. They are also
quite careless as to any danger that may result to white
people from their very free firing propensities. Tommy
Tanna, furthermore, as he comes back from Queensland,
makes more and more trouble in the land. The Queens-
land Tanna-man is notoriously the worst sort in Tanna,
always at the head of tribal fights, usually amongst those
most strongly opposed to the missions, and generally dis-
posed to make as much trouble as possible. . . . Tommy
Tanna, of Queensland, full of civilisation’s vices, sharper
and more knowing than his followers, yet a savage to the
tips of his fingers, joins the Conservative party of the
island—the older chiefs, who hate the white man and all
his doings, and the younger and more savage savages,
who are beginning to take alarm at the increasing power
of the missions. Tanna is working up for trouble.
This is rather rough on those good Church people
who, a little while back, protested against the de-
portation of the gentle Tommy Tanna, on the
ground that he had become highly Christianised
and civilised while in Queensland, and it was
rank injustice to send him home to be eaten up by
his savage countrymen.
In “ Litanies of Life,” by Kathleen Wat-
son, now of Australia, all the sentimentality that
lurks in every woman’s heart is expressed with a
poetical fluency and candor which should carry
the little book far beyond its present popularity.
Some years ago, when the “Litanies” were first
published under one cover, in London, they drew
a characteristically glowing notice from T. P.
O’Connor, and to-day it seems that they only need
to be talked about to find a host of new admirers
among men and women hereabouts. Regarded as
works of imagination, the quintette of romantic
yarns are merely commonplace in plot. Many
another woman writer has pictured herself in one
or more of the “Litany” situations, and has
pitied herself as freely as Kathleen Watson did.
But the charm of this book lies in the complete-
ness of the assortment of sorrows, and the delicacy
of the authoress’s art. Though it is the same art
in each story, it gains rather than, loses effect by
the repetition. Up to the limit of five “ Litanies ”
—l6O wide-margined pages in all —the reader’s
appetite grows with what it feeds upon, or is at
least unconscious of satiety. Enquiries for the
book from Melbourne, where the married authoress
is now a quite old-established resident, may have
accounted for the recent re-issue. The matter
can’t be allowed to rest at a third edition. Here
is balm of sadness for the yearning soul of
youthful maid, hopeless spinster, unwedded wife
and tearful widow. And, for the menkind, a
charming reminder that women were self-
deceivers ever.
Does G. H. Reid bait that Anti-Socialist
trap with some of the lollies he is so fond of
sucking ? Those wiser politicians who reinforce
their mental forces with well regulated “ drafts”
of D.C.L. whisky, may well laugh at poor George,
and wonder when he is going to seek inspiration
at its source. D.C.L., the whisky that makes
dullards wise, and wise men wiser.*

There is a mistake of a letter in the title
of the new piece at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. It is
advertised as “ The Shop Girl ” —which holds an
“h ” too many and an “1 ” too few. There have
been Girls of all sorts in musical comedy, but this
one obviously comes out of the ready-made, hand-
me-down slop department. “ A peautiful fit,”
says Yacob. “Isn’t two inches of shirt rather too
much to show ? ” you suggest, looking down at an
equator between vest and the other things. “Oh,
my tear sir,” the pained Yacob tells you, “ you
hafn’t an idea how much this peautiful material
stretches. But till it stretches—Mr. Bantoclc, for-
vart ! Hang four jokes on to this vais’coat—you’ll
find some jokes in the yart mit the old hoop-iron.”
“But the pants, Yacob —they seem to stop too
soon.” “My tear frent, the peautyof them. You can
use them for pants, or your v’ife can use them for
mixed-bathing in. But if you vant them a leetle
longer—Rosie, Rosie Musgrove, forvart ! Hang
a song unt tance on to dese pants.” And when
you point out that the last gentleman who tried
this suit on was evidently a person of cir-
cumference who burst the arm-holes—“ Mr.
Lauri, forvart ! Pin a prilliant stump-speech on
to this peautiful vaistcoat so that nobody’ll look
at the coat to notice that the armholes vant a
stitch in’em.” So you get “fitted” at Yacob’s;
and so you get “fitted” these days at Her
Majesty’s.
* * * *
The piece was played some years ago, but
is old and poor enough to have been long since
forgotten. There is little music in it of any con-
sequence, and the humor of the dialogue is for the
most part banal. On to this unpromising founda-
tion, however, the Comic Opera Co. hangs a num-
ber of presentable items ; and there are a few
lyrics proper to the piece itself which set one
wondering how they got there. Fortunately, with
one or two exceptions, the principals of the co. are
not wasted upon this Girl. Miss Margaret
Thomas drops in promiscuously late in the even-
■ ing, and sings a welcome song quite foreign to the
tawdry.“atmosphere.” She is really having a rest
from the hard work she has been doing, to prepare
for the better work she is going to do. Haigh
Jackson makes only a late appearance, too, most
effectively disguised behind an American million-
aire’s long white goatee whiskers. The ancient
millionaire sings one song; beyond that, he has
little to do, but his acting—not the fine baritone’s
Strong point—is justabouthisbest. Lauri, beginning
as a shop-walker in Hooley’s universal providery,
drifts into a number of disguises in a succession of
music-hall turns ; and Miss Rose Musgrove, ap-
parently some sort of a shop-walkeress to start
with, keeps Lauri company in the subsequent
quick-change-artist business —hanging the afore-
mentioned song and dance and stump-speech on
to the reach-me-down clothes in order to hide
their deficiencies. About the hardest thing
one can say of “The Shop Girl” is that
these music - hall tags are the most popu-
lar part of the performance. Some of them
are popular enough to be called for three or four
times. There are really two shop-girls—Miss
Clara Clifton and Miss Florence Young ; both of
them are foundlings, and one of them a million-
airess. Hooley, of the shop, gets an inkling of
it, and marries—the wrong one. That is the
plot. Miss Clifton knocks a good deal of fun but
of her foundling, and has the “ fat” of the songs
proper to the farce; and Miss Young struggles
with a part for which she isn’t suited. This is
Time’s fault, not Miss Young’s. Fred Leslie is a
deplorable French count, John Doran a passable
medical student who is going to marry the real
millionairess, and Percy is the usual johnny.

“The Passport ” is si'll in evidence at
Sydney Criterion, and will continue until Friday
night to develop unexpected complications on the
Russian frontier. After that the Russian frontier
will be taken to pieces by the property man and
stored away till wanted again. ‘ 4 Little Mary ”is
due on Saturday, and is described as “ an Uncom-
fortable Play in three Acts, by J. M. Barrie.”
However uncomfortable Polly may be, the fact
that Robert Brough—the Brough who, as a
humorist and a fine versatile actor, stands without
a rival in Australia —is to re-appear as the Earl of
Carlton ensures it a good reception.
** * *
The other day a Sydney somebody, at the
resumption of studies at a certain school, gave the
young Australians a piece of plain language out
of his own head. He told them they had “ neither
enthusiasm nor endurance.” Writer saw some of
the elder pupils while witnessing the resurrection
of “Monsieur Beaucaire,” at Sydney Palace
on Saturday night, and they at least didn’t lack
enthusiasm, however short they might be in
endurance. They simply revelled in the glowing
love-story of the Prince whose hair glistened
yellow in the candle-light, and the haughty Mary
of the red roses. It was merely a chance that
Beaucaire and Mary chose a cool night to
resurrect. Had it been a sweltering eve
the packed audience of mostly young and young-
old persons would have had a chance to display
endurance as well. Julius Knight and Maud
Jeffries appear in their usual curtseying, bowing,
and elegantly artificial roles. G. P. Carey is the
Duke of Winterset, and the remainder of a long
c ist fits its parts well enough. “ Beaucaire ”is not
a work of art. It ambles with an unreal glitter
through an unreal atmosphere of pomp and
jewelled aristocracy, and it has a painful rever-
ence for persons belonging to the royal family.
Also the story, before the dramatist fitted it with
the conventional happy ending, was shockingly
illogical, for the Prince refused to marry Lady
Mary, whom he loved, because she wouldn’t have
him till she knew he wasn’t the plebeian he was
originally supposed to be, and went away to marry
a French relative whom he didn’t love, and who
would probably have been more shocked at the
idea of marrying him if he had been a plebeian
than Lady Mary was. But all the same, “Beau-
caire,” largely on account of its knee-breeches,
seems to have got a solid hold on the great heart
of the public.

To-night (Thursday), “ The
Sign of the Cross ” is to ‘ Wee'p at the Palace by
general request, and Mercia in her long white
garment is now taking running exercise up and
down the furniture, so that she may keep ahead
of her leggy Roman admirer in the crisis of the
play. Also, the lions are sharpening their teeth,
and are getting up their tail muscles so that they
may lash the caudal appendage in the arena.
The fine old “Silver King” follows for four
nights, and then two samples of the “Lady of
Lyons ” will close the Knight-Jeffries season.
# * *
At Sydney Royal “ Tambour Major ” is in
its third week, and William Anderson’s luck is
still going strong. “Tambour Major” is pretty
old nowadays, and belongs to a class of comic
opera that, when Gilbert was in his prime, almost
seemed to have gone out of date. But by the aid
of a good company and lavish mounting, the
management has proved to the public that the
old show is almost as good as new. Solomon in
all his gold and weariness was a back number in
the matter of gorgeousness compared to W.
Anderson when that manager is really resolved
to do the thing handsomely, and Belshazzar, with
his diadem on his cranium in Babylon, was
only an unimportant blot on the surface of events.
Gold and tinsel, and military decorations,
and the pomp and fluff and circumstance
and proud cocked hat of war, and the glitter
of flags, and the rumble of ironmongery, and the
blare of music—all these effects rage through the
old play, and the back-cloth has been laid on with
a lavish hand. “Let there be scenery,” said
Anderson, when he ordered the play, and there
was scenery—the same as in Genesis. If ‘ ‘ Tambour
Major’’was ever done much better in its youth
than it is this time, it is an opera with a joyous
past to look back upon. On present appearances
William A. could make almost a record run with
it if he felt so inclined.
** * *
Newcomers to the Sydney Tivoli this week
include the Miles-Stavordale quintette, who may
best be described as trick musicians, getting out
of harp and zither or banjo, effects which come
within reasonable distance of the notes of the
human voice. Making their first appearance in a
sheaf of well-known selections from Irish airs,
they made themselves sure of a vociferous wel-
come, anyhow—and they got it and deserved it.
It would be a number deserving kind words even
if it weren’t advertised as “ Songs Without
Singers.” The Marionettes, the trio of trick
cyclists, and the Scotch Meisters build up a bill
of unusual variety.
«& * *
Probably the Melbourne chances of “ Leah
Kleschna ” are improved by the fact that it comes
a few months after “In the Bishop’s Carriage.”
The argument is the same in both dramas, and to
a great extent the characters, scenes and order of
events in the one play are reflected in the other.
Seemingly the story of “ The Bishop’s Carriage ”
was published as a novel before “Kleschna ” came
to light, whereas the latter was a popular play
ere the former was dramatised; but as to
whether “ Kleschna ” was a burglary from
the book, or both of them were stolen
from a foreign source, there is no convincing evi-
dence. This question of originality is a small
matter to Melbourne just now compared with the
question whether the drama at Her Majesty’s is a
better thing than the one Nellie Stewart and Co.
played at the Princess’slast year. Undoubtedly it is.
The points of resemblance and difference are all ini
favor of the present show. Leah, the daughter of
a sophistical criminal, Kleschna, has a more
genuine appearance than Nance, the pupil of Tom
Dorgan, a mere orthodox burglar, who was
supposed to love her for her chastity. Paul
Sylvaine, the politician with a taste for
criminology, is a less unreal preacher than priggish
Mr. Latimer in “ The Bishop’s Carriage.” Raoul
Berton, the contemptible young man about town,
is worth more to this story than any half-dozen
minor characters were worth to the other story.
In fact, “ Kleschna” most of the time is a stirring
melodrama, not so prosy at its prosiest or so weak
at its weakest as the drama of inevitable com-
parison, which was stagey nonsense all the time.
* * * *
Inconsistencies in character and plot are
obvious enough. Kleschna, the brave, brainy
burglar, shows too much of a disposition to stoush
his daughter the moment she angers him : also
he is blind to facts which his mild accomplice
Schram has accurately guessed. The dramatist
offers no reason why Raoul should coolly offer the
dishonor of his “protection ” to Miss Kleschna in
her father’s presence when they are supposed to
be decent people. Neither does the dialogue
in the first four acts seem to justify Sylvaine’s
proposal of marriage to Leah in the last act. The
girl’s reverence for him is explained. He was her
hero, the man with his photo in shop windows,
who turned her maiden heart from thoughts of
burglary, and made her so penitent that she went
forth and worked her sin away in a reformatory
market-garden. But her conversion doesn’t ex-
plain why Sylvaine, from Paris, should come
along to the remote cabbage-patch after a lapse of
three years for the purpose of picking Leah off the
soil and making her his bride —in England. It is
a silly last act, yet not so nauseating as the end of
“The Bishop’s Carriage” where Nellie Stewart,
in the garb of a ministering angel, spun her little
yarn about a Pot and a Pipkin.
* * * *
There are important changes in the cast
since “ Kleschna ” was staged in Sydney. Gaston
Mervale plays the heavy Christian politician in
place of Douglas Gerrard, and although Mervale
has always the manner of an actor, and a slow
tragedian at that, he is weighty and effective in
this part. Girls of the Leah sort are struck on
actors, anyhow. Harry Sweeney plays the very
young man character of Raoul, in succession to
Mervale aforesaid. Sweeney puts evidences of
talent and plenty of energy into his performance,
and the faults of enthusiastic inexperience may be
forgiven him for the sake of his brightness in the
first act. Incidentally, Maurice Dudley may
be commended for his lively sketch of the
conventional stage Frenchman in the first act.
His vivacity appears foreign to the surroundings,
because all the other French characters are played
on English lines, but such incongruities are the
salt of melodrama. Miss Brune, Roy Redgrave
and Atholwood are the firm of burglars, as before


Atholwood overacts his small part. He strikes
one key, and he strikes it too heavily. Redgrave,
on the other hand, has a large part in which he
gives only clever suggestions of a character which
might be more passionately expressed. And Miss
Brune, as she strikes the present commentator, is
not more than satisfactory in the name part. All
the droning dulness of her voice and enunciation
is brought out in her attempts to put strong
pathos into weak bits of dialogue. The character
doesn’t keep her tuned up to concert pitch. She
shows the same want of naturalness —plain, ordi-
nary, simple naturalness—that Nellie Stewart
shows. A strenuous actress striving to get the
right effect in the wrong way !
*# * *
A panto, at Melbourne Princess’s, sand-
wiched between intensely moral melodramas at
the Royal and Her Majesty’s, is practically
doomed to success, even in March, when the fes-
tive season is no more and the clown should be at
rest: The Anderson panto, represents frivolity
at a premium. Its arrival is opportune, and a
“Sindbad ” that grabs opportunity by the throat
is bound to squeeze a bit of money from
its victim. Anderson’s “Sindbad” is not
a brilliantly humorous nor a specially gor-
geous entertainment. The Interior of the
King’s Palace falls about 15 miles short of one’s
wildest dreams of magnificence, and there are two
or three fairies in the ballet who would look all
the better for a new face apiece. But “ Sindbad ”
will meet public requirements for a fortnight. It
goes very well, with the assistance of a new prin-
cipal “boy ” (Miss Marie Campbell) and the old
comedians, Fred Graham and Bert Bailey, of
whom Bailey will be remembered for his per-
tinacity in inviting other members of the panto,
to kiss Georgey. On Saturday night the “Sind-
bad ” proceedings were unduly prolonged, but the
audience mostly stayed to the finish and took out
its full money’s worth.
* -:tf * *
At Melbourne Iloyal, the success of “ The
Prodigal Son ” has reached the border-line where
a policeman prohibits further sale of tickets at the
door, and Bland Holt restrains himself with diffi-
culty from singing “Hallelujah.” Hall Caine’s
drama will carry the season to a crowded termi-
nation next week, after which Meynell and Gunn
will get an innings with “The Fatal Wedding.”
Meanwhile, the sorrowful Prodigal (Albert
Norman) is returning home in a new wig, not so
white as his previous head of hair, but a distinctly
better fit. In other respects than Norman’s wig,
the presentation of “ The Prodigal Son ” is less
glaring than at first. The co. is dropping to the
requirements of a drama that is not essentially
“ melo.”
** * *
Nothing in particular happened to the bill
at Melbourne Opera House last week. The Legs
in the Air are maintaining their former attitude
towards the atmosphere. Miss Raymond, Crim-
mins and Gore, the Two Tambourines, the Two
Bells, Fanny Powers and Others proceed as usual,
Others being a numerous performer of uncertain
merit.
** * *
Adelaide Tivoli has been re-opened by
Rickards, with manager Marcus in charge. The
little house has had a lot of money spent on it;
an asbestos curtain being put in, and new ap-
proaches provided for the stalls and circle. In a
strong mixed co., Talma, Leroy and Bosco are the
stars. They are almost as good as their own
picture-posters, and those are the cleverest on local
record. The Royal perseveres with its flicker-
films, having done especially well in Show week
with no, opposition running. The photographer
appears to have been close to both Nelson and
Charles Pearce when each of these historical
heroes met with a fatal accident.
The “first white woman” is very much in
evidence just now. Last week’s obituary con-
tained the name of a Mrs. Cooke, who died at
Kiama, aged 75, and whose friends claim
for her the honor of being the first female white
child born at the place where Parkes raised his
famous ghost. Kiama and its Blow-hole have a
lot to answer for.
Hans Heysen, a promising young South
Australian brush-wielder, has sold his picture,
recently exhibited in the A.N.A. Exhibition, to
the Melbourne National Gallery for £l5O. Hans
is not a master yet; but he paints the Bush with
honest observation and proper enthusiasm, and
some day, if he doesn’t rush to Paris, he should do
notable work.
One of the chief objects of civilisation is
to extract pleasure from the ordinary necessities
of life, hence the improvements in the art of
dining, the practice of sleeping, and so forth.
When Rome was at its highest the fact was made
evident in the artistic way the people washed
themselves. But our civilisation has taken (a
wider sweep, and cut deeper into the original
rough plan of things. Now-a-days the most
trivial details of life have been made luxuries.
If you want to dine in an atmosphere
of beauty, luxury, and quiet comfort, de-
corate your breakfast and dining tables with
silver and electro - plated ware from the
premises of Messrs. W. Mammat and Sons,
Empire Chambers, 93 York-street, Sydney. The
choice is unlimited, and the prices are all that can
be desired. This energetic firm has made a luxury
of shaving, which is one of the latest developments
of civilisation. Whatever the Roman may have
had in his marble halls and scented baths, he cer-
tainly never had a hollow-ground razor, and the
best of all razors is the Abbot, which while re-
moving the hirsute growth imparts a delightful
sensation. And while you are selecting the
electro-plate for the table and the Abbot razor for
the toilet bureau, have a look at the office, pocket,
and yachting knives. They are worth gazing
upon.*
The Continental C. and G. Rubber Co. Prop.
Ltd., of 225 Clarence-street, are making a change in the
management of their Sydney office. Mr. J. P. Wallace,
the Managing Director, arrived in Sydney during the
week together with Mr. George F. Hill, the new manager
for this branch. The progress of the Continental
Company has been very marked during 1905. The increase
in actual business over the preceeding year amounts to
over half a million pounds sterling, naturally Australian
business comes in for a share of this and the progress
made by the company in every State is of the most satis-
factory character. Motor and cycle tyres naturally are
the trading lines of the Continental Company.*
Artist Percy Spence, on the eve of his return
to London and steady graft, after what has been virtually
a twelve-months holiday in Austral sunshine, is disposing
of a charming collection of oil paintings, water colors,
and character sketches in various mediums. The collec-
tion, which includes much of the craftsman’s best work—
work which it i 3 improbable that he will ever excel in
freshness and grace—is at present on view at the galle-
ries of Walter Bradley and Co., 324 George-street, Sydney.
Since Spence originally left Australia he has mixed with
some shockingly bad company from a democratic stand-
point, and his collection of impressions of kings and other
large social and political dogs is numerous. People who
are interested in this sort of fry have a unique oppor-
tunity to gratify the vice.

CHINA'S ATTITUDE ; YESTERDAY AND TO-DAY.
“J.W.”: I witnessed a very touching scene at the
A.N.A. Exhibition (Melb.) the other night. Three charm-
ing young damsels stood in front of a dairy pioduce stall
smiling on the crowd and offering it dainty plates of
cheese to sample. Behind them hung an imposing
placard This is the stall at which Sir Reginald
Talbot stopped this morning and sampled some cheese
from these very plates ! ” The people were falling over
one another to sample the cheese, which, of course, did
not hold out very long ; and when it gave out, some
fervent loyalists kissed the plates. Others, I hope, kissed
the girls.
“C.M.G.”: Gaston Mervale, supporting Tittell Brune
and others, has a quaint mannerism. Gaston is usually a
very'composed actor, and there is a sort of .Government
stroke about his methods, but, when it is absolutely
necessary for him to wake up and get his emotions going,
he shakes the words out of himself just as a damp dog
shakes the water off his hide. In the stress of great
excitement, Gaston’s shake becomes so excessive that one
expects his clothes to fly off him into the orchestra.
Said that Bland Holt made an effort to get hold of that
jewel, Knight, and Miss Maud Jeffries for places in his
rendering of “The Prodigal Son,” but manager J. G.
Williamson had all rights reserved, and he asked a rental
for sub-letting Julius and Maud that put a sudden stop to
the negotiations. (This report will probably be contra-
dicted next week.)
Williams, the hypnotic subject who was fined for
furious driving in Ballarat, put up rather a record. He
somehow forgot to pay the fine in Ballarat, and got away
to Geelong. There a warrant of commitment was sent
after him. The policeman who had to arrest him arrived
just as Samuel (his name is Samuel Williams) was being
hypnotised. Sammy hurried up and got into the trance
in record time. The bobby was nonplussed. He stood
scratching his head and exclaiming “ But he’s my
prisoner,” in art injured tone of voice, while Samuel was
lifted away from under his very eyes and solemnly buried
about 6ft.'underground out of his reach. Bobby spent
the next week, during which Samuel remained in the
trance, hovering between Samuel’s grave and O’Calla-
ghan’s “ Plain Hints to Policemen,” which plain volume,
however, said nothing about how to arrest a live corpse
that has been buried.
“ Hiphen ” : J. C. Williamson, I congratulate you ! You
are a judge of human nature. You know to a nicety how
much strain your audience can stand per square inch.
You know more ; you know how to relieve that strain.
It was a brilliant—nay, humane—idea on Wednesday
week night, when La Tosca was carving-knifing the villain
with the convulsive name ; when the bouse was shudder-
ing and the ladies up above were breathing loudly, and
the lemonade bottles were slipping from their nerveless
grasp on to the stalls below, to introduce that low-comedy
cat, which leapt lightly before the footlights, walked with
dignity across the stage, and disappeared in the wings.
’Tis the light, humorous touch at the right tragic moment
that spells genius.
The Holman Hunt picture, “ The Light of the World,”
is travelling the globe at its owner’s expense, and the
English Johnnie-boy in charge, who has a lot of nice
clothes and an aristocratic lisp, has a very soft snap. He
receives a decent salary and all expenses to carry “ The
Light ” from place to place. The intention of Booth, the
owner, is not the encouragement of art ; his object is en-
tirely religious, and he regards the painting as a sort of
sermon on canvas, which turns men’s thoughts from
worldly things, and makes them think of their end.
Thinking of one’s end is an uncomfortable form of reflec-
tion, but the truly pious person’s highest ambition is to
turn our thoughts in that direction. This is what Mr.
Booth and the young man carrying “The Light’’aim at.
The mission movement comes to Sydney, thence to Tas-
mania, and then does the inland towns before going to
Maoriland and America.
“ Melb.”: We are having a row over policeman Curran.
This gentleman disdains - the trivial round of the blue-
bottle, copper, trap, or slop, and has developed a genius
for managing the police fetes, which are so popular.
They set the populace roaring over a mock robbery at
Prince’s Court, and then comes intelligence of some real
garrotting, bank robbery with violence, and what not,
executed with impunity. Commissioner Tom O’Oal-
laghan, Superintendent Mahony, and the other heads of
the force, get an idea that Curran’s genius is rather
demoralising to the force, and that he ought to keep his
nose to the grindstone on his “ bate,” and capture the
disorderly pushite. I’ve a notion that Curran is too good
for that work, and will yet be found running a circus or
an opera co.
“ H.L.” : Going home in the train last night I heard a
little, pale, fair child say to her mother, “Mother, when
• Sindbad the Sailor ’ is on, will you take me to see it,
instead of giving me a birthday present?” And the
mother, in pained, righteous tones, said, “I could not
take you to see that class of play, my dear, but if the
‘Sign of the Cross’is played again, perhaps your father
and I may take you, if you’re a good girl.” And, some-
how, I felt so sorry for that little kiddie.
Herman Merivale died the other week. He narrowly
escaped brilliancy as a dramatist and litterateur, but
seemingly he was an ill-ballasted vessel. Of late years he
got among the breakers. Perhaps it was a misfortune
for him that he grew up among scholars and writers of
the heavy sort, and so acquired the same early taste for
bohemiaiiism that comes just as naturally to the sons of
parsons. His father, the original Herman, was professor
of political economy at Oxford University before he
became permanent Under-Secretary for India, and his
uncle, Charles Merivale, was also a scholar who published
histories of Rome and the Romans. The late Herman
Merivale made his first mark as a playwright (in collabora-
tion with Palgrave Simpson) when he was stiff on the
sunny side of 30. Some years later he wrote “ Forget-
me-Not,” and “ All for Her,” the latter an adaptation of
Dickens’ “ Tale of Two Cities.” “ The White Pilgrim ”—a
poetical play—was his finest work, and there were others
more or less successful, all written before he was 45.
“ Forget-me-Not ” will doubtless be revived from time to
time in the future by actresses who have pretensions to
follow Genevieve Ward in the name part. The English
version of “ Fedora,” Merivale’s most lucrative job,.will
continue to keep his name before the public if theatrical
managers gave it a line on the bill which, as a rule, they
don’t. His knowledge of French and German—he wrote
graceful verses in both languages—was a deterrent to
Merivale’s talent for original composition. Having made
money easily by turning foreign plays into English, he
was easily discouraged by the comparatively poor finan-
cial results of his harder and better labor.
The actress scatters her real self in shreds over her im-
nersonations.
Oscar Asche, the ex-Australian, pitching another
romantic story to a London interviewer: “I had a good
deal of open-air life when I was ranching in Australia.
I spent my childhood at Geelong, in Victoria, and I well
remember when at school near the Dandenong Ranges,
seeing four horsemen gallop past. It was the famous
bushranger Ned Kelly" and his gang, escaping to the
mountains, with the police only a few hours behind. But
I had always intended to be an actor ; the ranching was
merely a preliminary.” Asche onght to collaborate with
Melba in a book of 'Australian reminiscences. Probably
Melba would remember that her father, “ the colonel” of
modern magazine fiction, was acting as leader of the police
who came galloping after Ned Kelly, at a distance of only
forty miles, what time Oscar, the future rancher, was
watching the mad career of the fugitives as they dashed
past the Dandenong Ranges. But why did Oscar forget
to mention that the bushranger, digging the spurs into
his foam-flecked steed, shouted : “ Ride like h—l boys,
we haven’t six hours to spare ! ” ?
Another advertisement for Australia ! Nellie Stewart,
in a letter to her sister Docy (Mrs. H. R. Harwood), says
that she received congratulatory telegrams re her Ameri-
can success from the Governments of New South Wales
and Victoria. If she had been a Maorilander, Seddon’s
telegram of congratulation would have run to half-a-
column.
Melbourne University Jubilee should be made thrilling
by a professional product, aGreek ode, “bookand lyrics”
by Prof. Tucker, “ music” by Prof. Petersen. This medi-
aeval custom, resurrected to give tone to the exciting
ceremonial, threatens uncanny results. Because the in-
stitution has lingered on for 50 years, that is no reason
why a hvmn and harmonium virtuoso should paddle in
music, and an erudite but uninspired philologist rejoice
in discreet verse (luckily, it’s Greek; the Professor’s
superior English verse seldom, if ever, condescends to the
publication level). The classic concoction seems likely to
amuse the ignorant even if it doesn’t instruct them.
Statement (received from London) in The Bulletin that
comedian George Giddens would be with the forthcoming
firm of Willoughby and Ward'is now said to be erroneous.
“S. ”: “ Prodigal Son, ”as produced at Melbourne Royal,
represents tbe joint effort of Mr. “ Haul” Caine and Mr.
Arthur Collins, which latter gentleman runs a thriving
melodrama foundry in London. There is a spell of prob-
lem in the play, and some little feverish effort at Neitz-
schean talk, borrowed from the German of Sudermann,
but the mixtureis by Collins, who is incapable of raising
Caine, and consequently the dead level of the thing is
below par. It is necessary to kill off the Prodigal in the
third act, and so a hairy and hoary medical inventor dis-
charges a pistol in the gambling hell. The lights are all
on, and as they come in all the people declare aloud that
the Prodigal has shot himself, but as there is no corpse
anywhere available, it must be assumed that the
Prodigal, after shooting himself, carefully removed his
own body, and hid it in the dust barrel. This magni-
ficent disregard for detail is in the best melodramatic
vein of the indefatigable Collins. The moral appears to
be that the prodigal son of our time, if he likes fat veal,
must carry his own calves, for the cupboard is bare when
Hall Caine’s prodigal returns. Bland Holt’s mounting is
good enough for anything, which means it is much too
good for Caine and Collins.
“Melb.” : I found the Cycloramaa wonderful sight at
the last Sunday night concert. Over 2000 people there.
An immense, circular, whitewashed place, with the crowd
heaped up in tiers nearly all round. A good orchestra
was prettily embowered in artificial chrysanthemum
flowers of pink, white, and blush red.
A modest person is George Bernard Shaw. He has
written to London Eka stating that he never gave Henry
Irving the offer of any of his plays, and adds : “ If I had,
and Mr. Irving had refused the offer, it would now be
very difficult to convince the public that such a refusal
was not a gross error of judgment.”
“ Hiphen ” : I want that pious person, Varley the Vir-
tuous, to refund me 3s. I read of the emotions roused in
him by the Primroses at Rickards’ show, so went to have
3s. worth of similar emotion. I didn’t get it. They are
very tame little wild flowers indeed, with artificial-look-
ing stems. The show is as wildly immoral as the action
of a household fly in walking on the ceiling. First they
stand on their feet and wave their heads. Then they
stand on their heads and wave their feet. The only dif-
ference is that one method is more restful. The very
name “ Primrose ” exudes modesty. But even if they
called themselves “ Scarlet Runners ” it wouldn’t matter.
Doesn’t the poet say, “ A primrose by any other name
would wave its feet,” or something to that effect?
Yon der Mehden, bandmaster of Fitzgerald Bros.’
Circus, who is now on a holiday in San Francisco, writes :
“ Musgrove has surprised the Americans with his Nellie
Stewart Co. He put the piece on splendidly, and had the
largest orchestra that had ever been known for a dramatic
show. The people here are crazy over Nellie’s acting,
and the papers say that the Australians have astonished
Americans with their stage settings.”
The Melbourne inquiry into the Gem-
brook railway disaster, which was apparently not
intended to elucidate much, has uninten-
tionally stumbled—or been pushed—on to some-
thing that nobody suspected. The Board went up
to Fern Tree Gully to inspect the damaged loco-
motive, and there ah interfering person named
Hulse—one of the most prominent committee-
men at the time of the strike, but now
out of the department—drew Engineer Ker-
not’s attention to the fact that it wasn’t
altogether the state of the track or the speed
that was responsible for the trouble, but chiefly
the engine itself. This remarkable machine,
which Tait used for taking heavy loads of
humanity for an airing, was in such a state of dis-
repair that “one left hand wheel was carry-
ing cwt. more dead weight than the right
hand one. The flange of one of the wheels was
very much worn and the bearings had worn
down to such an extent that the body of
the engine was riding on the frame instead
of on the springs. The centre of gravity had so
shifted that travelling on a curve was necessarily
dangerous. A driver had reported that the
engine was running low, and a solid steel plate
had been inserted, which would have the effect of
aggravating instead of remedying the defect.”
The board went home pondering deeply. Mean-
while a lot of departmental persons are wishing
that interfering people like Hulse would mind
their own business.

The elusive treasure on board the sunken
Elingamite has apparently gone for ever. Wel-
lington (M.L.) hotel keeper Gow, who equipped a
second expedition to secure the £17,000, was
sorely disappointed. The hull of the wrecked
vessel had been completely shattered since the
first expedition examined it, and it it had been
burst up with dynamite it couldn’t have been a
greater wreck. There was not the slightest trace
of the money, which was in wooden boxes, en-
closed in an iron tank or strong room. It has
been suggested that a couple of Americans, who
h id a boat on a fishing expedition in the locality,
may have happened to get near the Three Kings
where the buried treasure lay, and having, by a
queer coincidence, a diver and all appliances
aboard, fished up the money, then blown up the
wreck to conceal the fact. At any rate, the
money is gone, the wreck is gone, and the two
Americans are gone, and hotel-keeper Gow, cf
Wellington, has a gone feeling at the pit of the
stomach.
WHAT MIGHT HAPPEN TO A KING
IN AUSTRALIAN.
During: a thunderstorm near Brisbane yesterday a large
number of young fish fell with the rain.’ They were all
alive.
With the fussy vexation of a wet hen,
N.S.W. Divorce Court Justice Simpson lately
condemned the Act which allows a Judge of the
District Court to hear evidence in divorce cases,
and to send the depositions on to the Divorce
Justice for a verdict. The idea is to save expense
to parties in country towns which are visited by
District Court but not by Supreme Court Judges.
Justice Simpson snorted : —
The District Court was an inferior court, whereas the
Supreme Court was not, and the District Court must be
kept within its jurisdiction. Whether lie would ever
send important issues to be tried by District Court
Judges was another matter. He liked to see the wit-
nesses himself. Deception was practised over and over
again, especially in cases of desertion.
N.S.W. Supreme Court Judges are just now
rather often politely snubbed by the High Court
Judges. They seem to like to pass it on to the
District Court men.

ing asking that paper’s tame lawyer how long she
must have deserted her husband before she could
claim a divorce.
The man behind
the camera was ’most
always a superfluity,
but his latest freak
gives him an honored
place among the
Fiends. He operates
now with what he
calls the “ revolver-
camera ” —a murder-
ous-looking imple-
ment that closely re-
sembles a six-s.; < :ter.
Occasionally, while
practising his mac-
hinations in places
where he is not
wanted, he strikes
trouble. Recently,
in a nicely-sheltered
spot in the Gardens,
a couple were rudely
interrupted in their
fond observations by
a sharp, clicking
sound. On looking
up, they beheld a
man covering them
with what looked
like a revolver. The
lady instantly, and
without any diffi-
culty, threw a fit.
Not so the man ; he
jumped, and that
jump landed him on
the surprised neck of
the photographer, who,
for the next two minutes,
wondered what particular
part of him was getting hit
most. After finding out that
he was not quite dead, the
camera-man explained, and the
explanation caused more strife;
this time he concluded that he
had better be dead.

"* * * *
Nobody ever seems to have the least diffi-
culty in escaping from Victorian lock-ups; and
since it has become known that alleged prisoners
who escape before they are sentenced are guilty of
no offence, the pastime may become a favorite one.
Two energetic young men a few months ago carved
their way out of South Melbourne lock-up with
an old pocket knife. They subsequently got out
of a country gaol by means of a bootlace. They
were experts. A week or two ago a woman walked
out of Little Bourke-street lock-up. The door was
left on the chain ; the man who put the chain on
had been used to plump women, and this woman
being thin, squeezed out and strolled off. A few
days later a thief in Collingwood lock-up waited
till the door was opened, then he darted off, and
is still going. The only remarkable feature about
these escapes is that they happen so seldom.
With the exception of the Bourke-street West
establishment, there isn’t a lock-up in the metro-
politan area that is worth shucks. A sober,
ingenious man could get out of any of them. They
were built for drunks, and only a very drunk mail
need stay in them. They are flimsy where they
should be stout, and stout where it doesn’t matter.
They are dirty, evil-smelling, ill-ventilated, and
inhuman. Still, Thomas Bent won’t spend three-
halfpence in improving them. That is the way of
Thomas.
* * * *
The garrotte has, after some years ab-
sence, been once more installed as one of the
terrors that walk by . night in Melbourne’s un-
lighted, unpoliced suburban streets. A city mer-
chant was garrotted at 11 o’clock on a recent
Saturday night, a few hundred yards from the
main street in Kew, in a well-populated resi-
dential street. He was carrying home the day’s
takings, and though the garrotters got away with
£lO, he managed to slip 16 sovereigns between
his vest and shirt while they were dealing with
him, and they went away £l6'short. The merchant
lustily bellowed for help ; but none came. The
garrotters drove in a buggy quietly through the
streets of Kew, with the merchant racing and
roaring flOyds. behind ; but no one stopped them,
and there were, of course, no policemen about.
The suburbs of Melbourne are ridiculously under-
patrolled by police. Most of the suburban beats
take four hours to walk round, and none take less
than two hours. Every criminal knows that when
a bobby passes a certain point he won’t return for
two, three, or four hours, as the case may be, and
consequently the criminal gets ahead in his work
free from all worry on that score. All the crime
of any note that has been done in Melbourne for
the past year has been worked in the suburbs.
It is so easy.
* * * 9
Waxcellency Madden missed two beautiful
texts last week, just through not being there.
One couple was married, took a furnished room in
Fitzroy, and luxuriated in wedded bliss for three
whole days ; then the bridegroom departed to a
ploughing job at Werribee. Apparently he
struck an endless furrow, or ploughed himself in,
or something, for that was the last seen of him. In
the other case the husband was so paralysed by joy
over his marriage that he started a wild course of
tanglefoot that lasted, with a few brief intervals,
for 12 months. Then the bride wearied, and took
a billet, while her husband congratulated her on
her pluck in doing so, and faded away to parts
unknown. And if only Mr. Madden "had been
there he would have spread himself at great
length and to no purpose, and so filled a gap in
the weariful scheme of things.
* * * *
The proposed conversion of Princess Ena
of Battenberg to Roman Catholicism to qualify
her for the well-paid billet of Queen of Spain
recalls the fact that royalties are the prize religious
turncoats of the world. Take the British royal
family as a sample. King Edward’s sister was
converted to the Evangelical State Church to
qualify for the job of being Queen of Prussia.
His daughter Maud Charlotte suddenly saw the
beauties of Lutheranism when she married Karl of
Denmark. Two of his nieces, Sophia and Alex-
andra, saw that the Greek Church was the only
square thing when there was no other way of
getting a royal job ; and now Miss E. Battenberg
proposes to be honestly and sincerely converted to
Roman Catholicism, seeing that there is a rich
marriage hanging on to it. The person who is
Head of the Church seems to acquire from too
great familiarity with the institution, a great and
horrid contempt for the Body and Tail of the
Church, and among royalties apostacy becomes a
fine art. Britain may yet see one of its princesses
turning Buddhist to qualify for marriage with
the royal family of Monkeydom.
A VAST UNDERTAKING.
“ Yes, my hoy, I intend, to make a decent thing
of that before I’m done.”
“It seems to me you’ll have a job.”
One S. Australian member of the Federal
Parliament, anyway, is beginning to wander
whether there may not be two sides to
the weary old row between his
State, Victoria, and N.S. Wales re-
garding the rights to the Murray
waters. The Joe Carruthers brand
of officials in N.S. Wales and Vic.
say that the water in the stream is
theirs—anyhow, they and Queens-
land own it, for it is their rain
that makes the river flow. And
S. Australia says that you can’t
divide rights in a stream like that,
and that the man who owns the
bottom end of a river has a right
to some water. The vast row
over that point which has been
brewing for years, and is just
beginning to feed the lawyers,
is having a sort of dress re-
hearsal on a small scale. It
seems that President Baker, of
the Senate, owns a place at
Morialta, in the Hills outside
Adelaide; and on the same
creek above Morialta Tom
Playford, who represents the
Deakin Government in the
Senate, hashismarketgarden.
The season has been dry, and
the tomatoes of Playford and
his neighbors (though Play-
ford’s own specialty is cher-
ries) have been calling for
more water than usual, and
It. 0. Baker has had to go
short ; so in the row which
has resulted, Senator Play-
ford has been N.S. Wales,
and President Baker has
been S. Australia, and, as
remarked previously, Play-
ford, having regard for his
wilting vegetables, is begin-
ning to wonder whether there
isn’t something to be said for
N.S. Wales. But as the law-
yers haven’t been called into the business, there is a
much better chance of the dispute being settled
promptly and equitably than there is in connec-
tion with the inter-State row.
* * * *
A Wagga (N.S.W.) decision has upset the
notion that clergymen have any special privileges
in speaking of other folks’ characters. A Sydney
cleric, while crossing over in a ferry boat, met a
Junee cleric and asked after a certain family. He
was told that a member of it had got a gaol sen-
tence in Westralia, and so, later on, condoled with
the man’s sister. The information wasn’t correct,
and the Junee clergyman, action for slander being
taken, was struck heavily in the eye by a person
called Damages.
The trustees of a Melbourne suburban
cemetery are finding out what a magnificent mess
a really energetic messer can make in a cemetery.
The messer in this case was the secretary, now of
parts unknown. The trustees knew things were
bad; but the depths and heights of the tangle
didn’t occur to them until, the other day, a local
citizen who had bought an 8 x 4 allotment years,
ago proceeded to bury a relative, and was stag-
gered to find that the grave was already filled with
promiscuous and unknown dead. The deceased
was accommodated with a temporary home while
the trustees poked around trying to discover an
empty grave that was really empty, while the
citizen himself went to the Minister of Works and
unloaded a bagful of insulting remarks. But the
trustees are a calm, casual set of gentlemen, who
have decided to view the disturbance dispassion-
ately from a distance, and they disclaim all
responsibility.
* * * *
Apropos N.S.W. policemen’s revolvers.
Even the so-called “escorts” are supplied with
weapons that would probably do more harm to the
shooter than to the party shot at The coach from
Wilcannia to Broken Hill (which has been robbed
of valuable opal at least once) carries a policeman
armed with an old Winchester and an ancient
Colt. And what hope has Constable X. of
hitting anything, anyhow ? They hand him six
cartridges, and tell him he mustn’t use them except
in the execution of his duty—and it isn’t his duty
to practise hitting things. Also, as if the Depart-
ment knew that the things are not worth using
anyway, the N.S.W. policeman must wear his re-
volver on his left hip, where a man, a mounted man
especially, can only reach it with difficulty. The
best thing would be to issue an order to bobbies to
always wear their shooters above their kitchen
mantel-pieces.
* * * *■
It isn’t good to fall under suspicion of the
law in Brisbane. Just transpired, during a police
court case, that while he is in the cells awaiting
trial the prisoner’s fare consists simply of dry
bread and water —and Enoggera water at that.
Yet the law assumes that he is still perfectly inno-
cent, which is more than can be assumed of Bris-
bane’s water supply, anyhow.
• * * *
Victoria is quite up-to-date in health
matters ! The other day a female patient was
conveyed from Mirboo, in Gippsland, to Sale hos-
pital, in a railway van wherein cream, vegetables,
and other eatables- are carried. She was subse-
quently found to be in the last stages of typhoid,
and died the following day. Thereupon, there
being now no possible doubt about the disease, the
bedding and clothing were carefully returned in
the same van!

S UE: “My word, we better hurry 'n' put five

According to evidence given before the
N.S.W. Arbitration Court, some of the children
under the care of the State are boarded out to
dairymen, who are actually paid for their keep
whilst enslaving them to the cow Joss. Dairying,
as regards children, is a noxious trade, and no
State child should be subjected to the risk of
being put through the miserable mill in which the
cow-man so often grinds his own children’s lives.

Some are gone to gaol—we’ve missed ’em,
Some are going, and we claim
That the finger-printing system,
Opposite each hero’s name
Should be used ; then we’d be wary
When We saw why he was sent
To confinement solitary—
Saw it on the monument.
For this scroll of fame of his’ll
Be a record perfect when
To the sculptor’s cheerful chisel
We have joined the warder’s pen.
Where the sentimental tear rose
New significance is lent
In the records of our heroes
Graven on the monument.
When the glory’s all departed,
And that pompous sculpture falls
Into gibbers that are carted
To be pounded into spalls,
All the pris’ners, as they fret, ’ll
Weep upon each load that’s sent,
As they smash their names to metal—
Smash that famous monument.

Just to show what the Victorian railways
really can do, the special train that took the
Inquiry Board up to the scene of the Gembrook
disaster, dashed into a trolly manned by two
repairers, and knocked it to pieces; and on the
return trip the train pulled up only a few feet
short of a wood-carter’s team, placidly dwelling
on a level crossing. Also, a train went off the
line and bruised T. Bent.

* * » *
The foolishness of rushing into law has
been brought home to two residents of the
Hastings River (N.S.W.). Plaintiff holds an
oyster lease, and, in order to afford board and
residence for his oysters, he dropped into the
water a choice collection of bricks, kerbstones,
and other boulders. The river is shallow, and the
defendant used to bump his boats on these locks,
and scare the oysters out of their wits. Further,
the sawdust from defendant’s sawmill fell
into the water and gave the shellfish indiges-
tion. This was the plaintiff’s case. De-
fendant answered that the boulders among
the oysters were menaces to navigation,
and that he didn’t ask the wretched fish to steal
his sawdust. The parties hied them to Sydney
with a perfect cloud of witnesses, the district
being torn in two over the question. Five bar-
risters, including two K.C.’s, were engaged, and
the Judge, after hearing all these expensive
people, merely said in effect that it was a fool of a
case, and the parties had better go home again.
Which they did, after paying the cloud of wit-
nesses, and the five barristers, including the two
K.C.’s.
* * * *
The Gembrook (Vic.) train accident has
brought forth some fine large specimens of con-
tradictory evidence from railway experts :
William Blinkhorn, guard of the train that was derailedt
estimated that the train would be travelling at the rate
of not more than 10 miles an hour.
Harold Waters, porter, stationed at Upper Fern Tree
Gully, stated that he was riding in the guard’s van of the
train that was derailed. The train at the time seemed to
be going at a good speedi-faster than usual.
John Lajme, porter, who was riding in the guard’s van,
estimated the rate of speed at about 10 or 12 miles an
hour between Belgrave and the level crossing.
Neil M'lnnes, a superintendent road-master on the Vic.
railways, said he was in the van of the train on the day of
the accident, as a lt had been going 27 miles
an hour at the level crossing. It was not contemplated
that any train on a narrow-gauge line would ever be run
at that speed.
William Hambley, relieving porter, said he was in the
fifth or sixth truck from the engine. The rate of travel-
ling did not seem in the least excessive.
Lawrence Kenny, line repairer, deposed that he was in
the van of the wrecked train. For the last 500yds. he
thought they were travelling about 30 miles an hour. He
remarked, “ She is bolting.” Never before had he gone
down the gradients on the line at such a speed.
Some railway “experts” are like most jockeys,
seemingly “ deficient in the knowledge of pace.”
* * * *
To be “ found ” on licensed premises may
be an offence under N.S.W. Liquor Act. But
what is the meaning of being “found ” ? A Bow-
street magistrate decided lately, in a London case
brought into court under a similarly worded sec-
tion, that a man whom a constable saw going into
a public-house and whom a sergeant saw coming
out, was not found on the premises. And a Bow-
street magistrate is somebody these days.

At Gembrook, the scattered Gippsland
(Yic.) district where the notorious switchback
railway ends up against a gum-stump, the Protes-
tant residents have been toiling on without a par-
son for many years, but the long felt want has
become unbearable at length, and the godly set-
tlers have set about building themselves a church.
The church is well advanced, and now applications
are invited for the position of resident sin-shifter;
but the miki of Gembrook are frugal souls,
and seek to kill two birds with one stone.
The district is also urgently in need of
a doctor, mainly for the purpose of assist-
ing at local increases, and so what the dis-
trict demands is a doctor who can preach or a
preacher who can doc. In return for the services
of so gifted a creature, a certain number of
settlers guarantee to place 6d. in the plate every
Sunday, shine or rain, and in addition the minis-
tering angel is allowed “ all he can make.” It is
stipulated that the 6d. a week does not cover
medical expenses, and it is also understood that
in. the event of a little stranger needing the
doctor’s services, the congregation will excuse the
parson.'' The' little'stranger is to have precedence
every time.
Allen Taylor, Mayor of Sydney, has called
a meeting of all the public and private bodies
which have a statutory right to tear up the city
streets, and he hopes that some arrangement may
be arrived at among them. At present, no sooner
has the gas person finished digging up a footpath
than the water people decide to put a water main
in the same hole ; but they don’t think of it until
the gas gentlemen have filled up their hole. And
when the water-pipe is down and the pathway
mended the telephone department arrives to dig
it up again. And then when the hydraulic
people, and the electric lighting people, and the
sewerage people have all come and gone, the gas
people are about due again. In between times
the corporation steam roller goes up and down.

“Lil”: What a farce is the use of the
word “ solemnise ” in connection with many
marriage ceremonies ! A
reverend gentleman in my
suburb used to auction
second-hand clothing and
furniture in the intervals of
solemnising marriages. He
would just as soon solemnise
a pair of moleskin pants or
an alarm-clock as the knot
that many of his customers
have since sought to cut in
the Divorce Court. Both
brought in the shekels, and
his supposedly facetious pul-
pit stories served to advertise
either business. Then con-
sider the solemnity of the
marriage shop and the Voice
that Breathed o’er Eden
breathing over the counter.
Even when the chief of the
matrimony shop is not a
clergyman, there is still the
tame parson, eager for his
fee, lurking about the pre-
mises, ready to “ solemnise ’’
marriages between the
over-youthful, the drunk,
or the imbecile, and not
too scrupulous about unit-
ing a white girl to a Hindu
or a Chinaman.

N.S.W. Railway Commissioner Kirkcaldie
complained at the Railway Inquiry that Chief
Commissioner Oliver had not shaken hands with
him more than twice in four years. Whereupon
Judge Rogers: “Pooh, I know plenty of people
with whom I don’t shake hands that I like much
better than those with whom I have to.” Which
was a rather neat Rogers for his Oliver.
Justice Holroyd somewhat staggered his
audience at the Ballarat Court last week by-
sentencing a man to three years hard labor for the
crime of sending a murderously worded letter to
W. B. Grose, a former M.L.A. for Creswick (Vic.).
The prisoner’s foolish ness was incid en tal to a mining
matter concerning which he felt very mad, and the
jury accepted his statement that he had never
seriously thought of killing Mr. Grose. He merely
talked w'ildly on paper. The Law allows 15 years
for such things, but because the accused dian’t
mean it Holroyd let him off with three. Moral:
If you have no intention to kill a man, don’t put
your empty threat into writing. A verbal promise
of murder is a comparatively venial offence, and
can be done through a telephone.
* * * *
“ Yanco ” : T’other day saw a near-sighted
kid, about five years old, wearing huge glasses,
perched like a cat on the parapet of a city pub.
The top of the parapet was about 15in. wide and
sloped outwards, and then there was a sheer drop
of about 80ft. on to a hard pavement or a passing
pedestrian. The mother was reclining on a lounge
close at hand, and when I suggested that possibly
it was a dangerous position for the goggled infant
to occupy, she merely said “Yes, it isn’t very
safe. I told him that before.”

A QUESTION OF PREJUDICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On Thursday last Sir Reginald Talbot, 0 ">\ ernor of
Victoria, spoke of an apparent prejudice in favor of
foreign-made goods. On Saturday a Sydney daily paper
asked : “ What leads his Excellency to suppose that
people are prejudiced in favor of goods made in other
countries ? ”
Perhaps it’s just a funny little notion of his own
That woollens might be woven even where the wool is
grown i
Perhaps there’s someone told him that a prejudice exists
In favor of behaving as distraught philanthropists !
Perhaps he isn’t deaf and dumb ; perhaps he isn’t blind !
Perhaps lie’s not afflicted with an over-addled mind !
'Tis just a supposition, an assumption, or a guess—
Perhaps he got tiie notion from the Sydney daily

BOUGHT A GUN.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
“ Only things in the shape of guns.” —Tariff Commission
evidence.
The young man outward hieth,
In the grinning noonday sun,
While the pretty parrot flieth
And the raiding rabbits run ;
He sees their quick tails twinkle
And remarks, “ I’ve got a wrinkle ;
Here’s sport for me—l think ’ll
Buy a gun.”
Our Ernies, Willies, Harries,
They are thrifty in their fun,
For the gun each sportsman carries
Is a thirty-shilling one.
So our Willies, Harries, Ernies
Come not backward from their journejs,
And the verdict we return is—
“ Bought a gun.”

Her Health Wrecked.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“Four years ago I started to vomit blood,”
said Mrs. Mary E. A. Peek, of Blende : st.,
North Broken Hill. “The doctor said my
stomach, liver and kidneys were all diseased.
For the next three years life was one round of
misery. I went from bad to worse. At' last a
kind of paralysis settled in my right arm. I was
as good as crippled. Many a time I prayed to
God to let me die. But, in the end, I was cured
for good by thirty boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. They actually saved my life.
“ For a long time my health had been wretched,
said Mrs. Peek. “ Every few days I had a bilious
attack. Headaches came on just over my right
eye—and then shot through to the other side of
my head till I wanted to scream. My nerves
went to pieces, and I jumped with fright from no
cause at all. I could not get out of my head the
thought that something dreadful was going to
happen. I was so down-hearted that I took no
interest in anything. I fairly hated the sight of
food. I had no energy for my house-work. An
hour’s ironing left me gasping for breath. Every
week I grew paler and thinner. My cheeks fell
in, and great dark circles came under my eyes. I
looked the picture of bad health. I was as weak
as a kitten. One day my head suddenly started
to swim, and the next thing I was laid out in a
dead faint. After that I had these fainting fits
every few days. When down town I often felt
the sudden turn coming on, and I would have to
go in somewhere to lie down till the fit passed off.
“Early that winter I caught cold, and every
bone started to ache. There was a sharp pain
between my shoulder-blades that cut right into
my lungs. Every time I breathed something
stabbed me in the right breast. The pain from
my shoulders soon settled in the small of my back.
Sometimes it was a dull, dragging ache—sometimes
it was like a red-hot brick burning into my flesh.
Day or night I was never free from it. In bed I
lay first on one side and then on the other—but I
could get no ease. But the worst pain of all came
in the pit of my stomach. A few days after, I
started to vomit blood. That gave me such a
Shock that Mr. Peek sent straight for the doctor.
As* soon as he saw me he said my stomach and
liver were in a bad state, but they were nothing
to my kidneys.”
‘ ‘ Why, doctor, ” I said, ‘ ‘ It can’t be my kidneys.
My back aches fit to break—but I have twenty
other things worse than that.”
“ Oh, backaches aren’t the only sign of kidney
disease,” he said. “Ninety-nine backaches out
of a hundred have nothing to do with the kidneys
—and many a person dies from kidney disease
without ever having a backache in his life.”
“The doctor understood my case all right,”
Mrs. Peek went on, “but his medicine did me no
good. My skin was dry and hard, but often I
would break out in a cold sweat. Every week I
grew weaker and sicker. The fainting fits came
on more often, and every few days I vomited
blood. God only knows how I went through it
all. I would have welcomed death with all my
heart. The strangest thing of all was the queer
feeling in my right arm. It would be all pins and
needles one day—and the next day it seemed to
go dead. My fingers grew numb, and my whole
arm was useless. It would stay like that for two
or three weeks at a time. Everyone said it was
paralysis.
“I had given up the last atom of hope when
Mrs. Kern, of Hindley-st., Adelaide, made me
get some Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. I owe my
life to them. The first box gave me a great
appetite, but I was well on with the tenth box
before I stopped vomiting blood. After that the
headaches and backaches eased up. When I had
finished twenty boxes I was a different woman.
Everyone told me how much better I looked. I
kept getting more pills from Temby and Lower,
in Argent-st. When I had finished the 30th
box there wasn’t a healthier woman in Broken
Hill. Never again will I waste my money on
doctors. Whenever I see any of my friends in
ill-health, I tell them that half-a-dozen boxes of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are worth all the doctors
in Australia,”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills don’t act on the
bowels. They don’t tinker with mere symptoms.
They contain just the elements that actually
make new blood. That is why they are the surest
cure for all blood diseases like anaemia, biliousness,
indigestion, rheumatism, lumbago, kidney and
liver troubles and skin troubles like pimples and
eczema. And, for just the same reason, they are
the greatest help in the world for growing girls
who need new blood, and for women who are
troubled with irregular health, especially those of
forty-five or fifty, when the blood becomes de-
ranged again. But you must get the genuine
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People—price
2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d., from all chemists
and storekeepers, or direct by mail from the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney. If you are in
doubt about your illness, write to the same
addressfor free medical advice.

ROBEY
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The end-of-summer rains have been good oyer
the bigger half of Australia ; and, given nothing specially
Outrageous in the way of hot weather during the next few
weeks, the winter should be well grassed ; and for the
sheep-grower there are few things more important than
a well-grassed winter. Things, therefore, continue to
look extra-good for Australia, and the Treasurers ought to
he so flush of money that anything in the shape of a loan
would be viewed as an insult. In Victoria and Queens-
land the Treasurers do take something of that view of
the matter ; but N. S. Wales has J. Carruthers for its
Premier, and J. Carruthers apparently hasn’t a soul above
borrowing even in a time of extraordinary affluence. S.
Australia and Tasmania have no very definite views on
the subject; and Westralia hasn’t yet woke up to the fact
that its affluence is fading, and that it will probably soon
find itself lying out on the wood-heap or under the water-
trough, with its cheque “busted” and its credit “ex-
ploded.” But cheque and credit hang out so far, and it
is still drinking hard.
The past month’s revenue figures have been
particularly satisfactory :

An ostrich could do that much in the way of finance.

Except for the sake of frill arid peacock-strutting there is

need to be done, and when the next annual accounts are -
issued it will be found that thousands of pounds of loan
money has been spent on works which should have been
paid for out of revenue. Carruthers, however, continues
to pile up the loan bill and curtail the ordinary expendi-
ture ; and four months from now he will draw attention
to his vast surplus—-and the fact that the surplus is all
rubbish, seeing that over £1,500,000 will have been
borrowed, won’t he noticed. Probably something of the

Victoria is not borrowing for new works ; wherefore
Bent’s “ fake ” .differs from Carruthers’ in this respect—■ .
Carruthers pays for things with loans, and builds up a
surplus that way ; while Bent holds off necessary public
works, and, instead of paying for them as; he goes along,
builds up a surplus, makes a splash, and then proceeds to
spend the money. It's" poor, , cheap fireworks; but,
anyhow,-, it hasn’t the evils of Carruthers’ system.
The situation, so far as the wretched returns
furnished by some States allow it to be stated, is some-
thing like this: —
;N.S. Wales : We don’t hear quite as much lately about

the yarn has probably only 'been packed away,in some
handy place, for use again when politics grow a little
brisker; wherefore this brick, showing, the expansion of
N.S.W. revenue under Federation, is heaved at it in its
hiding place (the figures are for the July period
of each financial year) :

This shows an improvement, compared with four years
back, of £1,092,924. And yet, though matters have im-
proved at the rate of over £1,500,000 a year, Carruthers
quite virtuously chirps that he thinks he-will be able to
get along with only £1,500,000 of borrowed money this
year !
Victoria : Only revenue returns are published ; and
under the circumstances they are very little good. Last
year Bent contrived a surplus of just over half-a-million ;
"his revenue this year, so far, is £193,984 ahead of the pre-
vious year’s. But whether extra expenditure has mopped
up that gain there is no way of telling, though it doesn’t
seem likely.

But that doesn’t fully, express the improvement. In the
last days of the old Continuous Ministry, when the country
was scared off the Labor party because it didn’t consist of
“business men,” the things paid for with the Biggest
Debt on Earth were shockingly neglected, and hundreds
of thousands of pounds’ worth of buildings, &c., were
perishing. Having got things fairly shipshape in the
Treasury, Kidston is now spending a little on works and
in preparing land for settlement; in these two depart-
ments, Works and Lands, £96,906 has been spent this
year, as against £75,760 last year.
S. Australia : Quarterly returns only.
Westralia : This State shows a surplus so far of £13,479.
But it doesn’t set out its Joan expenditure ; and it has a
Premier and Treasurer whose policy is borrow, and borrow
again. Its apparently improved position is solely due to
fewer works and services being charged to ordinary
account; its revenue, which ought to be increasing along-
with the population, is absolutely shrinking.
Tasmania:.No expenditure or loan figures;, but the
revenue returns are a shade better than the previous
year’s.

“Other N’.S.W. stocks” consist of city and suburban
municipal debentures. The funds, apart from £71,225 in
“land and banking houses,” are distributed in the
following way : —Lent to State, 54 per cent. ; to munici-
palities, 6 per cent. ; to private borrowers on mortgage,
19 per cent. ; and .to banks, 21 per cent. The reserves
consist of a formal reserve fund, £270,660 ; mortgages
depreciation account, £45,049; and undivided profit.
No net reduction of the mortgages depreciation fund has
taken place since 1901; and that account is now in credit
to the extent of 4 per cent, of the total amount of the
advances on mortgaged. Though the Savings Bank of
N.S.W. is to the public still “ the Barrack-street bank,”
it steadily extends beyond that city and suburban con-
nection to which it was once almost wholly confined. The
deposits are now derived —
From Sydney £3,498,694
~ Suburban branches ... 1,158,920
~ Country „ .. 887,753
Four country branches were opened last year ; one of
them, at Broken Hill, has 1836 depositors already—a
number exceeded only by Newcastle among the country
branches.
The worst feature of the accounts is the extra-
vagant size of the loans made by the Savings Bank to the
private banks. Instead of lending to the mortgagor
direct, the Savings Bank lends a million and a quarter to
the banks, which in turn lend to the mortgagor, and
make, of course, their profit on the transaction. So a
quite unnecessary middleman is dragged into the busi-
ness ; and it is the sort of middleman that manages to
pay 10 and 12 per cent, on its capital. The people’s sav-
ings entrusted to a people’s bank ought to be used to
help the people—the farmer to acquire a farm, the
mechanic to acquire a home—and not to puff out
the profits of big financial concerns which do
pretty well without that little bit of help. If
Australia had a decent tariff, and thriving manufactures,
the private banks wouldn’t worry so much about this
mortgage business ; they would fin'd their own profitable
work in financing industry. Mortgage business would be
left to concerns like this savings bank and the insurance
offices. The latter, indeed, are at present cutting this
business so close that it is hardly worth while the banks
touching it—and yet in a country which has G. H. Reid
and Joe Cook, but no manufactures to speak of, what else
is there for them to touch? Big life offices are actually
lending money on Sydney property for 4 per cent., and
some banks are paying that much for some deposits;
so that field is about exhausted —even a bank which
sweats its clerks can’t make a profit by lending 4 per cent,
money at 4 per cent. The people who want the A.M.P.
to take risks in Johannesburg, Pietermaritzburg, and
other distant places, allege that something must be done,
because the society’s interest? earnings are falling so, and
bonuses must, therefore, fall too. Well, there is some-
thing that might be done : Throw over Freetrade, bring
in a tariff that will establish industries and provide an
outlet for capital, and then Si'dney wouldn’t see the
touching spectacle of General-managers French, of “ the
Wales,” and Dibbs, of the Commercial of Sydney, and
Teece, of the A.M.P., and Garvan, of the Citizens’, all
struggling to capture one meek little 4 per cent, loan on
a city freehold.
* * * *
A.M.P. policy-holders interested in the pro-
posal to take risks outside Australasia are reminded that
the meeting of members will be held to-morrow (Friday)
in Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall, at 2 in the afternoon.
