The co. has not yet issued its report in British money,
or its accounts in the form in which they are presented
in Australia and Britain. In the foregoing the American
figures are reduced to. sterling at £1 = 4-8665 dollars;
and presumably at this rate they will agree with the
figures in British money which the co. will' issue in a
month or two. Curiosity to see how a big American
office would show up last year, especially in the matter
of new business and expenses, justifies this rushing of
matters —as far as you can rush anything which is built
up on four places of decimals.
The figures are probably much better, so far as
new business goes, than most people would have expected;
the total income is over a million greater than it was in
1904, and twice as great as it was seven years ago. The
premiums, on new business, including consideration for
annuities granted, amounted to £2,924,827; in the pre-
vious year they totalled £3,315,282, and in 1903,
£3,397,234. This shows a falling away, certainly,
but to no very . alarming extent. In 1898 the
figures were £1,584,548, so that the new business
done last year was very nearly twice as great as that
done seven years ago. Taking into account the substan-
tial reduction of expenses, last year’s figures show very
clearly that the tremendous attacks made on these offices
—this office at least—have not destroyed confidence in it.
And the new business hasn’t been done only where the
news of the row hasn’t reached. A schedule is issued
with the American report showing paid-for business in
New York only. Policies assuring-£7,530,000 have been
taken out. in the year ; the total number issued was
17,294, and'the total number which ceased (through
claims, lapses, &c.) was 9530.
The gross assets in 1904 totalled £80,275,405;
on Dec. 31st, 1905, £89,555,195. The interest earnings for
the year amounted to £3,528,520, which on. the mean of
the gross assets works out at £4 3s. Id. per cent., an im-
provement of lOd. on the 1904 return. Most Australian
offices show more than this ; some show less ; but very
few English offices show as much. There is no certain
way of telling if the assets are inflated or not.
But one may assume from this very fair return that
the balance-sheet is not, anyhow, stuffed with, dead and
unprofitable securities. Also, realisation of securities
gave a net profit of £48,000, in addition to about
£BO,OOO “ realised from sale of assets not taken
credit for in balance-sheet, December, 1904 ; ” and if that
means anything it means that the securities are in the
books at less than market value. The officers’own claim
is that securities are worth in the market £1,121,000 more
than they stand at in the books: and, if it is so, it repre-
sents a solid internal reserve. There is no apparent reason
why it should not be so.
It’s a question, no doubt, whether the certificate
of the New York State Insurance Department is worth
much ; but in view of the ructions it is hardly likely that
the superintendent would give any rash certificates just
now. The department accepts the value of assets as sworn
to ; its business (it holds) is to value the co.’s obligations.
It finds, that, at December 31st, in onjer to pay its policies
and reversionary additions, the co. should have had
£77,074,000, whereas, after' paying general liabilities, it
actually had £10,857,000 in excess of .that amount; and
this amount is available forbonuses-and for contingencies.
Probably even that vast surplus won’t be sufficient to pay
bonuses of the size the fancy of some American-office
drummers has pictured ; but if the New York Life can’t
pay its policies and liberal bonuses to boot there is
something very wrong with the official valuations, which
can’t be detected on analysis.
The American offices’ weak point has been in
the matter of expenses ; they have paid too big a price
for their glittering figures. The course of the New York
Life for a few years past:—
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1896 .. £2O 4 5 per cent. I 1901 .. £lB 17 10 per cent.

1898 .. 19 15 '3 ' „ | 1903 .. 20 14 2

The figures for 1905 might easily have been made to lock
still better; over £120,000 has been written off, repie-
senting the value of certain printing plants used in the
offices, furniture, and agents’debit balances. Even allow-
ing for this, however, the rate is still-excessive. On total
income, the A.M.P.’s rate is £8 16s. 7d. per cent. But a
comparison of that kind is very deceptive. An effort has
recently been made to estimate the cost of acquiring new
business by various offices. First, renewal premiums
have been charged 5 p.c. ; then consideration for
annuities 3 p.c. ; and the difference between the total of
these items and the total expenses has been reckoned as
the cost of new business. Applying this method to the
New York Life, it is found that £2,539,000 of premiums
on new business cost £3,023,000 to get, or 119 per cent.
This, compared with Australian offices, is not by any
means appalling; the A.M.P. rate is 112 p.c. ; the
National Mutual’s 121 p.c. Yet, though it costs approxi-
mately the same to get new business in the A.M.P. and
the New York Life, one has an expense rate of £iB 9s.
p.c. of total income, the ‘other of only £8 16s. 7d. p.c.
The explanation lies in this : the New York’s new pre-
miums (which cost 119 p.c. to get) are about one-seventh
of the whole income, while in the A.M.P. they are only
one-nineteenth. This is what the rush to get big figures
means—while it lasts its costliness is a drag on the office,
and the bigger the new figures in proportion to the old,
the bigger the drag while the rush lasts.

This co., with a paid capital now of £42,138, sells pro-
perty on terms ; and part of its reserves is a mortgagors’
contingent account, to meet possible losses on the
sale of properties whose purchasers have de-
faulted. From this it appears that the co. takes
credit for its profits before they are fully
earned. This contingent account is now reduced to
£2490 ; on the other side, however, defaulting mort-
gagors’ balances have been reduced to £7060 ; and so late
as 1902 they reached £19,559. During the past year the
net total has been reduced by £3403, involving a loss, as
shown above, of £743 ; and in three years the reduction
has been £12,499, at a loss apparently of £2375. In addi-
tion to the outstanding mortgages in default, the contin-
gent account presumably covers the risk on £3230 of
current mortgages ; and there is another item of a similar
character, “ Balances due on land account, £8663.” Apart
from these,(items and £20,752 in deposits and cash, the
co.’s assetsnonsist of various estates (£112,769), against
which it owed on Dec. 31 on debentures and mortgages
£87,093, or £4577 less than it owed' 12 months before.
This reduction was mostly on account of Glebe property
debentures, a further instalment of which has since been
paid off. In 1900 they stood at £53,600 ;on Dec. 31st last
at £39,560. Under present arrangements no dividend
can be declared till these debentures have been paid. It is
impossible, of course, to tell the future of a co. whose busi-
ness it is to job in land ; but the foreclosed-propertiesitem,
which amounted to £23,109 in 1901, and which.has been
a serious drag on the co., is fast being got rid of; and
when that is gone, things will begin to look a shade
better—-though nothing in the-shape of real cheerfulness
can reasonably be expected from a co. which has thrown
two-thirds of its capital overboard. Still, its biggest
estate (held on a long, lease) is bringing in 9 per cent.,
and even its biggest farm-land estate shows 3| per cent. ;
so, though its debts are heavy, it isn’t in the"position of
the cold concern which borrowed to buy barren allot-
ments that return nothing but local-rate notices.

The society’s turnover is now too small to show satisfac-
tory results. Even only two years ago there was £19,827
of shareholders’ capital in the business, apart from
reserves; now there is only £14,547 ; and current ex-
penses are practically the same. The assets are nearly,all
mortgages ; and, mainly through the operation of the
State Bank, the rate for small loans to buy a home with
is so low in S. Australia that it doesn’t pay a society
like the Queen to go out and borrow in order to lend
again. So the Queen, though at present it looks sound
and solid enough, appears to have little hope of anything
better than a small, drab future.

Some of the figures for the last co.uple of years are, to an

1 per cent, bonus is paid ; but borrowers in certain cases

rately. The estimated amount received by borrowers is
in the foregoing deducted from profits. Advances now
total £38,868 (£32,344 the previous year), while on the
other side overdrafts and other debts' to the public have
been reduced to £1760. Generally a strong, sound,
healthy state of things is disclosed. But the society will,
for all that, need to keep hard at work building up
reserves. It has now £38,868 out on mortgage—£3B,B6B
that is liable to be struck by all sorts of disaster—and the
reserve to meet that liability as yet amounts to less than
6 per cent, of the total risk.

Run down the list to the line “ Profitss on brewing, &c.”
(in the balance-sheet this appears as “ Profits on brew-
ings, sales of wines and spirits, malt house, &c., after
making provision for bad and doubtful debts and trade
discounts”). In the half-year to Jan., 1903, this profit
amounted to £58,176 ; in the past half-year it was £23,320
less. Meantime management expenses have been out down
by only £7399; so there is a net falling-away on this account
of £15,921 —enough to pay nearly 6 per cent, on capital.
Beyond that the published accounts disclose nothing on
which to hang an opinion. The simple fact is that though,
in addition to the amounts written off, £57,000 has been
put into plant, properties, etc,, since .July, 1902, the
profits from brewings and sales are £15,921 less than they
were three years ago. In the table at the head of this
it will be seen that the net profits; have declined from
£25,606 at January, 1903, to £9930 at January, 1906.
But, as a matter of fact, the position is worse than
that; for whereas at January, 1903, £3507 was written
off for depreciation of plant, etc., latterly only £2500 has
been charged, notwithstanding that certainly over
£67,000 has been spent in additions to plant, properties,
&c., which should mean a heavier allowance for deprecia-
tion. The composite item into which almost all the
assets are bundled includes leasehold properties ; and
there is no indication that anything has been written off
these. Taking l the whole item, less £85,000 goodwill, the
amount charged against profit on depreciation account
has been less than l£ pel- cent, per annum ; and that cer-
tainly looks wholly inadequate. If it is, then even the
profit shown above has not been really earned.
A month ago Toohey’s shares were selling at
12s ; lately they moved along briskly to 13s. 6d. Some-
body apparently had heard that conscience or something
equally serviceable had smitten the trustees Of the two
Toohey estates, and that they proposed to do the hand-
some thing. Undoubtedly the business, when the public
rushed it, was over-capitalised ; and yet in that first full
half-year (to January, 1903) it showed a return at the rate
of £9 6s. per cent, on capital. How it made that much,
and why it can’t make it now, are matters which share-
holders might very reasonably like to find out. But as the
business evidently can’t make it now, the Toohey
estates, which are said to hold two-thirds of the
capital, propose to surrender £50,000 of their capital on
condition that the total capitalisation is written down a
further £lOO,OOO. First the Tooheys surrender £50,000 ;
then the co. (in which the Toohey estates hold about two-
thirds of the stock) writes off another £lOO,OOO ; so the
Toohey estates’ share of the £150,000 reduction will be
about £116,000, whereas if there were a proportionate re-
duction all round, their share would be only £lOO,OOO.
Practically, therefore, they make the other shareholders
a present of £16,000. Written down to £400,000 it would
require £lO,OOO to pay a half-year’s dividend of 5 percent,
per annum; and, apart from the question whether
enough has been written off for wear and-tear, the past
half-year’s profits could not have paid it., It is said
that the new Liquor Act, involving higher licenses,
means a smaller capital value of hotels ; wherefore
the need for this writing-down. , On the past full year’s
figures it is perfectly clear, however, that, presuming the
best use has been made of the assets, the latter were
much overvalued, and should have been written down by
at least £150,000. If they had been written down that
much they would still have barely shown a 5 per cent,
return. If the new law has further depressed the earning
power of the securities, a further writing-down below
£400,000 seems to be called for.

Apparently this co. has gone back to the old practice of
January and July balances; so the present accounts
cover five months only, and the dividend, nominally at
the rate of 5 per cent., works out for the shorter
period at an excessive 6 per cent, Apparently an attempt
is now to be made to ease the co. of some of
its heavy load of debt ; £3813 has been shifted in the five
months, yet mortgages and overdrafts still total £70,152,
and the interest charge for the five months was £1514,
or at the rate of £3636 per year. Recent improvement
in the look of things has been mainly the result of a
tighter rein being put on general expenses; they were
£12,805 in the half-year to February, 1905, but have now
been cut down to £9926 for five months, or at the rate of
£11,911 for a half-year. But sundry debtors (including
customers’ accounts) now stand at £19,836, against
£17,120 as at February, 1905 ; and that increase isn’t a
satisfactory feature, though it is stated that provision has
been made for debts considered bad and doubtful. Even
if sufficient provision of that sort has been made, this co.,
with its large interest-bearing debt, can’t view with
satisfaction such a pile of outstanding accounts due, upon
which it gets no interest.

out of order. Broken Hill Mines, the Mt. Lyell, and other

Important Notice to Mine Managers and
Users op Concentrators. —In the recent action for In-
fringement Wilfley v Card the decision of the Supreme
Court of Victoria did not merely refer to a Card top
which was altered without authority, but it did refer to a
Card Concentrator manufactured and worked at the New
Chum Consolidated Mine, Bendigo, under the supervision
and control of N. Guth ridge Limited, and to a Card Con-
centrator exhibited at Long’s works, Footscray, which
was officially pointed out as the true Card construction
durin°* the trial, and which defendant used for exhibiting
to customers and making tests, and on which they gave
exhibitions to witnesses for the purposes of this trial, ine
Judge specifically decided that the substance of the
Footscray table and of the No. 38 (New Chum Consolidated
Table) was the same as that of the plaintiff Wilfley and
were infringements. N. Gilthridge Limited were ordered
to pay damages and costs, the costs were taxed and the
Sheriff seized the goods of N. Guthridge Limited in Mel-
bourne and Sydney, but as they were all claimed by the
receiver for the debenture holders the execution was re-
turned unsatisfied. Bankruptcy proceedings have been
instituted in the Supreme Court of N.S. Wales to wind up
N. Guthridge Limited. As N. Guthridge Limited are still
offering to indemnify purchasers, take notice that all
users of Card tables which infringe the Wilfley patent will
be proceeded against .* The user is liable to an action as
well as the manufacturer.*

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and AVilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for'the British Nav.*
* * * *
In spite of all that is said to the contrary, the
Wilfley Co. did not get a general injunction against the
Card Concentrator. The judge’s words are “It is pro-
bable that the table as imported from America is not
open to \ny objection ; that I need not decide.” All the
Card tables in use are of this unobjectionable type.
Winding up the company will not affect the status of the
Card, which does not infringe any patents. The Card is
very largely installed in America, but no attempt is
made by the owners of the Wilfley to stop it. Mr. Wilfley,
we are told, thought it wise to sell out his interest. He
himself declared that the Card did not in any way infringe
his patents. N. Guthridge Ltd.*

* Ht #
Curious to notice how cheerfully people let other
people make their fortunes out of C.S.A., Cobar, now.
There have been two or three law actions over the litter.
In one (when it came out that J. C. Williamson hadn’t
been sticking closely to his book, but had partnered Frank
Gardner in his Cobar spec.), some of the Great Cobar
Syndicate wanted to be also declared partners in the
business. But, as partnership consists now, as far as one
can see, of the privilege of helping to pay a large sum for
a block next to a mine from which most of the bottom
has dropped out, the enthusiasm with which the
plaintiffs didn’t pursue their action was really touching.
They loved Frank Gardner like a brother, and they
seemed to almost feel ashamed of themselves for ever
wanting any of the claim. All that they would ask was
that he shouldn’t bring any action against them—in other
words, that he shouldn’t insist upon them having a share.
But Gardner and Co. wouldn’t agree to forego their right
to be generous. In the other case, in Melbourne, an
interim injunction was granted preventing the C.S.A.
Block 10 Co. from carrying out an alleged contract with
Everard Browne, as promoter, for the sale of the leases.
Grounds were that secret profit had been made, or would
be made, by Boldrewood, jun., and Co. As Boldre-
wood, jun., is to be prevented from making
secret profits, it might be expected that he would
put up just as vigorous a fight as he did in that
famous meeting with A. G. Hales many years ago, and
insist on the contract being carried out. But, up to now,'
there isn’t any fight; in fact, Browne’s friends’ worst fear
seems to be that the co. will carry out its contract and
supply him with the alleged secret profits alluded to.
Coppabella Co. (M.S.W.) has had its first half-
yearly meeting in Melbourne, with chairman Henry Gore
nearly as optimistic as ever. There was no reason, he
said, why they shouldn’t reckon they had 107,000 tons of
ore developed ; but cutting it down to 50,000 tons (which
takes in the lode exposed in workings—the other includes
scuff up to the top of the hill), that would give them
10,000 tons of concentrates, which, at present prices,
would show a profit of £36,634. But to base estimates on
present metal prices looks very like deliberately jumping
into a bog. Lead is quoted at £l6 7s. 6d., silver at 2s.
6|d.; and prices have got down to these points in the
past five years > v
Lead. Silver.
1901 .. £ll 0 0 .. 2/15-8
1902 .. 10 2 6 .; 1/911-16
1903 .. 10 18 9 .. 1/911-16
1904 .. 11 0 0 .. 2/0 7-16
1905 .. 11 18 9 .. 2/1 7-16
Coppabella concentrates are estimated at 60 p.c. lead and
lsoz. silver ; and chairman Gore says they should be
worth (at £l6 for lead and 2s. 6d. for silver) £ll ss. per
ton. “Deducting 15s. per ton for mining and milling,
£2 ss. a ton for freight, and £4 15s. for smelting and
charges, it made a total expense of £7 15s. per • ton,
leaving £3 10s. a ton profit, or a total of £36,634 on the
ore actually proved on three sides.” The ore is to be con-
centrated to one ton in five ; therefore to make a ton of
concentrates the mining and milling costs will be five times
15s. =£3 15s. per ton of concentrates, instead of, the 15s.
mentioned in the report; and even that will be low —it
cost the Broken Hill South last half-year £4 ISs. 9d. per
ton. Apart from the tremendous risk of basing estimates
upon present prices of metals, there thus seems to be a
big enough hole in the estimates for practically all
the contemplated profits to fall through. If that is the
best the co. can look forward to, then the thing had better
be given up as a bad job.
The Coppabella proposal is to issue 30,000 fully
paid shares in lieu of present issue, and offer share-
holders another 30,000 at Is. apiece, which will bring in
£l5OO. Further idea is to use this in buying some sort of
a concentrating mill. But obviously the proposal ought
to be carefully checked. Take the concentrates, as
stated, to average 60 per cent lead and 15oz. silver (the
silver contents seem low, by the way). Take then fair
average prices—£l3 10s. for lead and 2s 3d. for
silver. Total value per ton of concentrates £9 15s. 9d.
Deduct costs —mining and milling (at same rate as
Broken Hill South, about the cheapest-worked Barrier
mine), £4 13s. 9d. ; freight, £2 ss. ; smelting and charges,
£4 15s. (frequently higher); total, £ll 13s. 9d. Loss,
£llBs. per ton of concentrates, or 7s. 7d. per ton of ore.
The cost of mining and milling may, under the circum-
stances, probably be reduced, but by no great amount.
The concentrates, if they go only 60 per cent, lead and
15oz. silver, would be low grade ; B.H. South concentrates
go about 70 per cent, lead and 20oz. silver. Further, in
the estimates made nothing has been allowed for smelting
losses and deductions : and soz. must at once come off
the silver on that account.
« * • *
“Bromide”: Whatever the origin of the
Broken Hill mine fires, this fact remains—that had the
provisions of the Lead Prevention Act been complied
with, and water laid on underground, one at least of the
two outbreaks could have been extinguished soon after
discovery. But in most of the Barrier mines water is not
laid on ; consequently, by the time water was brought
from the surface, the fire had obtained a big hold. Co. ’s are
supposed to supply water and hose to minimise the dust,
and thus check “‘leading ” ; but in some cases not even
drinking water is supplied.
* * * *
The B.H. North may blame itself for its stop-
page. It should have had its boundary tight before the
fire ; for years past everybody has admitted that every
Broken Hill mine should be cut off in this way from the
others—that the mines on the line should be just like the
water-tight compartments in a modern ship. But the
grab for dividends stood in the way of what everybody
knew ought to be done. Even after the fire started, the
North dawdled. Airways might even then have been
blocked with bricks ; but the co. waited till the workings
were filled with gas, and the.n the work became dangerous
and costly. It is because of the danger, and because it is
certain that, in spite of the danger, the work will have to
be done whenever a fire occurs, that the Mines Depart-
ment should take a hand and insist upon these barriers.
It may not be the department’s business to check the
companies’ rush, for divs. ; but it is its business to look
ahead and compel the co.’s to do things in safety which,
if neglected, will have to be done [at great risk of life
later on.
The Sulphide Corporation (N.S.W.) shares that
Australia deals in are almost exclusively ordinaries. Aus-
tralia leaves the pref. shares to J. Bull; and that shows
what sort of an ass Australia is. The Sulphide Corpora-
tion’s is one of the best mines on the Barrier ; if it doesn t
rank next after the B.H. Prop., it ranks third on the
field. And Australians, who ought to know better, buy
these Sulphide ordinaries at high prices on the strength
of these good reports ; and without stopping to see what
shares of this class are loaded with. First, there is
£79,600 of 5 per cent, debenture stock, redeemable in
1907 at 105. Then there is £550,000 of 10 per cent, prefer-
ence capital. Then there is the directors’ percentage
bite. All these must be satisfied first. On top of that
there is the fact that the management in England is
almost exclusively in the hands of the pref. shareholders
(Australia having the ordinaries) ; and, in order to keep
the 10 per cent. pref. dividend steady, huge lumps are
written off for depreciation whenever returns are extra-
good. Nothing has come the way of ordinaries, therefore,
since 1900, in spite of recent high metal prices. And now
there is to be another barrier for profits to climb over
before thev reach ordinaries. The subsidiary co. that is
to be formed (mostly in London) to handle the co.’s zinc
concentrates is to be guaranteed 5 per cent, during the
first 10 or 12 years. In other words, what amounts to a
new lot of 5 per cent, cumulative pref. shares are to be
created. Certainly the ordinary shareholders may them-
selves take up some of these shares, but the prefs. will
get the larger proportion. So the prospect of divs. for
ordinary Sulphides fades into the still dimmer distance-
in spite of the quality of the mine.
Prospects of Etheridge (Q.) railway are causing
a little stir. Einasleigh Co. is moving its debenture
holders to grant a strong Adelaide coterie an option,
under which, if it were exercised, close upon £40,000
would be spent on the property Unlikely the debenture
holders will refuse ; also it is unlikely that the money
will go up until the wherewithal to build the Etheridge
railway is in hand. * , »
Sydney papers have had a new crop of hifalutin
paragraphs about the “great Shale Co.” that has been
formed in England with an £BOO,OOO capital to develop
“ the greatest deposits of shale in the world at Capertee
or thereabouts, where, for 30years past, the N.S.W. Shale
and other co.’s have been struggling for a crust. The
panjandrums of the business, it is mentioned, have
arrived from London, and called on the Acting-Prenner ;
later on, of course, they were interviewed. After wan-
dering about airily amongst millions, the interview seems
to have taken a twist. A great deal, it was hinted,
depends upon labor conditions. “It may be necessary to
get concessions.” Watch the cat! If, in order to make this
thing pay, it is necessary to “get concessions” (which
apparently means cheap labor of some sort), why did the
co. pay this huge sum for the property—as much as
£250,000 has been mentioned? There are scores of shale
deposits in Australia that would show a profit on the
same terms ; but nobody sells them for a quarter of a
million, and turns them over to co.’s with £BOO,OOO capi-
tal. This “ may be necessary to get concessions ”is the
first hint of what is coming. The bloated concern, is
doomed to failure ; and when it does fail the bricks are
going to be thrown as usual, not at the promoters, but at
Australia and its laws.
By the way, in the published interviews about
this shale business it is again said that it is “ a semi-State
enterprise.”. In London, the ill-advised remarks of
Premier Carruthers, whooping about “ the introduction
of British capital,” have been scattered broadcast, pre-
sumably to convey the same impression. As, on the lines
on which the co. is worked, the thing must be a fiasco,
the sooner the N.S.W. Government makes it plain that
shale mining at Capertee isn’t a semi-State enterprise the
better. J. Bull is being drawn into just a plain private ;
gamble; and if the Mines Department isn’t game to ex-
press an opinion about the prospects of that gamble, it is
somebody’s'duty anyhow to see that the State doesn’t
have to stand any of the racket when the collapse happens.
* * * *
The Victorian Mines Minister threatens to
forfeit the Sebastopol Proprietary lease, Ballarat, and
there is a wail from the dress circle, while the gallery
chuckles. Analyse the situation, It is pleaded that the
Proprietary put up £16,000 for works before it handed
the claim over to the tribute co. This isn’t so, however,
as a good deal of that £16,000 is a mere book
entry on account of the mine, and means nothing.
Take the way the Central Plateau Co. was treated.
Royalty had to be paid on all gold that came up the
shaft ;'and Gilpin used to enforce this outrageous condi-
tion, even when gold was got from outside the tribute area.
Money was also made by charging a higher rent than
was paid bv the leaseholder to the State, and the royalty
was high. ‘ The tribute ought for every reason to be
quashed with as little mercy as one would show to
a toadstool. It serves no good purpose, and is best out
of the way. But to that add a word of caution. An
attempt may be made to boom Star of the East, Central
Plateau and Sebastapol Plateau No. 1, on the Minister’s
decision. At present there isn’t one of these mines on
stone that would pay for mining and hauling even if there
were no royalty.

Brvant, who has gone to manage the Charlotte
Plains Consolidated Mines, Carisbrook (Vic.), isn’t having
his first experience with the claim. He was in charge
years back, before J. Bull was brought on to the scene to
nurse the baby. Since then, Bryant has travelled. He
went to the M’Evov, at Eldorado, and he came thence to
the Main Leads North. This he made a success, beating
his detractors, and pulling the Harvey crowd out on the
right side of the ledger. Bryant’s one brother is a Balla-
rat broker, his other is at the Glenfine South ; so they are
all used to underground mining. Further, it will be a
great help to him to have known Drysdale Brown.
“ Dredger” : If you judged by what they give
you to read, you would suppose there is one bucket
dredge in Bright district of Gippsland (Vic.). The Tewks-
bury has certainly done well, having paid 60 per cent, in 10
months, but the Kia Ora, Phoenix, Junction, Persever-
ance Altona, and others have done almost as well for
several years. And the Enterprise at Bright, owned by a
syndicate of 10, broke all Australian records last year
(1905) Although only working 48 weeks (owing to an
accident which cost the syndicate £400), it paid 150 per
cent on the capital of £4OOO. The Enterprise had weekly
returns of 130ioz. and 1090 z., and many others from 80oz.
to 920 z. Enterprise shares are mostly held by Araluen
(N.S.W.) “grafters.”

“ Qiiidam ” : Re Lady Nelson Extended, St.
Arnaud, Vic. (8., 22/2/’O6), Manager H. R. Oroyden is a
sanguine soul ; at the same time the sincerity of his belief
that the gold will come in at a depth is proved by a large
portion of his income going back into the L. N. Ex. in
calls Further, Croyden’s belief is shared by most work-
ing miners of St. Arnaud. As to the management of co.
outside the manager, The Bulletin was not condemna-
tory enough. If the shares held in St. Arnaud could
speak the directors would go out on their heads instanter.
Unfortunately, their still small voice cannot make itself
heard till next August, by which time judiciously aimed
3d calls, and the withholding from sale of the forfeited
shares (already nearly 9000) will probably have shifted
the balance of power from St. Arnaud to some place
more in touch with the present directorate. St. Arnaud
probably possesses a few' shows besides the old Lord
Nelson which will come in again at a depth, and a
majority which won’t; but if the sanguine part of human
nature is to be entirely eliminated, mining in the locality
will soon be as defunct as anywhere else under similar
conditions. The two shows about which local opinion is
most sanguine are Lord Nelson North and Lady Nelson
Extended, and it will be a big lift to the field if either is
tested and not found wanting.

New Ravens wood (N.Q.) appears to have picked
up a rich shoot in the Grant, which is crushing for well
over 7 oz. to the ton. This makes the Grant and Sunset
Extended chances interesting, and returns from the
present crushing of 170 odd tons are anxiously awaited.
The parcel was sent to Charters Towers to be crushed.
Why not have sent it to Gympie, or, for that matter,
Bendigo? Economy be hanged !

Digbv Denham, Queensland’s new Minister for
Railways, states that the Richmond-Cloncurry line must
be carried right through to Cloncurry. Denham is enthu-
siastic ; also he is right, probably for reasons that he does
not see just yet. The Hampden-Mt. Elliott people pio-
pose to go right ahead with a north and south tramway,
for which the necessary leases are being obtained. Witn
this constructed, and the mines producing, and WillcocKs
people smelting, either the Government will have to
build to Cloncurry or private enterprise will connect per
tramway lease, and snap the traffic, for a tramway lease
confers all the privileges of common carriers upon the
holders. No wonder the Hampden-Mt. Elliott people,
with whom the name of Knox is associated, are not
frightened to go ahead. . . #
North White Feather, Kanowna (W.A.), is
approaching one of its periodical collapses. The ore
above the 500 ft. level is nearly depleted, and the 600 ft.
level is too poor to pay. While prospects were good,
scrip was boosted, but since the reef was cut in the shaft
at 575 ft., in August last, and proved to be only bin. wide
and worthless, a discreet silence has been observed. Un-
likely that the mine will be heard of much in future, as
its neighbors (White Feather Main Reefs and Reward)
both petered out at comparatively shallow depths, ihe
last two monthly returns have been : December, 1770 tons
for 10460 z., and profit £2100; January, 1570 tons foi
7810 z., and £3OO loss. No need to enlarge on that.
* * * *
Golden Ridge (W.A.) has had a rough time of
it lately, and holders have been getting rather scared of
the result. Recent developments have, however, been
encouraging. Trouble hitherto 'has been that develop-
ment has always been starved through the grab for divi-
dends as soon as ever a bit of profit was made.
* * * *
Amid all the disappointment and heart-burn-
ing over Tasmanian tin co.’s, one of them pays steady
divs., and mocks at those who think that puff and bounce
and share-mongering make a mine. This is the Pioneer,
managed by Cecil G. Ryan, brother of Plevna Rj an,
the Collins-street surgeon. Shares are hardly ever asked
for, and more rarely dealt in, save in lots of 5 or 10,
because they are well held by people who believe in
the mine and who hate Stock Exchange sharking.
Dividends to date aggregate £68,625, 9s. P er
share having been paid during the past half-year.
Since 1900, 1,565,600 cubic yards sluiced have averaged
2*2141b. of stream tin, at a cost of a shade over per
ton. Last half-year, however, 222,000 cubic yards went
only 1'8751b. per cubic yard, the mine cost being close up
to £42 10s. per ton. The poorer average arises
from the plants having had to tackle the upper
drifts, but next six months should see them
down into lower and better-grade dirt. As the
mine workings have extended costs have gone up,
and it seems improbable that they will come niuch below
present rates. Still the high price of tin more than com-
pensates for any increase in expense. Briseis Extended
plant is a duplicate of the No. 2 Pioneer ; and the fact
that the lift is about 80ft. indicates that, it the latter co.
wants to work the'ancient drifts about which so much
has been heard, it will require a second plant. With
only 5£ tons for over two months’work, there is not
much chance of that being got without calls.
* * * *
When manager Baudry went from the Briseis
Extended and Williamson came to it from the Briseis,
Langford and Turner were cocksure there was to be no
more trouble about yields. But Williamson seemed un-
able to escape the fatal influence of the claim, and soon
set to work sending off reports. Some of the wise birds
flew' on to another perch to look on out of harm s way, like
the crow when the gunner is about. When Williamson
talked about a record yield, the bull party joined hands,
and with a joint heave-ho, got shares out of the quag-
mire into which they had fallen. Now they are back
there again, and are, in fact, a good deal deeper in the
mud. The yield was, truly enough, a record yield, but it
was not the right sort of record. It was the lowest yet got
five tons against the previous 12. As sluicing has been
goino- on since December, it would be interesting
if the directors let shareholders behind the scenes
as to the financial standing of the show. Now the
has been lowered, and Northey s ancient drifts
are to be washed down. So far the ancients seem to have
had the best tin out of the mine—the old miners knew
more about tin than they got credit for. W hat with the
narrowing bottom, and the poor outputs, there won t be
much call for Briseis Extended shares in the future. The
best advice to give is to avoid the whole family—Weld-
brook, Mutual Hills, Sarah Ann, Mary Ann, and the rest
of them—until at least one has shown that it deserves
the name of a mine.’ #

An item of £425 was dropped out of the City of
Newcastle Gas Co.’s profits as reckoned up for a para-
graph in B. l/3/’O6. Previously the Insurance Fund had
been included as a reserve ; this time the doubtful item
(£425) was dropped without remark, thus reducing the
half-year’s net profit from £3544 to £3119.

A recent Customs prosecution let a little
glint of light on the deceitful ways of middlemen. It
appears that for some reason the importer often desires to
conceal his identity in entering goods at the Customs,
and iti obedience to instructions, a Customs agent made
an entry that certain goods were imported by one Smith.
On the fact being questioned, he grew quite enthusiastic
about Smith, and made a solemn declaration about
S nl ith. Whereupon a magistrate fined him £5 and £3
costs. Now, why does the importer want to disguise the
fact that certain goods were consigned to him ?
* ■ * * *
Melbourne suburban councils are looking at a
proposition to build and run ai) electric tramway from
Kew through Malvern, Hawthorn and St. Kilda to the
beach. They have obtained figures (partly supplied by
experts) purporting to show that a profit can be made
(after £2 17s. 6d. per cent, sinking fund, 4 per cent,
interest, and working expenses are paid) if revenue equal
to 10s. per head of the population can be obtained.
Melbourne, Sydney and Fremantle trams are all quoted
as yielding ’ over 18s. per head, but the comparison is of
little use as regards' the proposed line, which is purely
inter-suburban or “ railway feeding,” The biggest
tramway revenue always comes from a working
population moving to and from the city, so as
a business proposition this tramline looks doubtful.
But the most distressing part of the proposal
is the organised attempt to hand over the streets to
“private enterprise;” The last conference was evenly
divided between municipal construction and private ex-
ploiter. A proposal from Malvern (which seems to find
favor) is that the municipalities should guarantee U per
cent profit to a private company, with an agreement to
share with the company all profits over 6 per cent. So
the councils are to take all the risks, possess none of the
assets, and have in prospect only a minute share of the
profits.
Instructive freights (for same loading) quoted
-o the Tariff Commission :—Melbourne to Penang, 655. a
ton; London to Penang, 20s For certain boilers-
London to Fremantle, £96; Melbourne to Fremantle,
£2OO Yet the Freetrader tells Australia that it doesn’t
need a tariff, because its distance from the European
workshops and markets is huge Protection in itself.

“Two Seas”: “ Olim ” (B. 8/2/’O6) says
cats prefer starvation to rabbits. Which reminds
me that I was once told .by an observant new-
chum that horses wouldn’t eat corn, because he
saw a stable-fed horse leave corn to bite at green
grass. Have six cats of my own, and would
much prefer that they hunted mice or snakes, as
their habit of leaving the skins and paws of
rabbits all over the premises is a nuisance in fly-
time. Cat didn’t acquire her taste in this coun-
try either. On the Allanbank Estate, in Ber-
wickshire, we used to cut the tops off the ears
of all the house-cats to prevent them visiting
the rabbit-warrens in the early morning. Noth-
ing enrages cats more than to get water inside their
ears, and when going through the long dewy grass
they always depress their ears to keep the wet
out. Hence the reason for cutting off the tops of
the ears. The cats I’ve'seen couldn’t be trusted
even with tinned rabbit if there was a tin-opener
handy.
“ Ancle ” : Re “ Yia’s ” par (B. 28/12/’O5)
on a goanna. The late Prof. M‘Coy says in his
Zoology that the lace lizard, or go. (or any
other name that the loathsome coal-black reptile
with the sickly yellow stripes goes by) averages
sft., while he iias heard of some of 7ft. and Bft.
Five years ago, in the Whipstick district (north
of Bendigo), I saw abush man kill one (swung it
over his head by the tail) that measured 9ft. We
disregarded the inches and fractions—just laid it
along a panel of fence, and it stretched from post
to post. The local people merely remarked that
it was a big go., but nothing unusual, which
led me to believe the beast occasionally ran to the
size of a whale or thereabouts. Nobody seemed
to get hysterical or think he had been drinking
and saw an alligator or crocodile. The largest
ones seem to belong to the Murray districts. In
South Gippsland they seldom go over 4ft. 6in. ;
but they are an awful nuisance. Thev like sugar,
and go through the bags for it. and they delight
in milk and cream. Most of the people are dairy
farmers, and the South Gippsland go. trains
for sprinting. In wheat country the farmers wel-
come the go. because of his mice-eating pro-
pensities, but strictly warn him off the chicken
coop and nests.

Katanning is the source of W. A.’s only vegetable
supply.

Moocher : The Jap bitterly resents
being taken for a Chow, Once got a Jap cook.
John rolls up with his baskets, looks at Jap, and
bursts out, “My countleeman ! yoki! hoki! my
wud ! cheeawdollayikoyaugtzekiang. . . The
Jap turned green with rage and bashed John
hard on the mouth with a tomato out of his own
basket. Chow rushed out into the yard spitting
out tomato and bad language— “ You plully cow '
vah ! come on ! you Wantee fi’ ! I fi’ you ! ” But
he did a fine exhibition run when the Jap accepted
theinvitation. Again, a big railway porter said,
Hullo,, John ! ” to a chrysanthemum friend.
Ihe J ap s tactics were those of his brethren in the
war—he made war first and declared it after-
wards. He took a flying leap on to the porter’s
neck and kicked and bit and scratched worse than
a tiger-cat. When they pulled him off, the
porter was mostly blood and rags.

Recent reports of a number of cows dying
at Benalla (Vic.) through eating poisoned rabbits
raise further doubts as to the Danysz system of
rabbit-extermination by disease. The cows were
well-fed animal's, and would apparently have
attacked diseased rabbit corpses out of sheer
cussedness, just as readily as they did phosphor-
lsed bunny. It looks as if things will be only
middling for users of cow products (which means
everybody) if Danysz gets fairly going.
* * * *
“D.H.R.”: It, is surprising how easily
pie-melons grow, and to what a size. If, by any
accident, a solitary pie-melon seed gets left in
the middle of a scalded plain, and there
isn’t any sign of rain, next year will
reveal a vine extending some ten or twenty
yards on each side, and containing dozens of
big melons. Travelling once on the Maranoa
River, I suddenly came upon a pie-melon
patch of some hundred acres or so in extent,
beveral of the melons must have weighed
considerably over a hundredweight each.
Another time, on the Burdekin (Q.), I was
riding along with a nigger, wondering 011
which side lay civilisation and flour, when
suddenly I was surprised by seeing green
stretching out in front of “me as far as
the eye could reach. On approaching, we
found it to be a large area of pie-melon
vines, thick with melons. We had been
lost for five days, and during that time had
only discovered one iguana; so the pie-
melons were received with open mouths.
As this part of the country was then very
little frequented by whites, the origin of the
plot is a mystery. “But most likely a bird
carried a single seed, which had grown and
multiplied. Pie-melons are a God-send to
many folks at the back of the last storm
and between the last two bush-fires,
being the only thing in the shape of
fruit they ever see; and when made
into jam they are a welcome change from
“butter,” i.e., bullock-fat.
“ Bug ” : Be vine-caterpillars (“ Orthos,’'
B. l/2/’OG). The insect referred to is probably
sEchalia schellembergi , a well-known enemy of
the vine-moth-grub, and of the larvse of the fig-
leaf beetle. The insect is described in “Nates
on the Hemipteria,” N.S.W. Agricultural
Gazette, March, 1902. It would be difficult to
propagate it, though, where the eggs are plentiful
they might be collected.

Some of the west country of Queensland
is so flooded that a resident of Warbreccan, 100
miles from Longreach, had to ride 230 miles to
get to that town. Provisions for the far West are
being carried many miles in boats, constructed
principally of galvanised iron. And yet, only a
few weeks ago, the residents had almost forgotten
what rain was like ; while children several years
old had never seen anything more in that line
than an occasional light shower.

ON GARDINER’S GREEK.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
,' SP °i fc w s? re Gardiner’s Creek junctions with the
ilmh r 8 & ° ld house of Gardiner, the first over-
lander from -Sydney to settle on that side of the river,
r Slte of the first cattle station in Victoria.
iner s run extended over a large area of red-gum
country, and included the district of Boroondara S
A mound of earth half-buried in the grass,
Where piscatorial youths and sweethearts pass ;
There’s nothing else, though one might search a week,
Of old John Gardiner’s homestead by the creek.
On every side suburban villas stand ;
There’s been a steady rise per foot in land,
And speculators with unblushing cheek
Long since cut up the homestead by the creek.
Twas heieabout the long-horned cattle strayed,
Or chewed the cud beneath the peaceful shade.
’Twas here black joeys played at hide and seek,
And queer old lubras dwelt beside the creek.
Beneath his broad verandah, at his ease,
Old John looked out among the red-gum trees ;
He -watched the drovers herding cattle sleek,
And saw his goods increase along the creek.
He saw the rich flats burgeon to the plough ;
He felt Arcadian peace descend—but now
We have our Queen Anne villas, and the reek
Of passing- motor-cars by Gardiner’s Creek.
I sometimes wonder as I wander past
If John thought his Arcadia could last—
While summers burned and winters whistled bleak
He prospered kindly by the shady creek.
They say old John’s bright sky at length grew dark—
He shifted camp and went to Mooroolbark ;
He found the old place crowded, so to speak
Three other squatters came to Gardiner’s Creek.

“Gr-D.M.” tells his troubles through
“ going on the land ” :
I caught the microbe of going on the land through
daily reading the probates of wills of dead farmers, none
ever under £3OOO. Got a lease of 30 acres while under
crop. When the hay was shifted a splendid self-sown
crop of poverty weed became visible. This won't want
any cultivation, and I hope the market price of weeds
keeps up next spring, as my hired assistant says they’ll go
3 tons to the acre. Bought a pony with a weak set of
bowels, 20 years old by the rings on the two teeth left.
Gave him a bundle of green maize ; he got at the water
himself. He was as big as a Clydesdale when he died that
night, and the stable boy said his temperature was 200°.
Rabbits ate my turnip tops, and I set three traps. The
first night my neighbor’s dog walked away with a trap,
singing like Marshall Hall’s lyric orchestra attacking the
Yalkyrie. Next night we lost our best hen. Then suc-
cessively we caught a prize Australian terrier, a brace of
chickens, a kitten rabbit and two ducks. Never thought
trapping was so easy to learn. A fox took our turkey
chicks one pale blue moonlight night, and then our cow
died through eating half an Australasian. We cart
water three miles, but only have half a mile to go for
beer, which is some compensation. I’m beginning to
think better 50 years in an East Melbourne boarding-
house than a cycle “ on the land.”

“F.”: “ A.S.” (B. 12/2/’O6) is in trouble
about keeping his pet kookaburra fed. If, as I
gather from his screed, “A.S.” is a respectable
family man, with a stake—in the shape of a
garden—in the country, the solution of the pro-
blem is easy. Let “ A.S.” put in an hour, morn-
ing and evening, digging his garden properly.
Let him take the kookaburra to the scene of
operations, and, show him the resultant worms,
grubs, beetles, &c. He won’t have to show
them more than twice. Jacky will be on time
every night and morning, and will probably end
by waking “ A.S.” up if he sleeps-in any morning.
I kept a Jack for a long time by this process, and
the bird got so eager that I several times nearly
shaved his head off with the spade.
• * •
“Melb.”: Having at one time been two
years buried in the Bush, I have an eye for the
Bush bride, and see her here nearly daily, with
her rig-out of the Noah’s Ark store. To-day I
met the Bush mother in this Babylon. Quite a
girl, tugging four children. This mother stepped
right out of Lawson’s pages, in “Joe Wilson and
his Mates.” She was tall, thin, angular, looking
like a Bush paling, or the wild Yankee girl’ im-
personated by Rosa Gore, of Crirntmns and Gore,
at the Opera House. Her hair seemed to be com-
posed of several birds’ nests. Her voice, when
she called incessantly, “Come along, Maggie,” or
“Where’s Susy?” was like the silk lash of a
bullock-driver’s whip on Poley’s hide.

“F.C.”: Can anybody say which is the
most “ overpubbed ” township in Australia? I
don’t pretend to know which holds the record,
, . . . , • ,tt- , ,
but, just for a stait Castlemame (Vic.) has a
population of 5703 and boasts 49 hotels, the statu-
tory number being 11. Lancefield and Romsey
(Vic.) between them carry some 1019 souls, who
periodically drain the beer from 16 hotels. Probably
the droughty West can show better figures.
“X. 0.”: Re the cremated ant (“Krex,” B.
28/12/’O5). At a Darling station the bread-
oven had collapsed, and as the composition of
ant-bed is of a glutinous nature, the boss thought
the stuff might serve as mortar in repairing the
oven, so Pat and I were despatched with a
horse and dray to annex a portion of the per-
forated mound the ants call home. We made a
large heap of wood and leaves on top of the nest
and set it alight, whereupon half-a-dozen warriors
rushed out on the roof to find out where the sun
had fallen. They soon retreated again to the
cellar. When all was reduced to ashes, we started
disturbing the ant architecture, and then up
poured the black army, seeking fresh air and
vengeance. We had got a few shovelsful on the
dray, and I was endeavoring to arrest a battalion
that had trespassed upon my person, when the
horse suddenly started off like mad. The ants
had crawled along the shaft, and got to work
upon the moke’s carcase. We raced two miles
round that paddock to stop the astonished equine.
The ants drove him over logs, across stumps, and
into tlie dam. The temperature was 108 in the
shadowed parts of nature when we got the animal
out, minus the wheelwright’s handiwork ; that
had been mislaid somewhere in the scrub. The
quadruped gazed sadly at us with his bleared
optic, and wondered mournfully what would be
the next item on the bill of fare.

Thus “ Lionel Kelly ” :
The reason why the big sugar-grower in Queensland
prefers the kanaka to the white —apart from the question
of wages—is precisely the same reason that makes the
rice-grower in India prefer the servile plains man to the
bold, free, colored circumstance from the hills. He can
boss him and give him a flogging occasionally if he is
annoyed with someone else whom he isn’t game to flog.
Not long ago I spent four months on a big cane-grower’s
place as overseer. One day the boss, who always poked
about in the field, had a row with a neighbor, and got the
worst of it. Returning he marched into the field, boiling
with rage, and walloped the most adjacent nigger with a
thick green-hide belt. Several times during my four
months’ stay I witnessed similar instances. Once when a
native’s back was allowed to remain unattended to till
flies and heat caused it to become dangerous, he had to
be admitted to the hospital, and the boss was afraid of an
inquiry, not being on good terms with the police. So he
gave Tommy a bottle of whisky and half a pound of to-
bacco and said, “ You fall it on wire-barb fence, Tommy.”
“ Ya-as, boss,” said Tommy.
Which reminds a Bulletin man of the frank con-
fession of ati Orient steamship captain who used
to make the smoking room a misery by taking the
floor at all hours of the day and night, and holding
forth about the iniquity of the Labor party in
opposing the employment of black labor. It con-
cerned a crew of nigger stokers and a scarcity
of steam. The engineer briefly informed the
“serang” or boss nigger of the difficulty. The
boss nigger said brightly, “What? You wantee
steam ? ” Then he fled down below with a stick,
and presently there were howls of anguish from
the stoke-hole—and there was also more steam.
The fact that the white stoker can’t be walloped
like that accounts for a good deal of the prejudice
against him.
* * * *
One of the coming curses of coastal Queens-
land, the pretty water hyacinth, has got such a
hold in the Brisbane River that the small steamers
can only get to Ipswich with great difficulty. The
local authorities are hoping for a flood to do the
work they have systematically neglected.

“ Timbarra ” : Re bush telephones. I was
on a station in N.S.W. where they had telephones
to "J 1 the " a^ s ’.
used to ring up the man in charge of each out-
station, make a through connection on all lines,
then fix the switchboard transmitter near the
mouth of a gramophone and reel off the latest
records. They transmitted very clearly and dis-
tinctly, and the “concerts’’ were greatly appreci-
ated by the poor lonely devils.

“ W.S.G.” : A Chow used to be a rarity
in Mildura; now he’s an important personage
there. New owner of block was making channels
between his trees for the expected watering,
when a Chinaman got off a bike on the road and
called: “Hi, you —where Smith?” “Smith!
Oh, he’s not here now, John.” “You wulkin’
tis block, t’en ? ” “Yes.” “ Well”—preparing
to mount—“you can take the water on Thurs.”
“ Take the water on Thurs. ! What the blazes
has the water to do with you?” “Oh! I’m the
water-ganger,” replied the Celestial, smiling.
“You be blowed ! Green’s ganger on this sec-
tion.” “ Him ! No feah ! He only put on when
I too busy.”

“ Moodier ” : Re starting bush fires.
“ F.C.’s ” explanation (B. 15/2/’O6) only accounts
for a small part of the trouble. In the Riverina
and Western country stations pay 255. per 24
hours to fire-beaters, and in one case, on the
’Bidgee, this year, 265. was paid. That's where
fires start. Incendiarism is a fine art among the
dead-brokes. A few of the methods : Fires were
started lately round Narandera by throwing about
bits of dough containing phosphorus. Another
trick is to make a hole 2in. or 3in. deep in the
ground where grass is heavy ; floor with loose
grass, and set firmly on this a piece of lighted
candle, which, when it burns down, fires the grass,
the contriver being by that time miles away,
having a beer and creating an alibi for emergen-
cies. Another dodge is to cut the bottom off a
bottle and stand it over a bit of candle on the
grass ; as before, candle burns down and does the
trick. But the most ingenious plan is to place a
tin full of water, which leaks slowly through a
hole in the bottom ; in the water is placed a bit of
phosphorus ; and loose grass is strewn around and
over the tin. When the water has leaked away the
phosphorus is left dry—and there you are ! Then,
when the fire is going strong, Bill arrives in a
hurry, and, after 24 hours’ heroic loafing, draws
the price of a decent drunk.

The English game-preserving landlord,
who tramps with a gun in his hand over the
crops of his tenant, farmer Hodge, has arrived in
Australia. Two squatters who own an estate at
Terang (Vic.) have sent out a circular in these
terms to the grovelling agricultural worm who is
permitted to scrape a livelihood off their acres at
a high rent: —
Mr. Tenant. Dear Sir, —As the shooting season for
quail opens on Ist March, I shall be obliged if you will
strictly preserve the shooting on your farm for myself and
friends.
A good part of the early population of Australia
was sent out for taking Squire Bloggs’ hare on a
moonless night. Now Squire Bloggs has pursued
the expatriated population, and is making a row
about his blessed quail.

An ostrich feather expert told the Tariff
Commission that the Australian-bred ostrich ran
to more stalk and less plume than his S’African
progenitor—he developed too much quill. Herein
the fowl seems to resemble the Australian gums—
a good, solid stem, and precious little foliage.
“ Drifter ” : Re “ Spring’s ” par (B.
8/2/’O6) telling of a goanna that climbed a youth
in a treeless paddock, when chased by his dog,
and his horrible experience in trying to fight the
reptile off. “Spring’s” youth hadn t a grain of
resource. Once I met with an exactly similar
experience. Was crossing an arid area when my
fox-terrier fiercely attacked a big go. As I
was the only perpendicular object handy, the
startled reptile raced up on to my shoulder and
commenced poking borak at the leaping terrier s
impotent attempts to seize him. Did I strive to
pull that go. off by the tail, and get my soft,
white flesh scarified by his claws ? No ! With
commendable presence of mind, I threw myself
upon the ground. Thinking the tree had fallen,
the chagrined go. made tracks, only to be assassi-
nated by the terrier before he had galloped 10ft.
from my form. I mention this simple dodge so
that any bushman climbed by the sinister-looking
go. will immediately know how to act. Lie down
and roll. The same treatment is efficacious if
your whiskers become ignited.

SHEEP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of wild deeds done in crowded yards, where high the
red dust flew,
You know, who worked the west with me from Mae’s to
Timbaroo.
We’ve sniffed, across the southern breeze, the sheep-
smell, strong and stale.
And breathed the scent of blanky skins that stained the
post-an’-rail
Around the woolshed paddock where the waters fell and
died,
We’ve waded in the trampled tank to rob a rotting hide.
Or sweated in the water-holes among the hollow stock —
A hundred weaklings done to death beneath the straining
flock—
And, though we cursed them in the bog, and cursed
their dying cries,
We wept with rage to see the crows pick out their plain-
tive eyes.
We’ve soused the swimming wethers as they passed the
crutching-plank ;
We’ve sorted out the blooded rams by horn and greasy
flank ;
And, napping at the gates, have crashed among the
purple stars
That fall when plunging “studs” are fain to break their
drafting-bars.
So, all day. long, among our pals inside the swarming
yard,
Or out across the droughty run, knob-kneed and saddle-
hard,
We toiled—with no fat cheque in view, no cattleman’s
renown,
Just dust and sweat and blood and grease until the sun
went down.
From Miser Mac’s to Timbaroo, from Gothog to The Blow,
We’ve worked the long, slow, scattered line from dawn
to dark —and know !

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Mr. F. W, Haddon, consulting editor
Melbourne Argus, is reported seriously ill. Mr.
Haddon, who was born at Croydon (Eng.), com-
pleted his 77th year on the Bth ult. He became
editor of the Australasian in 18C5, and of the
Argus in 1867.
At Brighton (Vic.), the other night, T.
Bent, Premier, had been gassing about himself
and his magnificent performances in private and
public for a solid hour. At length he had finished,
and was backing towards his chair, when a mourn-
ful voice arose : “And now, Mr. Bent, please do
tell us something about yourself 1”
Vic. Chief Justice Madden, before he left
for Europe, acting as Chancellor for Melbourne
University, conferred the degree of bachelor of
laws on his only son, Guy. The son has been
articled to his uncle, the Speaker, and, when
“ father comes home,” he will duly instal Guy as
a member of the Devil’s Brigade.
Delohery, of N.S.W. Public Service Board,
flits to Europe on six months holiday. Of the three
Commissioners, Delohery is the one who obtrudes
himself least, and he is generally called the
“Chorus” in the Board’s inquiry work. When
he gets as far as “Do you really think so, Mr.
Barling?” those present wonder why “old
Delohery is so argumentative this morning.”
An old, long-forgotten Sydney identity
died near Southampton (Eng.) lately at the age of
85. Mr. Francis Webb Wentworth Shields, C.E.,
came to Sydney in 1843 and practised his profes-
sion at 174 Elizabeth-street, near where Went-
worth Court now stands. He was shortly after-
wards appointed City Surveyor, and has the
credit of, having designed the first railway
built in New South Wales. He returned to Eng-
land in 1851 and became resident engineer at the
Crystal Palace.
DOUGLAS GERRARD, actor, the hero of a
hundred ladies’ letters.
Adelaide’s oldest doctor is Surgeon-Major
Hinton, who served in India at the time of the
Mutiny, and now draws a military pension.
Although 93 years of age, he travels about the
country without any attendance, taking long
coach journeys by himself. He is father-in-law
of Under-Secretary Sholl. Recently Dr. Gun-
ning, aged 88, visited the old veteran, and as he
left, Dr. Hinton put his hand on his colleague’s
shoulder and said, “ Now, take care of yourself as
you cross the roads. Remember you are growing
old. Shall I come and see you safely to the tram ?”
Parson-politician-pressman Harold Rylett,
visiting Australasia in the interests of a new British
Liberal newspaper, was treated to some disrespect-
ful and much-needed language in Maoriland.
Having just finished an agreeable interview with
Premier Seddon, the British person was feeling
quite as large as the big man himself when some-
one introduced John Rigg, the Labor M.L.C.

to foster your agricul-

never be a manufac-

Henry
Parkes, whose
immigration
card was re-
cently facsimiled
in The Bulletin,
lectured at Bir-
mingham on the ad-
vantages of Austra-
lia, and ome got a
very bad reception.
“Don’t believe him,
mates,” cried a hulking
descendant of toil; “Vs
gammonin’ us. ’E’s paid a thousand a year
to draw us to Horstralia.” “It’s a lie,”
replied Parkes. Great uproar followed, the lie

An old identity “along the wharves”
joined the great caravan “on the other side’’
last week when William Short, of Ashfield (Sydney),
died after having seen 91 winters. He came to
Sydney in 1838 from Cornwall, and worked his
way up to be a large ship-owner and wharf-
master. His wife died three years ago at the age
of 91. He kept on until he overtook her years,
and now he’s gone to join her.
All the Australian battlers are
not out on the tracks of the Never-
Never or on the Stock Exchange. One
of them, a young Australian, is manager
of a farm labor-home, which has
branched out from the Blind and Deaf
and Dumb Asylum of Adelaide. A
while before he died, J. H. Angas,
finding an unworked farm of about 200
acres, 15 miles from Adelaide, on his
hands, gave it to the asylum trustees;
and the latter, chronically hard-up as
most such bodies are, didn’t well know
what to do with it. But one of the
teachers, who years ago had come off
a farm to go into the asylum as a pupil
teacher, undertook to wrestle with /the
new gift; so he walked out driving an
old cow and a pig. Now, though that
is less than half-a-dozen years ago, the
place, which is used as a home for
maimed and halt blind, or deaf and
dumb, is rally self-supporting. Prac
tically there isn’t an inmate who hasn’t
some other misfortune on top of his
blindness or his muteness; and yet,-
on the farm, this young Australian
manager Cox finds something for every
one of them to do.
W. E. Slade, Assistant-Engineer
of Harbors,lat Adelaide, who died re-
cently at Fremantle (W.A.), aged 49,
did one very useful piece of work in his
latter days. He was sent to England
to purchase machinery for making large-
sized cast-iron pipes at the Glanville
(Adelaide) workshops, of which he was
superintendent. He reported to the
Premier that he had decided to buy less
than half of the plant in England, be-
cause what he had seen there convinced
him that S. Australia could manu-
facture the rest itself. Slade had no
Stinking Fish tendencies about him.
J. Pumpsaint Jones is the name of the
pastor of Westralian Welsh Free Church. Pump !
Saint ! ! and Jones !! !
A train on which Tommy Bent was travel-
ling broke down on the Gippsland line the other
day, and gave Thomas a bad shock to the system
and a bumped elbow. “The condition of the
road is blamed for the sensational occurrence,”
but it must not be forgotten that everything on
which Tommy travels breaks down sooner or later
—cabs, steamers, motor-cars, and now a train. It
is reasonable to assume that it is the country’s turn
next.

AMELIA : “ True for yer, Surer, us women ’art
a ’ard life.”
Alan Major Millard’s appeal to the Full
Court on points of law having been disallowed,
his name has been removed from the roll of N.S.W.
solicitors. Millard still retains his seat in the
N.S.W. Assembly, and intends, it is stated, to
hold it until his appeal to the High Court is
determined.
Among those left out of the new British
House of Commons is Baronet James Ferguson,
who was Governor of S. Australia from ’69 to ’73,
and of Maori land from ’73 to ’74. His (second)
wife is a South Australian. Ferguson was
first elected to the House of Commons for Ayr-
shire in 1854, when he was with his regiment in
the Crimea. He is now 74 years of age, and was
within two of being father of the House when the
deluge struck him.
For this week Victoria’s Bent can't re-
member any new thing to say about himself
except that he used to be a j achtsman and a
mariner. Once he owned the Fleetwing, with
which he used to run stone from Spottiswood to
Brighton. But someone cut the hawser one day,
and the blessed vessel drifted into the Sandridge
baths, and it cost £5OO to get it out. After that
episode the Fleetwing and Thomas parted, and
Thomas went cabbage-growing. , Cabbages don’t
frisk as ships do.
“The Mussel”; Detective O’Donnell is
the best pistol shot in the Melbourne police force.
When his sofa was perforated with bits of bomb,
he told this to the Argus
Yes, I reckon I can smash a jam-tin every time out of
ten or upwards—that is to say, a real jam-tin—not a cor-
rugated iron tank.
To the Age he said :
Yes, it’s 33yds. across this street. Now, you put up
half-a-crown on the fence, and I’ll fetch it nine times out
of ten.
Then the NhillFree Press rose to the occasion
Detective O’Donnell is the crack revolver shot of the
detective force. His aim is absolutely deadly, for he can
be relied upon to hit a sixpence at 100yds.
“ W.” writes
A. W. Howjtt, who explored some, and made a book
about blackfeilows and other Australian peculiarities, has
pitched his last camp on the slopes of Lake King (Gipps.
land Lakes, Victoria), and may be seen most days dissect-
ing native timbers at a big wood pile and moving surplus
geological and botanical specimens from the front garden
with a bunch of ti-tree. Howitt, who is a doctor of
something or other, draws a pension like a P.M.’s salary
all the time, and draws water at intervals. He is tough,
even for an aged pensioner, and when the steamer brings
his box of groceries along, wheels the garden barrow
a quarter-mile down a steep hill to the jetty and trundles
the whole outfit up the hill again to his diggings. For a
man who buried Bourke and Wills 45 years ago this is
pretty good work.
The busiest little lady in Adelaide is Mrs.
Ennis, secretary of the Society for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Animals. Her husband is Professor
of Music at the University, but she spends her
time in looking after horses with sore shoulders,
starving dogs, misused cows, and other trpdden-on
quadrupeds. Last week she was up at 5 one
morning and stopped about all day to succor cer-
tain sheep which had been neglected in the park
lands and some of which had broken limbs. On
the hottest summer days she is about the streets
to see that horses and other animals aEe properly
watered and looked "after. Human beings often
think her a nuisance, but dumb creatures recog-
nise her as a benefactor.
Robert Gemmell, teller at the Bank of
Australasia, Creswick (Vic.), lately fell in over
about £lBO shortage in his cash. He had been 22
( years in the service of the bank, and had risen
gradually to the dazzling salary of £145 a year
£2 15s. 9d. per week. And the Bank of Austral*
' asia, that stupendous British financial sweater’
makes over £lOO,OOO each half-year without turn-
ing a hair. But it turns its employees’ hair-
grey.
The widow of Hugh Lewis Taylor, for
some years manager of the London office of the
Bank of Victoria, died iri 1004 intestate, leaving
an estate valued at £51,084. As no relatives can
be discovered, the estate reverts to the Crown
the good old absorbent Crown, which takes in so
many things and disgorges so few. The Crown is
like a quicksand—it gives a heave and draws a
deep breath, and unclaimed estates go down—
down—-down.
Frederick Hermann Gray, late teller of
the Bank of Australasia, Bendigo, was, in the end,
convicted last week on two charges of larceny,
and four charges of falsifying the books, and
sentenced to 32 months “hard.” Gray has been
tried till he is the most tried man in Australia;
but the Bendigo juries always seemed to include
one of his personal friends, and no agreement
could be reached. The final trial was in
Melbourne, and the jury ran in verdicts of guilty
at an astonishing speed. This is the first defeat
barrister Maxwell, the man who looks like the
descendant of a Jew who was wrecked on' the
Scotch coast in the Middle Ages, has had for a
long time.
Another .claimant has set up for the “first
white womanship ” of Melbourne. Mrs. A’Beckett,
relict of W. A. C. A’Beckett, M.L.G (Vic.), who
died last week, was born in Melbourne-in
her father, John Mills, being one of the buyers at
the first land sale in the. previous year. His pur-
chase was a half-acre in Elinders-street,-between
Elizabeth and Queen streets, for which, he paid
Auctioneer-Surveyor Hoddle £35. In 1888 the
half-acre was valued at £llB,BOO. The buyer im-
mediately behind him, Flinders-lane separating
the lots, was Michael Pender, who gave’ £l9 for
the half-acre on which stands (or stood) the
Criterion Hotel, valued in ’BB at £198,000. This
area is nowin the possession of the Union Bank,
which paid, in ’77, £33,000 for that portion on
which the hotel stood. Mr. W. A. C. A’Beckett
arrived with bis father (afterwards Chief Justice
of Victoria) in Sydney in 1838, and migrated with
his people, when his father was appointed Resi-
dent Judge at Port Phillip in 1846,
A rumor is current in Melbourne that
Thomas Bent will probably be made a knight
next time Edward VII'. is scattering round a bag-
ful of Imperial “honors.” The Bulletin gives
the idea its moral support and trusts that the
rumor is true. It is difficult to imagine anyone
who would do more, in a bear-like and humorous
way, to reduce knighthood to its proper level as a
complicated joke than Bent. He is calculated to
make other knights realise where they have, got
to, and if knighthood requires another shove on
the path to its natural home in the monkey
department of the Zoo, there is no one better
qualified to give it. that shove. The B.ulletin
has for years ignored Australian titles*; but it
would feel inclined to make an exception in
Bent’s case. Thomas the Bulgy, as the man
whom the King delighted to honor, would be a
thing to be kept before the public. He would
tend to undermine the monarchy with two per-
petual conundrums : (1) What kind of king can
it be who admires Bent ? (2) What kind of Bent
can it be who admires a king who could admire
Bent?

Edward MacFarlane, N.S.W. Undersecre-
tary for Lands, begins to loom up before the
Lands Commission in a way that leaves him not
far behind Willis, Peter Close and Crick as an ob-
ject of public interest. Of late he has been so
much in the witness-box that he might almost as
well'bring down his bedding and sleep there, and
his explanations have been numerous and interest-
ing. Even when he declared that he had had a
certain Gazette in his hand before midnight on a
certain date—it was essential, if a lot of things
were to be legal, that that Gazette must be pub-
lished before midnight—and an official from the
Government Printing Office declared he couldn’t
have had it, for the paper wasn’t ready till about
2 a.m., two hours after the legal hour—even then
Mr. MacFarlane was able to explain.
JOHN BULL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“A Chinaman named John Bull was charged”
Xews Item.
John Bull was once a yeoman
Who stoutly toolr his stand,
A harrow and a hoe-man,
In merry Engle-land.
John Bull was once a white man
Beside his salt North Sea ;
If not a very bright man,
Yet great and strong was he.
Now Bull’s became a fellow
Of every alien clan ;
His blood runs brown and yellow
Cross-striped with black and tan —
A harlequin and trickster,
A clown in cap and bells,
A mad and motley mixture
Of undesirables.
Bull cuts a curious figure
With lineage askew,
White man and brown and nigger,
Jap, Kaffir, German Jew ;
Pig-tail and yellow label
Now crown his chequered brow,
This last recruit for Babel—
John Bull, colonial Chow.
VIC.
L.E.
