A harmless-looking individual walked into
a Queensland barber’s shop t’other day, after the
early-closing hour, and asked for the loan of a
razor to shave himself. He obtained the imple-
ment, and then proceeded tocut histhroat. The bar-
ber interfered. “ Look here, ”he said, “ you don’t
do that with it for less’n a bob. It’s worth a bob
to cut any man’s throat.” But there was no
business.
“P.D.” turns a sidelight on to an old
subject:—
The deuce of a fuss made by Baptist parson Sharp, of
Goulburn (N.S.W), over the removal of his teacher-wife
to Arihidale, alleging' sectarian (Anglican) influence, has
ended in smoke. But it has given Director of Education
Peter Board a chance of dealing with the married-woman
teacher; and the inevitable consequence of a strong
mail’s wrestling with that matter would be the shunting
of the wives of Parson Sharp and others,' I have known
women earning £lBO per annum, and thereabouts to marry
men with salaries of £3OO. Result: Single women unable
to secure promotion, and (this is what I want to say in
particular) no increase in the birthrate. In some case§,
children have arrived, but on account of the admittedly
severe physical strain of teaching, the short leave of
absence granted at such times, and the almost unavoid-
able neglect of the offspring, the new arrival has been a
very temporary young Australian. I know a mistress of
infants who married, and continued teaching. She has
had four children. Three died at under 12 months, the
other at tvi»> years. To deal with future possibilities, a
regulation could be framed making, marriage per se an
act of resignation on the part of a woman teacher.
On theotherhand, consider the case of Ermyntrude,
who would be quite willing to do her duty to her
country of great empty spaces if only she were
allowed to hang on to her job, and so eke out with
her own screw Edwin’s wage of 30s. a week ? Cer-
tainly, the real reform is to make the country so
prosperous (with-J-rotection, etc.) as to allow
Edwin to get at least 50s. olf his own bat.
German Prof. Klaatsch, who lately rum-
maged amongst the Queensland blacks for the
missing link, is now in Java, looking for further
traces of the man-monkey. He is also comparing
the Java mountain dog with the Australian
dingo.
Mills “Political Economy,” book V. chap,
iv.:—
A country cannot be expected to renounce the power
of taxing foreigners unless foreigners will in return prac-
tice towards it the same forebearance. The only mode in
w'hich a country can save itself from being a loser by the
revenue duties imposed by other countries on its com-
modities, is to impose corresponding revenue duties on
theirs.
This should be read slowly and very distinctly to
persons like Bruce Smith, who, when they en-
counter a Protectionist, glare at him and ask sternly
—‘‘You must be grossly ignorant of political
economy. Why don’t you read Mill?”
“ Itan ” : A feature of the artesian bores
in Western Queensland is that, around, the marshes
which the surplus water creates, huge reedy growths
spring up, 10ft. or 12ft.. high. There is nothing
to show where the seeds come from, for within
white men’s memory no such weeds grow natur-
ally in the west, even in the wettest season.
Where irrigation is applied the blue Mitchell
grhss disappears. It is the great, stand-by of
those vast stretches of country, but will not
flourish if the season is too moist. It wants about
12 inches of rain in the year, split into three
parts. When mature, this grass is spoiled by an
inch of rain, for it then rots off and blackens.
The common coastal grasses have been found to
be useless as ensilage, .for they crumble away
under the most scientific packing, but the blue
Mitchell opens" out of the stack or pit as sweet as
lucerne.
Not long ago some of the heads of the
Anglican Church in Australia solemnly rebuked
Australians for wanting to exclude the colored
brother. They get no sympathy from the
Guardian, their own press organ in England. It
writes: —
The danger that Asiatic immigration would create a
racial penumbra—such as the half-caste population which
exists in Natal, and is a source of endless difficulties to
the educational authorities —and injure the morale of the
dominant race, is another argument on which men of all
Australian parties lay emphasis. . . . That Australia will
continue to exclude all non-European elements is an
absolute certainty. We have no doubt whatever she is
following the course of wisdom and justice in refusing to
allow the immigration of racial types which cannot be
absorbed and assimilated by a white people born into
Occidental civilisation.
Education Minister Sachse, of Victoria, is
hatching out the egg of an idea for. a system of
free, circular and movable education. He pur-
poses reaching those youngsters in the back-blocks
who are too far removed from State schools by
sending teachers round to them in vans. The
perambulating school is to be about the size of a
furniture van, and is to carry a teacher, a small
boy assistant, and all necessary means and appli-
ances. This vehicle is expected to bring enlighten-
ment to the back doors of settlers in the Dim
Distance, giving small doses of the Three R.’s to
one family, then whipping up the neddy and
passing on to the next. Difficulties by flood and
fire and on bush roads that would bog a mosquito
and engulf an elephant, Minister Sachse expects
to overcome one way or another. But he hasn’t
decided yet what to do with the bad boy who’re-
quires to be kept in. The school has to move on,
and can’t wait to keep bad boys in. Perhaps the
defaulting scholar will be tied to the tail of the
trap and dragged behind till his sin is expiated
by bitter concussion with the hard road.
In re-naming Vic. railway stations Com-
missioner Tait appears to have studied brevity
without any regard to shock. The little back-
yard stopping-place known colloquially as Sydney
Flat—a few steam-whiffs from Eaglehawk—he
has gazetted to be henceforth known as - ‘ Sydney.”
In agricultural implements, S. Australia,
which uses a lot, seems determined to be its own
customer. Inter-State imports fell in 1905 from
£32,000 to £5OOO, and over-sea buying from
£lOO,OOO to £48,000. S.A. has had a record har- .
vest, and all local implement factories are
now working overtime. Martins, of Crawler,
have 500 men going (as against 300 a year ago),
and are making a lot of machinery for Broken
Hill and Westralia, and even a little for Queens-
land and Tasmanian mines.' • >

All women have an instinct for dress, but if you
have artistic instincts you will pay attention to
the details of dressing. It is the little things
that count, and Kleinert’s Featherweight Dress
Shield counts for more than its weight in gold,

It is a significant fact that S.A. Audit
Office has never yet gained a conviction in any
Crown prosecution in which it has been engaged.
No doubt this is partly due to the fact that the
counsel for the defence is generally Josiah Symon
—a legal terror who rarely loses a case. Symon
has something of an uncanny reputation in this
respect. After a particularly flagrant case, in
which the jury returned a verdict of “Not guilty,”
someone asked a juryman how he managed to
arrive at the amazing conclusion. He said : “Do
you think when a clever chap like Symon says
he’s innocent, I’m going to say he’s guilty ? Not
much.”

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Mr Dear Arini, —
Sydney, March 6, 1906.
The Girl who Doesn’t Mind the Weather joined
ime, Clammy One, in a tea-room as pre-arranged.
She came in with three women whom I did not
know, talking as usual, and left them in the
middle of a sentence. “They,” she explained,
'“are great friends of mine. I call them the
Three Declensions, because they droop and wane
as the summer waxes. I found them just now in
George-street, looking in at a shop window, and
they were so moist, not to say wet and exhausted,
that I offered to give them first aid to the
apparently drowned. As they seemed of-
fended I changed the subject, and asked
them if they’d seen the Irish crochet in
the other window. They hadn’t, and brightened
perceptibly. Some of the lace was lacey-looking.
Other pieces were more like cordage—ropes of
white cordage welted into artistic design. This
sort, I told them, was the newest thing for trim-
ming yacht sails. Every yacht that thinks itself
a real bird—an albatross—has this gigantic crochet
•on its pinnies—sails I mean. Now, would you
believe it, those three Declensions didn’t know I
was talking “guff.” They fell on me as one
female and insisted that that sort of crochet lace was
used on dresses—linen dresses and twills. They
had seen it. In fact, the youngest Declension, who
had waned more than the others till she looked like
the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire,
was having made a white, washing serge outrigged
with just such lace. I gave in, and +v> ey were so
flattered at having taught me something that they
told me where I could get a floral silk, ivory white
ground and delicate tracery of grey with tiny pink
buds, for a shilling a yard. Screw your head
around, Clammy One, and look at the second
Declension. She’s trying to drink soda ice through
a straw and reskewer her hat at the same time.
Notice the yoke of her blouse?”
“ It seems to have spots of rose and turquoise
on it,” I answered. “Anyhow it’s very pretty.
So’s She.” “The yoke is pretty,” assented my
neighbor, “and the wearer is an artful little
dodger. I was with her when she bought that
lace, a pretty enough thing in oriental. In the
design was a detail like—like the nail of your
littlest finger. Well, she herself worked up the
yoke—painted the lily, so to speak. Into the
little half circle receptacles she inserted bouquets
—Titania bouquets, of colored ribbons. Some girls
can gerrymander with their clothes so successfully.
Don’t let us talk any more drivel. Clammy One.
Take the cover off the teapot and let the tannin
cool. Heavens, why, when I was in the pride of
my youth and beauty, why did I not form a
platonic affection for some Chinese or Indian
duke, so that I might to-day get presents of
tea— tea! Sydney is swamped with the sweep-
ings of the foreign markets. Can’t you say any-
thing, Clammy One ? Cheer up, dear girl, you’ll
soon be dead. Most people are dead.”
“I had such a disagreeable dream last night,”
I replied, “ and your mention of ‘ drivel ’ reminds
me of it. I dreamt I was walking by the shore of
a sinister-looking lake, or sea, and discussing a
Great Thought with a dream companion. The
whole scene was as distinct as our present sur-
roundings. A carnation and grey sky in the west,
a mighty cloud the color of gun metal in the east.
I was walking with my thumbs stuck in my belt ”
“ What kind of a belt ? ” asked the Girl Who
Doesn’t Mind the Weather, eagerly. “Just a white
kid with an enamel buckle —oh, I don’t know ! It
was any old belt. What does it matter ? ” “ I’m
so literal,” she apologised. “ All the same, I can’t
imagine your discussing a Great Subject with
your thumbs stuck in your belt.” “It must have
been through reading an article in an English
paper,” I said, “telling that during the recent
English elections the feminine nobility and gentry
who helped their husbands’ electioneering took
that attitude while telling the voters who to vote
for. I must, in my dream, have been copying
the duchesses, countesses, and other titled
spokesses. The dream does not interest you ? ”
“ Hurry up then.” “ We walked by the dark water
absorbed in our talk when, suddenly, there
on the calm surface we saw the dead body of a
young man in evening dress. He was floating
upright, only half submerged—and how do you
think he affected us ? We stood, and calmly
gazed and criticized his tie and studs, and
wondered at which of the Pointer houses he’d
been dining ! I tell you the drivel habit is in our
marrow—more, it’s reached our dreams.”
The Girl Who etc. had half risen, and was
looking at me with horror. “O!” she said,
presently. “You, you, you thing on the
ham-pot!” We all of us know the trade-
mark on devilled ham. It was a horrid impu-
tation, and I said, coldly, “ You are no true
lady.” She burst into peals of laughter ;
then: “Forgive me, Clammy One, but your last
remark reminds me that this morning I looked at
a book called ‘ Saints in Society,’ a hundred-pound
prize novel by a woman. Opened it at the fatal
statement, made quite seriously by one of the
characters, that ‘ a true lady always obeys her
husband.’” She pealed some more, but I wasn’t
going to please her by duetting, and as a local
mummer says somewhere, there were “shrieks of
silence” from me. But in a few minutes we
resumed tannin relations, and the Girl began
to justify the use of fluff in conversation. “Did
you see ‘The Shop Girl’ on Saturday night?
Remember Lauri’s gag of ‘Excuse me—a
bit of fluff ’ as he seems to pick a fraction of
cotton wool off each customer’s clothes and
blow it into space? Well, we must have
bits of fluff on our mental clothing so our friends
may have the fun of picking them off. My dear,
talk about color ! Don t those dresses worn by
the eight tall girls ‘of the Syndicate Theatre’
make a garden of heart’s delight ? That old rose
of Georgie Musgrove’s—with its blouse of cloth-of-
silver and skeleton zouave of the old rose silk
patterned Greek with gold thread ! And the
Undine green with rose du Barry velvet, worn by
another charmer. Clara Clifton’s gown—by
the way, Clara was Ada Smith in the piece, and
the name reminds me that a recent writer
objects to Smith being called a common
name. Most of the people who originally had
it made armor. It is a frequent name, not a
common one. Well, Clifton’s gown of pearl grey
satin and silver star dust is a peach, fits her like
the skin on a peach, too. Say, I saw a very tall,
plump girl trying on that dress for .Miss Clifton
who couldn’t come for a fitting, and the fitters had
padded out the understudy with the whole of'an
ironing blanket.
“ Didn’t notice anything specially new in the
dressing of the audience. A stallite had one of
those new pelerines, a regular maze of chiffon and
swansdown. Saw one in a shop window to-day,
but the down was tinged a beautiful lotus blue.
Perhaps the swans had been swimming among
blue water lilies. Have you got a spare hairpin ?
Thanks. I’m off. Just look if the back buckle
of my belt is over the centre knob of my spine.
If it’s not it makes one look so out of drawing.”
She went, and a great peace settled over the
dusty face of things.
When some months ago Miss Nora Long, the
M.L. soprano, slipped into the place left vacant
by the absence of ex-Governor Clarke’s daughter
at a concert, she grasped unconsciously the skirts
of happy chance. Said concert was at Lord
Methuen’s house, and so pleased was the section
of music-loving peerage present with the soprano’s
work that-it raised its coronet to her, and said
that as soon as she liked to give a recital on her
own it would take all the higher-priced seats. The
recital occurred last week, and the cable says the
success was artistic, financial and unmistakable.
Which, as Mr. Pepys would say, “is very nice.”
Miss Sylvia Blackston’s recital, given after her
Bechstein Hall ballad concert, seems to have been
a success. Critics of five leading dailies say
pleasant things of her present and hopeful things
of her future in song.
Ferdinando Karoly is the romantic-sounding
name of the romantic-looking young musician who
has been deported to Australia to prove to us
what a wonderful invention as a piano player
is the claviola under skilful management.
Ferdinando and his hyacinthine curls held
a sort of combined concert and At Home
at Paling’s last week, and showed how
the charm was worked. There .is a great
demand these days, when the world is more and
more in a hurry, for royal roads to the cardinal
arts. The claviola seems to be a direct and very
royal avenue to Beethoven, Chopin, and the other
musical immortals. They became immortal by
hand. Nowadays you press a button and become
immortal by machinery.
Next Thursday evening Mr. Samuel Turner,
F.R.G.S., an Alpine climber and explorer, will
speak on “Siberia Revealed” and “Mount Cook
Explored.” The colossal revelations will be illu-
strated by lantern views. The advantage of
climbing a mountain in a lantern view is that you
get up in less time, and if you fall off the top of
the peak in the illustration you don’t suffer so
much damage.
Miss Ruby See, second daughter of ex-Premier
John See, of Randwick, is to marry Mr. John
Meeks, of Auburn, Melbourne. The bride-elect,
who looks well in a smart riding habit and white
helmet, has been about considerably on horseback
with her future owner. All engaged couples who
go. a-riding acquire a fancy (if they’ve not already
got it) for that poem of Browning’s, “The Last
Ride Together.” When this daughter is married
the paternal See will have only his Lily maid and
a couple of young sons at home. The ex-Premier,
it may be remarked, has shaved off his beard, and
looks what the Yankees call “a Society Shine.”
The summer stagnation of the waters of society
life shows signs of breaking up. Yesterday
Mayoress Allen Taylor stirred the pool a little
when she held a reception in her enlarged and
re-decorated rooms at the Town Hall. And the
Governor-General North Cote will come here
towards the end of this week, and abide with us
during the falling of the leaf, which doesn’t fall in
Australia unless the leaf was imported. Now it
behoves all absentees •in Maoriland, Bowral,
Katoomba, and elsewhere to come right home on
pain of forfeiture of their cell in the society ant-hill.
“ Many inventions ” (as the Bible and Kipling
have it). Last week at Mrs. H. L. Jones’s fare-
well ladies’ luncheon at the Australia, the menu
cards were fashioned after the style of the dandiest
little deck-chairs. The- list of choice eats was
written on the canvas of the chair, and each guest’s
place was indicated by a miniature life-belt in-
scribed with her name. If you didn’t know your
neighbor you just exchanged life-belts and became
acquainted. About half the large party was wife
of the American male colony in Sydney—very
pleasant, well-dressed Aunt Sams they were. The
hostess, herself an American, is wife of the
American vice-Consul here. She goes to Denver
with her husband and infant to regain her physical
condition —salted by too much of our nor’easter.
A son of the Randal Careys was married last
Saturday to a Manly girl named Brown, grand-
daughter of the late Mr. Alexander Dean, and a
big slice of the wedding party took the centre of
the horseshoe at first night of “ The Shop Girl,”
and put its posy on the rail. All the blessed
damozels leant out over the wooden bar above in
the gallery, and asked “Is them the Gov’nor’s
crowd ? ”
The trustees of the National Art Gallery are
not likely to hang the travelling “ Light of the
World” without guarantees of its authenticity.
Meanwhile five out of every six of the population
of other States seem to have visited or to be.
visiting it. Holman Hunt got enormous prices
for his pictures. “The Scapegoat”—the real
original old Scapegoat—is a little water color,
and its price sounds like the war indemnity paid
by a conquered nation. Talking of pictures:
The new acquisition of the Gallery, “A Snow
Scene in Northern Italy,” an oil, hasn’t sent
the trustees mad with joy. As for the public, it
goes and stands opposite the "picture and says “I
wouldn’t give ten bob for it.” A solitary dingy
figure of a woman plods through pale violet snow
that has evidently been laid on with a trowel and
then curry - combed. Bleached - violet snow
stretches back of the figure and lies on the
mountains almost to their summits, where it turns
to dirty white snow. Apologists for the picture
say Maggi, the painter, knows his scene of action,
and that we do not. We have not studied snow
scenes in all their moods and tenses. Send a
typical Australian picture to England, and the
English ask patronisingly of the color-soaked
landscape “Is your country really run on so
chromatic a scale ? ”
There must be some sort of upheaval at the
historic Goldsbrough-Morts. Six managers of
departments have quit there and started as wool
brokers. Another, Mr. Ivan Fraser (he is married
to a daughter of the late George Maiden) is about
to start a monthly paper to be called Australian
Country Life.
From the School Music Review, condemning
the forcing of children’s voices:—
The infant school was led in its little songs by the
superintendent, a young woman of energetic tempera-
ment. At the preliminary rehearsal her constant exhort-
tation to her unhappy charges was : “Children, sing out
more ! You are not half singing—sing out ! ” The con-
sequence was that the unfortunate infants, instead of
using the sweet but delicate tones natural to childhood,
roared out “We are Jesus’ little lambs” like so many
little wolves.
I wonder why people do not alter the usual
wedding-present, which takes the monotonous
shape of a cruet or a butter-dish ! A friend on
the Richmond River, up Lismore way, writes to
me that they do things sensibly there. Amongst
the list of wedding-presents one sees “Mrs.
Gubbins, a cow; Mrs. Cowper, a poddy calf;
Mr. Piggen, a creamy pony ; whilst Mrs. Saltbush
gave the happy pair a brace of turnips, etc.” —at
least something like that. If people can’t do that
sort of thing in town, they might hand over a
ham, or a sideboard, or 12 pairs of boots, or
something equally useful.
Perhaps the tallest school-girl in Sydney is a
granddaughter of Mrs. Tyson, of North Sydney
(Mr. Ernest Tyson’s mother). The young lady
lives with her grandmother, and, though only 16,
is over six feet in height.
A girl writes me this: —
I had a fad. I wanted to earn my own living. I
wanted to prove to people that girls who would accept
ss. as lady helps when they could get 15s. as general ser-
vants were snobs and duffers, and I started out to prove
it. I wasn’t anything in particular—only a petted girl
whose father had once been rich and now was poor. So I
went to service and told my people I was governessing. I
stayed less than 24 hours. It wasn’t the work—it was, as
I sobbingly told the bewildered and wronged lady who
had engaged me when I tore off her carefully goffered’cap
and went to my room to don my old tailor-made, the feel-
ing. To take my dinner off the hacked joint in the half-
litkitchen, tobecalledby my Christian name, patronisingly
by the minx of a daughter, and familiarly by the dirty
oid man who milked the cow—to wear a ridiculous cap
that added ten years to my age, to listen to the company
chatter in the distant dining-room, to be cast into outer
darkness and sentenced to solitary confinement near the
kitchen sink, it was these things that gave me “ the
feeling.” And I sobbed and sobbed till my basket was
strapned and I was outside the gates, and then I nearly
yelled for joy. After that I tried being a lady help. I
went to a farm, where I arrived at daylight. Light rain was
falling, and the curlews curlewed dismally, and some
bird or other lamented bitterly in a swamp. My new
employer spoke quite shyly to me. She took me to a
blazing fire, and gave me tea and toast, and made much
of me, but best of all she talked to me as an equal.
The work was hard—too hard for a girl delicately
brought up—so in six months I came away a wreck.
Had to be carried in and out of the train. I got ss. a week
—as a general I would have got 15s.—but now I know why
girls who have “ come down ” prefer being lady helps to
being general servants. To feel that people respect one a.
bit—that one isn’t a worm crawling on the kitchen floor
—that makes all the difference.
But in all the households of my acquaintance,
including mv own two-person one, where only one
maid is kept, she, the general, is a female poten-
tate, and dominates the plan of campaign.
Missuses are so afraid of her quitting when she is
fairly competent that it is they who do the worm
apt. The average serving damsel has a better
time and more respect than the average governess.
As for the “ ridiculous cap,” nurses and waitresses
don’t look specialised and coquettish until they
put on their caps, and Merely Mary Ann’s cap is
very like theirs and just as becoming as theirs.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

At a big N.S.W. country town, some
years ago, a man was found guilty of murder, and
sentenced to be hanged. The execution was fixed
for a certain date, and, the day before, “Nosey”
and his assistant arrived from Sydney. That
night, a public ball, in aid of some local charity,
was being held. Amongst the throng of dancers
was a fine-looking, athletic man, who had no diffi-
culty in getting introductions to numbers of nice
girls. They all voted him a good dancer, and
“ hoped he was going to stay in Slowtown for
some time.” Suddenly a change came, and little
groups were seen hurriedly taking their departure.
In a quarter of an hour the ballroom was empty.
A whisper had gone round ; the hero of the even-
ing was the assistant hangman. . -
“ School Miss ” wails from Broken Hill:—
It is 112° in the shade here at Broken Hell, with a hot
wind filling the air with blinding red dust. The Pro-
prietary and Junction North mines are on fire, and have
been burning for some days. The engines play on them
with steam, so you can imagine how close to Sheol we
feel. People are praying for a copl change—but not for
rain. A good dust storm is what we want to kill the
awful heat. Bain would most likelymean typhoid to all
not yet acclimatised. The summer climate here is simply
demoralising. • I get home to the boarding-house each
afternoon with no brain power left, and just sufficient
energy to bath myself. Then I lie down and swelter
till a badlv-cooked dinner is ready. Afterwards the
whole household flops into the balcony chairs and doesn’t
move till the bed procession starts. It is a spectacle to
meet 30 people, each person shouldering a mattress and a
bundle of bed clothes, and all making for the open air.
We have a comfortless, broken sleep for an hour or two,
then day breaks again over the town of Broken Sheol,
and the weary-eyed, haggard procession returns to the
bedrooms. At 7 o’clock comes tea, for which we are
craving, and with it the day, far, far worse than the
night. I have to wade backward and forward through
half-a-mile of red dust to the school twice a day, and most
times arrive with all energy absolutely at its lowest ebb,
Oh, for a dust-storm to cool this inferno ! I am writing in
a bath of perspiration, and the glare outside is something
terrible. I would give 12 months'of my life to feel a hiss-
ing, white-capped Manly breaker burst over me at this
present moment.
Yet the man' who writes this tag to “School
Miss’s ” wail is prepared to swear that he never
felt as limp during many long summers spent in
Broken Hill as he has in this last Sydney summer.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 6, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie.—-
Every smart and hopeful girleen in Melbourne
turned out to peer at the union of Miss Frankie
Oliver and Mr. Frank Murray. A wild desire to
get away from the conventional prompted the
bride to go pink-trimmed to the rails, and to do
so in a rose-pink atmosphere. For once, chiffon
was introduced into the church. It fluttered
flippantly from arch to pillar of the solemn
fcMliiis-st; kirk, and pink blossoms played ring-a-
firig-fl-rbSy overhead. Above the rails, doves
■vkith outspread wings prepared for flight down
the pathway of the piously-joined. True-love
knots added a further note of sentiment in the
blush-pink scheme. The bride’s decollete rig had
a train as long as a sigh of dejection. Dawn-pink
and moonlight-silver gleamed from the ivory gar-
ment, and vaporous chiffons rolled languidly oyer
the paradoxical effects. A posy of pink orchids
and stephanotis added to the blushfulness of the
decoration scheme. A quintet of girleens fol-
lowed the star—Misses Maud Fenner, Alison
Fairbairn, Marjorie Levey, M. Manifold and L.
Kerr. These looked too picturesque for a church
ceremony in their Mary Carlyle rigs and head-
caps with curls a-dangle. Coquetry in chiffons
reached the absolute limit in their case, for they
had all let their hair down in comic-opera style.
The Oliver guests included the cream of Mel-
bourne. Their decorations ran to hat-streamers
and ear-drops. Every alternate She sported coral
or diamond ear-pendants. Mrs. Jack Fisken led in
a marvellous shape, sporting an immense buckle
and full peacock tails, other streamers of heavy
green-shot crepe hanging like a portiere to the
rear. The dame really looked like a peacock’s
funeral, with the dead bird, a band, and a pall.
Mrs. Willie Manifold went into velvet black
streamers of a deadly character. No dame looked
as well as the bride’s brilliant mamma. She wore
biscuit and hyacinth blue. Mrs. Oliver even now
is a more gorgeous Grace than her much-boomed
daughter. Of the younger fry Miss Lesly Madden,
in her old Cup chiffons, cut the greatest dash.
The canary and cream creation has a Parisian
finish, and the dicky-bird tails to the coat carried
out the canary notion. A cafe-chantant hat had
a riot of gay roses. Miss Vera Clarke topped a
109‘-in-the-shade, flowered frock with a lO’-below-
Fahrenheit hat. Banker Urquhart’s dame sur-
passed all the opulent in a display of gems,
antique and modern,
Jewels formed a large portion of the Oliver
wedding gifts, The bride’s passion for trinkets
led to a wholesale offering of earrings, neck gew-
gaws and sundry illuminants. She wore some

Brighton supplied a final February flutter in
the hitcliing-up of Miss Ivy Webb and Mr. Stan-
ley Hammond—both popular young people in the
salt suburb. The bridal gear had a startling
decoration of silver roses and some covetable
Limerick lace. It was otherwise a subdued affair.
Pancake Tuesday saw a frantic rush to the
Church rails. Some couples, discovering at the
last moment that the close season for Church-
mating was at hand, hustled along to the
ecclesiastic joiners with the ring in one hand and
the vow in the other, before the Lenten shutters
dropped. One parson tied five knots in one warm
February noon. That stands as the record of
years.
It’s time the bride protested against the papers
that publish her photographed smile all awry and
her eye on the turn—“skew-whiff” I have heard
it called. Grotesque effects have been produced
by inconsiderate and careless printing. Last week,
one of South Yarra’s loveliest lassies appeared in
a paper with an uncanny squint which suggested
that one eye had a suspicion it was being pursued
by a dog.
One of the loveliest of Melbourne’s greying
spinsters has her considering cap on while a
woolly adorer waits on the mat. The affair is
being breathlessly discussed. •

goes to the Cold Country for the purpose. All

mers are pounding: the sheepskin implement to
E reclaim the engagement of Miss Alison Fair-
aim to the chief caddy of the Governor-General’s
staff—Captain Stewart Balmain. The Aide has
the right of sporting the 15th Hussars regimen-
tals, and boasts a soldiering record. On the
present staff he glories in more energy than there
is demand for, and has a spry manner of carrying
out his microscopic duties like an ant hurrying
home loaded with nothing. The Miss is the only
hope of M.L.A. Fairbairn, the richest member of
the clan, and the one who raises the best wool in
the Western district. She’s a good sportswoman, a
good chum, and a beauty.
A small flutter rises by reason of the tidings of
another newly-clinched engagement —that of Miss
Bona Syme and a Sydneyite—Mr. Eric Chapman.
Miss Rona is the plump, cheerful daughter of the
Balwyn York-Symes and the niece of Yes-No
Reid. She used to take but an indifferent interest
in her mamma’s hundred charitable schemes.
It was the ease of Dickens’ Mrs. Jellyby and her
Caddy all over again, except that Miss Syme
wasn’t inky like Caddy. Even the news that the
biggest savage at Borrioboola Gha hadn’t a bell-
topper to his name never roused the ycung lady to
any great excitement.
There was an early autumn nip in the air on
Saturday that vindicated the wearing of the new
cold rigs at Flemington, but sufficient warmth
remained in the sunshine to give excuse for a last
turnout of the November chiffons. Becoming as
one of the last Roses of Summer, in powder pink
and fleecy white, was Miss A. Fairbairn, making a
triumphal tour of the Flemington tiles with her
vice-regal attache. In autumn tones of royal and
cornflower blue, Mrs. Norton Grimwade achieved
her usual dress sensation. The dame of drug
wealth takes a loud, gay glory in forward fashions.
Mrs. Willie Manifold, queening it over a dressy
detachment from her woolly quarter, was ahead
of the lot in a mauve basin-crowned bowler top-
piece with a brush at its sporty side. A peram-
bulator-dealer’s young wife was one of the ser-
pentine summery figures on the strut. She wore a
dress fitting like a glove, composed of many miles
of narrow Val. Probably the lace drawn out to
its entire length would cover the distance from
this city to the Apple Isle.
It upsets the Cream to have a Commonwealth
Ex. that gets lost in the crowd, At Flemington
on Saturday the G.-G. arrived. Everyone knew
by the equipage and the guards. And he left.
All knew that. But among the crowd during the
day he was lost, stolen or strayed. They saw him
for a little while, and then no more.
Ex-Private-Secretary Fitzpatrick revolves again
about Toorak. He begins to lose his band-box
look, and now has the swarthy air and surging
gait of the man on the land. The Carpentaria
promontory seems the best place for losing the
fashionable crease of the Vice-Regal habit. The
temperature leads on to the neglect of hat-gloss
and fancy socks.
Simultaneously with the return to the Bent
State of a gadding sport pops up the most noto-
rious young charmer who has ever startled the
Flemington flock. Last Saturday she caused a
thrill through the whole gee-gee throng by her
autumn magnificence.
The first of Menzies’ tea and music junkets
started on Monday. As a way of using up the
off-days of the gee-gee week, this muffin and
music notion is reckoned tip-top. The private
entertainer is becoming an unknown quantity here.
In fact, she is mostly away on the heaving deep,
and her Chippendale suite is in its bib and pinny
during her jaunt.
The Ogilvie family mounted the P. and O. gang-
way last Tuesday. A shoal of chums swept over
the decks in a tidal wave of farewells.
Mrs. W. Manifold, whose daughter made her
first long-skirted appearance at the Oliver wedding,
takes the great gad abroad before long with her
pretty fledgling.
The Banker Urquharts are due to lay out a
large order for cabin trunks and deck-chairs.
They go with their flock to the Cold Country
before the Government starts its winter fires.
Hundreds of farewell wafers and parting cups
of popular cheer have been got through by the
Cliveden people and their friends at the various
headquarters of Charity and Culchaw. Even the
newsboys swelled the farewell chorus, and helped
to persuade the dowager she was something
higher than an Excellency. And all through the
flattering sprees, she wore a two-inch smile, with
a spray of wired exotics pinned over her palpi-
tating heart. The florists really lose a valuable
customer in the dowager. She never attended a
sewing-bee, presided at a mothers’ lamentation
meeting, or chaired a bush-blaze committee with-
out the bosom-spray of wired blooms, and her
orders for table blossoms were a nice fat item.
Mrs. Nial and her chick, whose gamps have
been hanging on a Menzies’ rack for many a
month, start for crowded London in the wake of a
large Toorak contingent. All the hopeless marms
with cash and girls to spare are off on the track.
Tommy Bent has damaged his funny-bone.
That may stop a lot of his clowning ; but it won’t
stop the barrel Premier visiting the theatres.
He’s one of the circle regulars, and cuts a wonder-
ful figure in an immense area of fried shirt and a
coat that doesn’t meet soon enough, while above
his blunt, whiskered countenance his specs, are
balanced on the bump of variable temper. The
Bent Premier generally brings a niece along, and
gets her to repeat any of the applauded jokes he
has missed. It must be trying to be niece to a
fat politician whose attention wanders.
A Melbourne pair has cut the marriage
tie in the land where they who seek divorce
shall surely find it. This time it’s the Comic
Opera tripper Mrs. Eric Neill, who always footed
it under her original title—lrene Outtrim —on the
Williamsonian stage. Captain Neill had served
in the Victorian Contingent among the kopjes
before Irene, of the “Florodora” gentle maidens,
decided that as she must love someone, it might as
well be he. In Uncle Sam’s country subsequently,
he turned from active captaincy to Inky pursuits,
and traversed Dollar Land for copy and distrac-
tion, while his liege Missus twirled her toes in
vaudeville. The Outtrims form a large clan about
here and are mostly given to music and church
choirs. Even Deputy Postmaster Outtrim pedals
the organ magnificently and glories in a capital
basso-profundo voice. His only son was the
second member of the clan to take to a career of
grease, abandoning pulpit ambitions to raise his
melodious voice in opera bouffe.
There has been a to-do at one of the recent
salt-dip competitions over a candidate who caused
a sensation, after a splendid double-somersault
dive, by appearing in as much paper-covering as
ordinarily suffices for a bag of sweets. It seems,
before throwing herself into the brine, she posed
as a paper sunflower, but after conflict with the
waves her petals were no more. As she had to
come up for the third time and stay up, the Purity
Brigade raised a cry of horror even though the
child would only have represented a toy Venus.
Young “Mac.” Sharwood, the Central Queens-
lander who died in London t’other day, un-
attended by a doctor, and thereby brought his
landlord and fellow-Christian Scientist into the
ken of coroner and jury, is the only brother of
pretty little Mrs. Steve Fairbairn. Said dainty
pocket-charmer was formerly Miss Nellie Shar-
wood, of Aramac (Queensland). Her father, the
late Sydney Sharwood, was a well-known mid-
Bananaland identity, and when his motherless
girl and boy grew up, the former kept house for
Pa, and shone out such a chic little bit of Dresden
China among the sordid surroundings of the
western town that in no time she was wooed and
married by Mr. Stephen Fairbairn, a neighboring
station-owner, and a scion of the well-known Vic-
torian pastoral clan. Her bridegroom, tall, dark,
handsome, and an ex-member of the Cambridge
Eight, was, prior to his marriage, regarded as the
best parti of the hour in Melbourne society.
When Sharwood pere died, his son spent most of
his time with the Fairbairns, followed in his
brother-in-law’s footsteps at Cambridge, and, like
him, rowed in the Eight. He was the first
Bananalander to volunteer for the Boer war,
saw the fighting through, and, altogether, was the
last man one would have expected to die of “ fatty
degeneration of the heart, brought on by per-
nicious anaemia.”
Some readers of Nellie Stewart’s recent letters
from ’Frisco to her sister here are surprised at. the
unconventional Helen’s fondness for religious
phraseology. But Nellie at all times revels in
religious sentiment. ’Board ship she yards in her
daughter every Sunday and imparts theological
instruction. On her father’s tomb in Kew (Vic.)
cemetery, Nellie, served up in white marble, is
depicted as a praying angel, and on at least one
occasion she took the floor as a preacher and
moral teacher, and while handing a riding-whip to
Rupert Clarke’s jockey treated the boy to an un-
expected slab of pious eloquence. The versatile
Nellie is a model mother, and when other travel-
lers’ children are making nuisances of themselves
on board ship, her one ewe lamb is tied to
mamma’s apron-strings and trained in the way
she should go.
In consequence of the evil weather and other
things, the annual tournament —usually the sum-
mer swan-note of excitement—is postponed till
dewy April, when the remnant of Sassiety here
will rush the gymkhana revels.
The she-bowlers of South Melbourne and Albert
Park —twin suburbs devoted to perambulatory
habits—had a tournament the other day and tried
conclusions at the round man's thirstiest game.
As the South Melbourne players persisted in look-
ing nice and wearing their straight-fronts with the
usual pull on the girth-strap, they lost the game
ignominiously. The other team went without
stylish appointments, and let its waist gang its
ain gait.

(it sounds something like a cat when it is angry),
still lingers, though the Stirling season is long
finished and done with. At the “ Leah Kleschna ”
show on Saturday he wore his long locks in the
raw. They’re only worn in the fricasseed or scal-
loped state for the platform.
On one of the recent hot days, the ArtGallery offi-
cials fainted so badly that they gave up confiscating
brollies at the door, and women took them inside,
and used them to prod small children out of the
way, and rested them on the corns of their fellow-
creatures, and pointed excitedly with them at the
“ Light of the World.” No woman drove her
gamp through the “ Light,” but such a thing may
happen if the whalebone object is allowed to
accompany its owner as a regular thing. Some
women are very bad judges of distances, and
shockingly bad shots with an umbrella.

TRYPHENA.
“Ralph Rodney”: It seems very appa-
rent already that the N.S. W. Liquor Law is not
to be pressed home to the inconvenience of the
lover of the late cup that beers, &c. This is to be
sadly regretted, for the city breath cleared wonder-
fully in those few brief weeks during which the
Act lived and bit round regardless. But what is
really wanted is an Act to render shouting illegal.
That would bring drinking within the range of
sanity, and drunkenness would soon be a thing of
the past. Nowadays a man of 70, riding into town
on a raw day. feels, on reaching his destination,
that a drop of spirit would do him good. As he
enters the bar he is met by Jack, Tom, Dick, and
Harry. “Hello, Jimmy,old son.” “ Hello, Harry,”
“ Hello, Tommy,” &e. &e. Then he feels com-
pelled to say, “What are you going to have?”
And bang goes half-a-crown. Then Tom realises
he must shout. Harry next, and then Dick and
Jack. Finally old Three-score-and-ten struggles
out flustered and foolish, and the publican rattles
five half-crowns into his till. It’s time the shout
was made punishable with three months hard and
bitter “jug.”

Patronising local industries isn’t a strong
point with Auckland (M.L.) Hospital Board. It
is sending all the way to Paris for two skeletons
to train the nurses on. Yet the dead Maorilander
has as many bones as anybody else.
W. N. Willis—the eternal W. N. Willis—
has been re-arrested in Natal, so the struggle over
the extradition question begins afresh. A time
may yet come When the N.S.W. parliament will
pass an Act' declaring that there is no W. N.
Willis and never was one, merely because it feels
it will go mad if it hears the name any more.
“S.R.” : Haul Holman off. What the
devil does he mean by playing into Reid’s hands
like this? Reid’s a clown, a political buffoon,
one to be ridiculed and gibed at. No self-respect-
ing politician should lower himself by taking
Reid seriously, and as for taking the arena with
him in debate, well, I’m surprised at Holman.
George Reid magnanimously declares that
“he will join forces with the Protectionists,”
with the object of fighting Socialism.
Sydney Benevolent Society gives further
proof of how that city is being “ruined by
Federation.’’ In 1905 an average of 563 cases of
poverty were relieved per week. In 1904 the
average was 730 cases. In 1903 902 cases, which
was about the average of the previous years. So
there’s a decrease in two years of 339 cases—or
over 33 1-3 per cent.—in the poverty of the city.

the State documents under the copyright law. One of
the States has objected to handing over its records. This
may necessitate Copying the documents.
Can anyone indicate that contrary kid in one
guess—the futile snarler that makes extra Federal 1
expenditure necessary, and then howls at the
bill?

THE POT TO THE KETTLE: “ Hullo , old smutty
face!”
Melbourne Tramway Company is doing
well in its fight to have its present monopoly ex-
tended beyond 1916 —the year in which the muni-
cipal trust is supposed to take over the business.
The company has met the cry for penny sections
by suggesting an extension of its lease in return
for this “ concession ” ; and, instead of demanding
that the State should exercise its right and compul-
sorily revise the fares, one council (Port Mel-
bourne) has already empowered its representative
on the Tramway Trust to grant “reasonable con-
cessions ”if the penny section is established. Other
councils seem likely to follow suit.
The N.S.W. Labor Council has again de-
clared for free primary education, and now the
Labor party should try its band at forcing the
Government into action, A majority of the
Ministers are committed to the principle, but will
wriggle out of giving effect to it, if they can.
A private member’s bald resolution re the expe-
diency of the thing counts for nothing. Ever
since 1.883, when Teece, M.L.A., carried such a
resolution, the Assembly has been “resolving” in
favor of free education, but has never taken the
slightest notice of its own resolutions, though it
has yelled in its own ear almost annually.
Be land nationalisation. Away back in
the early ’sixties, “ P&ddy ’’O’Sullivan, then one
of the West Moretoh hunch. ‘made'an attempt in
that direction in the Queensland Assembly. The
first Land Bill ;was going through, : and O’Sulli-
van moved that the whole of the Darling
Downs be leased and not alienated. He
found two supporters—Tames-Morgan- and
William Henry Groom —out of a House
of 36 members. Then the work of
“ consolidating” the runs Was com-
menced— dummying, and
every known device for embezzling \
the people’s property, were resorted
to. Every available rood "of the r
water-frontages was thieved, and '
bona-fide selectors were thrown back
on stony, waterless ridges. Later on,
the cry for room went up, and the
“Continuous Ministry” started to
repurchase from big syndicates, into
whose claws a majority of the large
estates of the Downs had gravitated.
As high as £4 10s. per acre was paid for
Gowrie, and over £3 per acre was passed
over for Glengallan, Toolburra, and Head-
iagton Hill. Selling land at 10s. per acre
and buying it back for £3 per acre would
be set down as a silly piece of finance in private
enterprise, but the State seems to live on that
kind of business. In fact, Queensland has had
one long, continuous drunk on it; but when
it has to pass its horse, saddle and bridle over to
publican Cohen, and hump bfuey over a new
track, the day of wrath will be mighty adjacent.
Kaiser William of Germany has again been
waving his mailed salary-hook, and talking
threateningly about what the Germans did in
1870. Some day, perhaps, Europe will get tired of
this sort of thing, and set out to remind Germany
of what it did in 1806, when it set an example of
fast running to all Europe. This year is the cen-
tenary of Jena, and Kaiser W. is waving the
mailed extremity with an energy which makes
Europe think of the fact.
From a last week’s cablegram about the
obliteration of some missionaries in China
On learning of the massacre the Empress Dowager and
the Court fell into a state of collapse.
Presumably the empress flopped, and then the
court fell down insensible to be in the fashion.
And doubtless, when the news spread, the army
and navy fainted, and the civil service sent in a
medical certificate that it couldn’t get down to the
office by reason of nervous prostration.
A Melbourne wicker-worker witness told
the Tariff Commission that two Victorian wicker-
work perambulator bodies were sent to China by
a Melbourne firm, and that China is now sending
back hundreds of copies of these samples. Even
Labor Freetraders woke up at that. These illogi-
cal persons have enthusiastically excluded the
Chow ; and when it was thus plainly put to
them that, so long as they admitted the Chow-
made article duty free, the country would actually
be a trifle better off had they admitted the yellow
aliens themselves and put them under some sort
of restraint as to working conditions, they began
to think there was something wrong with their
fool theory.
Brisbane’s Board of Waterworks is
marked for extinction, having earned that
fate by many sins of omission and commis-
sion. Brisbane Council has power to take
control of the water supply. It is doubt-
ful, however, whether such a change would
improve matters. The city has to face the
problem of a new water supply early, and
to grapple with some sort of a sewerage
scheme. That means increased taxation, and
the aldermen, elected under .a conservative
franchise, who would talk about raising the
rates would go out next election.. The water
supply would be much better conducted by
an expert board of three commissioners until
such time as Parliament passes an Act
widening the municipal franchise.
Justice Cooper, of Maoriland, lately
quashed an indictment in which a man
was charged with incest, the female being
his step-daughter. He said that although
the crime was a matrimonial offence under
the Divorce Act, it wasn’t a criminal
offence, cognisable in Maoriland in a court
of law. In England it was not only a
matrimonial offence, but also an ecclesiastical
offence, punishable by ecclesiastical courts
where their jurisdiction existed, by penance.
In Scotland it was a criminal offence.
There was no tie of consanguinity between
step-father and step-daughter here, so there
was no crime disclosed. As several cases
of this kind, have happened in M.L. lately,
it’s about time the Act was amended, or
added to, or something.
A pathetic circumstance at the Australian
Manufacturers Exhibition in Melbourne was a
show-case full of beautiful rugs and tweeds, ex-
hibited by a city firm which had bought the rem-
nant stock of the Australian Woollen Mills, at
Footscray. They were splendid specimens of
workmanship, superior to any imported shoddy
or mungo; but the day the Exhibition opened
the Bank had closed on its mortgage, and the
factory was lying idle and empty —killed by the
mongrel tariff that George Reid fought for, and
the Freetrade Laborites weakly helped him to
get.
According to the evidence evoked so far
as the inquiry has gone, the three N.S.W. Rail-
way Commissioners have long been divided—
the two men who had been trained in railway
service against Commissioner Oliver, the man
who came from the Lands. The two sections
didn’t shake hands, they didn’t eat together, they
drank separately, and the only amenities which
passed between them seem to have been bricks.
But amid the endless tales of their personal
squabbles, which seem often very like the anger
of two old women over the back yard fence, there
is a tendency to lose sight of the trouble that
began the shindy—the very remarkable deal in
coal whereby Commissioners Fehon and Kirkcaldie
gave such a very remarkable lift to the Lithgow
Coal Association. The public wants to hear more
about that, and less about the personal disagree-
ments which made it impossible for Commissioners
11. and 111, to hang their hats alongside Commis-
sioner I.’s hat without feeling degraded by
proximity to the hat of Commissioner I.

Camperdown, one of the little
hanky boroughs around Sydney, has
decided, so far as a five to four vote of
its miserable aldermen can decide the
matter, not to amalgamate with the
Sydney Council, and thus give a small
start to a Greater Sydney consolida-
tion. Camperdown is a bankrupt
municipality, with a receiver in charge
of its cash-box, and presumably it will
now sit in frozen dignity, with its
bailiff in its mortgaged Town Hall.
It won’t even be able to take him out
for a walk; for the Camperdown streets
are so badly kept as to be dangerous to
traffic; they are mostly holes, and
grass, and debris, and bush. An Act
is badly wanted to take the question
of consolidation out of the hands of
pottering little mayors, aldermen, town
clerks, and similar small human
truck, and to let the people have a turn
at it.
Vie. M.L.A. Fairbairn gave the
gag about capital being “drivenaway”
a new application at a recent meeting
of Women’s Leaguers. “Capital,” he
said, “was rapidly accumulating in
Victoria because capitalists were afraid
to invest it in consequence of the enact-
ment of Socialistic legislation.” “Yes,
sir,” says the poor fellow as he looks at
his swelling bank-book ; “ yes, sir, this
Socialism’s ruining me. Last year I
had £2o,ooonow. I’ve accumulated
£25,000. If. I don’t stop, I’ll have
£30,000 soon., The country’s going to
the dogs, sir—going to the dogs !”
Queensland’s discredited ex-Premier Philp
has no policy except borrowing. At the cheap feed
provided for him recently by the Toowoomba
Chamber of Commerce, he expressed a desire to
see a big loan floated to encourage immigration.
Oneensland has already borrowed more than all
Queensland lias already borrowed more tnan au
the other States put together to encourage lmmi-
gration, and hasn’t made much out of it. But the
old Loan Brigade is incapable of learning by the
awful experiences of the past.
Though Brisbane Board of Waterworks is
obviously doomed, it talks now of importing a
Yankee engineer to prepare still another sheaf of
plans for an improved supply. Almost enough
money has been spent already on reports about
the Stradbrooke Island and other schemes to give
the city a decent service; but the reports and
plans have only beenby Australian engineers, and
the Australian engineer isn’t good enough for the
Brisbane Board. The last time the same crowd
imported a Yankee engineer he pocketed his fee
and then sold the State three expensive machines
which were a dead failure from the start. But the
Brisbane Board of Waterworks has a memory like
a sieve for some things.

They get a notion into their heads that they are
there to administer justice and to hang the law.”
■ —Queensland J ustice Beal on the Full Court Bench ,
Vic. Railway Commissioner Tait may not
be able to do much towards preventing derail-
ments and other accidents—there have been
5343 persons injured on the Victorian lines
during the last 10 yeats as against 3073 dur-
ing the previous 10—but as 'a' color-expert
he shines with the brilliance of a Leighton.
He lias had the huge Spencer-street
offices stained a charming ginger-pink,
and the locortlotives he has altered from
their former green to a sweet chocolate-
brown. Now he has noticed that some
of the luggage-porters and other small
fry deck themselves in neckties of gor-
geous hues, and forthwith he issues an
edict that in future “in all cases black
ties only should be worn.” Tait is, per-
haps, anticipating the mourning that will
presently be Worn when the starved and
neglected lines cause ’ a big smaSh. By
the way, it now transpires that whereas
there used to be Hi permanent way men on
the Gembrook line—where the last disaster
occurred—there are now only six ! 1
According to experts, the British Admiralty
is trying to work off another swindle on Australia
in" the fleet provided on loan for the Naval
Tribute. The Euryalus was a type of the vessel
to which Australia was entitled (on loan). It was
withdrawn and the Powerful substituted, an
enormous hulk of much inferior fighting power.
Australia protested and the Monmouth was
offered —which is an inferior boat to the Powerful !
Withal Australia is only getting the treatment
that a people which hires its defence must expect.
The drollest feature of Bent’s Railway
Accident Inquiry thusfar has been the Court’s
picnic trip to Gembrook (Vic.) for the purpose of
inspecting the delinquent engine. The engine lay
neglected on the place where it fell for a whole
week after the accident. Then the Railway De-
partment suddenly remembered that it was pos-
sible for this important piece of evidence to be
tampered with leisurely by any unknown persons
who anight care to come along and do it. So, to
,make amends for its strange oversight, the De-
partment sent three men —at the end of the week
—to keep guard over the engine. They covered
it with a tarpaulin, and each man in turn sat upon
the blessed thing for eight : hours, whilst his mates
filled in time looking for snakes and 1 old bird-
nests.
The unspeakable Brisbane Courier is the
first Australian newspaper to advocate inter-
marriage of the Jap with the white race !
Not until the Japanese enter into a closer relationship
with the people of the West can these barriers be
removed. ‘ Inter-marriage is the only permanent means
by which the peoples of the East and West can expect to
bridge the great natural gulf that divides them.
“The great natural gulf,” i.e. , the gulf fixed by
Nature or Providence or God —whichever you
prefer to call it. Well, the person who goes fool-
ing with great natural gulfs generally falls in.
