SPORTING NOTIONS.
Pendant’s capture of the Newmarket Handi-
cap, from whatever point of view it may be regarded,
points to the conclusion that the mare was either lucky
in getting a very straight run, with the inside rails to
guide her as soon as she reached the course proper, as it
is called, or she had a merely moderate field of horses to
run away from. Naturally the Pendant backers cried
“ What-did-I-tell-you ? "—after the race. All the week
she had been talked of as a “certainty.” The
two popular certs, were Lachlan and Pendant, and
at the start they were equally first favorites.
All the same, the horse offered much the stronger case
for a special pleader, and it still remains to be proved that
the astute Leslie Macdonald had made a mistake in think-
ing that Lachlan, given a favorable run, couldn’t be
beaten by anything carrying more than a nine-stone
burden. Pendant had two sets of “ form ”to recommend
her—one ancient and the other modern. Her modern
“form” dated from the beginning of February last,
and it had to be finally judged by her running
in the Futurity Stakes a week previous to the
Newmarket. In the Futurity Stakes (seven fur-
longs) she led by not more than a length, until
Gladsome came out and smothered her. She finishes
a trifle behind Duke of Melton, with Lachlan a head behind
her. And in the Newmarket she had to meet Duke of
Melton at the same difference in weight, and Lachlan —a
big, long-striding novice, likely to be greatly favored by
a straight course—on 121 b. worse terms than before.
Pendant’s ancient “ form ” included a second to Mairp
for the Newmarket of 1904. She was then a 5-year-old
carrying 9.12, and the race was the slowest for 15 years.
It has to be noted that last Saturday’s time was I.IOJ.
Pendant has been winner and runner-up in the two
slowest Newmarkets run during a long term of years. This
year’s field was supposed to be rich in fliers, but somehow
it happened that they didn’t fly.
Pendant, the Newmarket heroine, had run only
seven times in two years (without scoring a win) previous
to her present visit to Flemington. She was not favor-
ably handicapped, except in comparison with Mairp, who
beat her two years ago, and had been growing very old
since then. But Pendant also began to be “ aged ”on the
Ist of August last. It is a remarkable coincidence that
she and Mairp are the only two aged animals that have
won a Newmarket. Only one of the other winners—Sedi-
tion, in 1889 —was six years old, and in all three cases the
time was slow. The most slippery winners on the list
were Sir Foote, Amiable, Playaway, Cranbrook, Carlton,
Laundress, and Chantress, all of them getting over the six
furlongs in 75 seconds or less. Sir Foote’s 1.13} is the
record. Within the last fifteen years eight of the winners
have been mares.
» * * •
One of the many good points about Tartan is
his bloomin’ moniker. By Lochiel—Colors, he is one of
the most aptly named racehorses in the business.
* * * *
Unsatisfactory to find Blue Spec starting at
33 to 1 offered for the Essendon Stakes last Saturday,
whilst Scot Free was backed at 5 to 1 to beat the “ dead
bird ” Tartan. In last Melbourne Cup there was only a
neck difference between Scot Free and Blue Spec at level
weights. On Saturday the Spec was giving only 31b. to
the horse he beat last November, and he hadn’t a backer.
» * « *
A Maoriland nag named Jack Pot, owned by a
native, carried recently 4st. 21b. dead weight in a race at
Wairoa, the mannikin being perched on top of a couple of
sacks of ballast. Result was that the jockey and his horse
were utter strangers, the lad not being near enough to
his mount to get a winning gallop out of him. In fact,
he was so high above his horse that he should have had a
speaking trumpet with which to say “gee up” to the
remote beast.
* * * te
The unco guid may say what they like about
citizen J. Wren’s management of Melbourne unregistered
racing, but since the tote man took over the busi-
ness there’s been a tremendous exodus of the unre-
gistered tribe from Sydney to the city of the Yarra. The
fervent wish of Sydney is that Wren will, like Moses and
the locusts, draw ’em all that way. They will be missed
joyfully !
* « * «
The impending arrival of Professor Skuthorp’s
buckjumping show in Sydney calls to mind the slim, deaf
horseman’s celebrated repetition of the crack-brained
Lindsay Gordon leap near Mount Gambier, in South
Australia—a fence sft. 4in. high, with, on the far side of
it, and a couple of feet below the level of the bottom of
the fence, a ledge 9ft. wide, and then a clear drop of
450 ft. of sheer precipice. To negotiate this leap safely it
was necessary to jump the horse over the fence, and
actually turn him round while in the air, so as to land on
the 9ft. ledge below. If the horse played up or lost his
balance when he landed—sudden eternity. Skuthorp’s
friends were wildly anxious to tie a rope round his waist,
so that in the event of the horse failing to drop on the
ledge, or rolling over after he had landed, Skuthorp might
have a chance of life. The Professor declined the rope J
he had an idea that it would get tangled up among the
horse’s legs and lead to the very disaster it was intended
to mitigate. He went at it at a gentle trot, and
the steady horse rose like a bird, clearing the fence
by several inches. As he rose clear the Professor leaned
over and, with a tremendous wrench, tried to swing the
horse round. The animal, looking down that awful depth,
screamed, and, with a great muscular effort, swung him-
self as desired, landed on the ledge, and stood trembling.
The Professor gave the reins a shake, and his steed,
reckoning the ledge a good place to get clear of, trotted a
few yards and then leaped the fence once more. It was
a great performance, and in its way unique. There have
been other marvellous jumps, but that was about the
narrowest landing space ever jumper negotiated. Away
back in 1811, at Cairo, when Mehemet Ali had trapped
the Mamelukes in the citadel, one of the beys jumped his
horse from the ramparts and made his escape. Some
accounts say that the horse was killed under him, and he
fled on foot; others that the noble brute carried his
boss off triumphantly through the shower of bullets
that the murderous Albanian soldiers poured round him.
There’s no sporting instinct or spare chivalry about an
Albanian, for that was a sheer drop of 80ft., and even after
the bey had negotiated it; Mehemet’s blood-shedders didn’t
reckon that he had'fairly bought-his life. In 1889, Mark
Hilliary, a celebrated Canterbury horseman, rode on to a
bridge on the Mt. Peel (Maoriland) road. A landslip had
carried away the supports, and the bridge was just hang-
ing, and as Hilliary rode on to it the far end. went down
suddenly, tearing the rest with it. Hilliary, afraid of
being overwhelmed and crushed in the ruin, swung his
horse round and jumped him over the railing on to the
shingle 40 odd feet below, man and horse escaping with
only a few bruises. There are several other remarkable
jumps on record. Writer disremembers them at present,
but none of them save that of Gordon and Skuthorp
ended on a 9ft. ledge. The Professor Still has the horse,
now old and toothless, pensioned off in a quiet paddock
on the Darling Downs, Queensland.
**, * »
A bad wind and intermittent showers which
made the track dangerous for fast travelling did not pre-
vent the Sydney Thousand meeting opening on Sydney
Cricket Ground on Monday night last. Five heats were
run off, and considering,the greasy, state of the track .the
front-markers should have swept the board, but the back
men apparently took big risks and went for their lives in
more ways than one. Fifty-seven started, and of these,
owing to a tie for fifth place in the third heat, 26 qualified
for the sehn-finals on Wednesday night. Of the heat-
winners one started from the 90yd. mark, one from the
105, two from the 110, and one from the 145. The lowest
handicap was 70yd., it must be borne in mind, and of the
26 placed men the 14 men between 70 and 100yds.
inclusive supplied no less than seven, including
one heat-winner. The men on the next 30yds.
supplied 14 more starters, three heat-winners,
and seven more placed men. The men between 135 and
165yds. supplied 17 starters, one heat winner, and six
placed men. The last group (170 to 190yds.) comprised
12 starters and six placed men. So, in spite of adverse
conditions, the back men kept their end of the log up
fairly well. The Killara Five Mile scratch event brought
out 19 starters. At nine to go Arnst, Pye, Horder and
Goodson broke away, but the field came up in a lap rush.
Then Levey went away on his dead own, and kept in front
for four laps before the field caught him with his boiler
badly burst. At two to go the strong ones started to break
up the weaklings by making the pace. At the bell
Mutton, Arnst and Pye were in front, Horder, Goodson,
and Farley in the centre, and Brook, Rutt and Mayer
bringing up the rear. At the shilling stand the red
jersey of Pye streaked to the front, big Farley bent down
and fairly lifted his wheel off the ground, and the foreign
cracks were cracked right up in that final rush. Fast as
Farley flew, little Pye shot away from him and won by a
length, Brook third. Then all bunched together came
four more, and Mayer’s dope, piebald legs, devil’s num-
ber, and all the rest of his bag of tricks could bring him
no nearer than seventh.
* » * *
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen proposes to spend
£5OO on road-racing during the coming winter. The
League reaps no harvest from ibis branch of cycling, but
road-racing illustrates the real value of the bike as a
means of locomotion. The League, by the way, has 87
clubs, 1086 racing men, and a financial membership of
2700—a very prosperous outlook. #
Sandberg, winner of the Austral Wheel Race,
last week took a sudden dislike to cycling, and scratching
himself for Sydney Thousand, announced his “definite
retirement” from the business, or, as some people prefer
to call it, the sport. The push, which is always loose in
its pronunciation, calls this gentleman “Sandbag,” which
is wrong.
* * * *
What is the exact difference between the secre-
tary of a League of Wheelmen and the secretary of a
union of Hebrew hod-carriers y In N-B-W. the League of
Wheelmen was nearly rent to ribbons deciding who was
its lawful secretary, and now the Westralian League is in
the same quandary. In W. A. two gentlemen are claiming
to be the lawful secretary, and wasting money on legal ad-
vice ; and in the meantime the weary cyclist who has to
pay is cursing both secretaries. There must be something
faulty about the constitution of these leagues when this
crazy dispute can occur to more than one of them.
* * * •*
Royal Yacht Squadron’s annual race for the
Rawson Cup on Saturday was a humorous affair. The
punch bowl has to be won three times before anyone can
take it home and drink out of it. Heather and Scotia
have both owned it fora year, and Saturday’s win was the
second in succession by Bona, so one more victory by Mr.
Binnie will see the cup permanently lodged on his
sideboard. The wind came from the south-east, and the
grey, overcast sky and greyer water gave things a wintry
aspect. As time allowances were to be adjusted at the
finish, the yachts came up in a cluster, manoeuvring for
the flying’start. Just on the line ancient deep-keeler
Oithona crashed into Magic, and seemed to lift her out
of the water, and hurl her over on to the starter’s float.
The starter’s boat caved in and sank with a rush. The
way that starter and his companions scrambled aboard
the yacht was beautiful to see. The agility of the
lady ’ who changed horses midstream was nothing
compared to it, and it takes rank with the “ now you see
it —now you don’t ” performances. The disgusted Magic
had to transfer the officials to the steainej, and then,
with a protest flag flying, rushed away after the yachts
that were careering towards Manly'. Bona was not
careering—her topsail halyard had carried away.
Down by Middle Head one of the Scotia’s forward hands
fell overboard, and was nearly drowned through the
desperate attempts of two yachts to rescue him. Even-
tually his own lugger got him aboard again. Then Fleet-
wing’s ballooner split, something else carried away, and
the yacht drifted out. of the race. Petrel damaged her
gear rounding the buoy at Manly, and she, too,
drew out. Crossing the Heads, Scotia and Sun-
beam both fell overboard, or something, and de-
cided that it was too wet to continue in the
contest, and started for home (on the way, Scotia
had a narrow' shave of being piled on Middle Head). Cul-
w ulla’s captain, forgetting for the moment which flag he
.was sailing under, successfully rounded the Prince Alfred
Club’s buoy, and the time be lost going about to round
the Squadron’s mark probably cost him the race. After
that, there was no more excitement. Bona, which was
in front, kept there, and none of the others got close
enough to make their time allowance of any value. The
second and third boats were Oithona and Oulwulla.
Bona and Rawhiti will sail another match this
coming Saturday, presumably over an inside course.
What writer would like to see would be a race outside
with half a gale blowing—then there would be excite-
ment.
* ■* « »
A drab sky and grey light on Sydney wickets,
on Saturday afternoon, with faint intermittent showers
thrown in, were not conducive to brilliant cricket. The
solitary century of the day was A. B. Clarke’s 122, for
Gordon District against Middle Harbor. The next best
batting performance w 7 as that of W. E. Pite, who made 87
for Sydney District against Redfern, before Wordsworth
bowled him. Consequently the bowlers had a day out:
Fisher, of the University, bowled two overs against Pad-
dington and took two W’ickets for no runs ; Penman took
three for 23 ; Mcßeth, of Sydney, took two of Redfern’s
wickets for no runs at all; Gregg, of Burwood, took five
of Balmain’s stumps for 28 ; KeeJe,of Waverlcy, absconded
with four of Central Cumberland’s for 19. These are tall
figures, and speaks volumes —for the state of the light.
At Waver ley (Syd.) the local captain having
won the toss, suddenly displayed the slimness of the ser-
pent, and sent his weak opponents in on a wicket
that still showed traces of the morning rain.
As anticipated, Central Cumberland struck disaster
at once. Newell gathered five of its wickets for 52,
Fuller one for 4, and lleele took the other four for 19
terminating the innings for a miserable 77. When stumps
were drawn Waverley had only eight wickets dow'n for
193, which means that, barring miracles, Waverlev’s
position at the head of the list next ■ Saturday is quite
safe. Neither does there seem much chance of Burwood
falling from the second place, which it shares with Glebe,
and the latter also seems fairly safe for its position in
double harness.
* « * •
In the Great Public Schools Premiership tussle
(Sydney), St. Joseph’s College passed on its own unholy
walloping of a week before to Scots’ College, which it
defeated on Saturday by an innings and 63 runs. The
Josephs didn’t make any centuries, the nearest approach
thereto being Brown’s 71. At Parramatta, Newington
College broke up King’s School by 155 on the first
innings, the highest score on the part of the victors being
Pawley’s 89. Sydney Grammar School had no hope against
the Church of England crowd, which has evidently gone
in for a very muscular brand of Christianity, and beat the
seculars by an innings and one run.
* * * *
There was a time when they flattered them-
selves they could play cricket in Canterbury (Maoriland),
but on Saturday the district team was beaten by the
Melbourne C.C. team, the margin being nine wickets.
There is something fatal about the number nine for the
Maoriland cricketers —Auckland, Manawatu, and Canter-
bury were all beaten by nine wickets. The Melbournians
beat Wanganui by an innings and 59 runs, and Wellington
just escaped the fatal nine wickets by two —but that was
probably an oversight, or else the Melbourne men got
bogged in the Basin Reserve.
♦ * * *
Apparently the Melbourne C.C. has sent all its
big guns to Maoriland The same day that the invading
team beat Canterbury by nine wickets another team from
the same club only made 55 against Richmond (Vic.).
W. J. M'LEOD, winner Prospect-Sale Road Race.
Still, Richmond had nothing' to get wildly elated over, for
although it won the matoh it only managed to make , 80.
Why this was so it is not easy to understand at this dis-
tance, for the weather was fine and cool, although a.
rather gusty wind may have interfered with the bats-
men.
* # # *
Clem Hill will be 29 years old on March 18.
His wife, who rejoiced to see him play cricket before she
married him, is stated to be anxious that he shall now
retire on his laurels. The influence of friends is, how-
ever, being exercised to persuade him to help his own
State and Australia generally for some time longer.
George Giffen (who will be 45 on March 27) would like to-
go on playing in first-class cricket till his last tooth falls
out, but the selectors are averse.
■* * * •
The latest row in cricketing circles is a hulla-
baloo over the action of the N.S.W. Cricket Association ini
returning to Victor Trumper accounts for £1 and 28s.
respectively with the request that he will get the people
who received the money to sign receipts for it. “The
players” are indignant, and talk of forming a,
rival association, or league, or something, which
will give the the players “justice.” The blessed,
players know the conditions under which they
draw expenses from the Association, and if they
are not prepared to abide by those conditions they should
not ask for expenses. It is quite a relief to learn that
there is at least one sporting body which does keep its
accounts on a business-like footing.
• * * *
Blake, the Victorian long distance runner, who-
goes to Greece to compete in the race from Marathon to
Athens, wilj thereby take a hand in the most classic event
on earth. It Was first run in an accidental sort of way
2396 years ago, when a breathless Greek covered the
distance in a time which wasn’t put on record, with the
news that the Persian army had been licked on the sea-
shore, and that a White Greece was still possible. If a.
White Australian wins the 23-mile event it should be a.
good omen. So far as is known no Persian has entered.
* * * *
The Half-Mile Swimming Championship of
N.S.W, was among the, problems set down for elucidation,
at the Watson’s Bay Club carnival (Sydney), Saturday,,
the preliminary heats having already been decided in the
windy suburb’s baths during the week. Six had qualified
for the final, the brothers G. and R. Healy, T. Tart-alcover,
R. R. Craig, L. McQueen and O. Dickman. It was a fore-
gone conclusion that it was easy for R. Healy, but the-
bunch was still with him at 200ydS., when the two
brothers went to the front and put up a desperate
struggle for several laps. Behind, Dickman and McQueen
swam neck and neck till 300yds. were covered, when
Craig retired. The feature of the race was a brilliant-
spurt made by McQueen at the finish, but Healy was too-
far ahead and too strong, and, although the clever Fort-
street boy made up a lot of-leeway, he had no hope of’
gaining even second place. R. Healy beat his brother by
22yds., and the brother left McQueen about'lsyds. further
behind. It was a race of great effort, and the fact that-
the winner took llmin. 59 4-ssec. to cover it as against
Kieran’s llmin. 11 3-ssec. proves what a marvellous;
swimmer Kieran was.
* * ■ * •*
Swimmer Cecil Healy left Sydney last Tuesday
by rail to overtake the R.M.S. Mongolia at Adelaide en
route for Athens, and, provided the money is forthcoming,
for England later on. The appeal of the Swimming
Association for funds to enable Healy to go to Athens was
not very well responded to., and but for the donation of
£5O by the Phriedphish Government the sprinter would
have had to rest content with Australian laurels. As it is
a further sum of £63 is required to enable him to' proceed
to England with a view to claiming the shoes of dis-
qualified champion Billington.
1 The girl swimmers of Sydney are now running
their carnivals absolutely “ on their own,” and the super-
fluous male has no voice in the matter. Out at Coogee,
or some other outlandish spot, last week they bungled
things sadly, and made the competitors swim some scores,
of yards more than necessary. Also, it is said said they
scrambled among themselves at the last minute, and that
the carnival was distracted by two sets of angry ladies
issuing diametrically opposite instructions. But the slur
on feminine management is understood to have been
wiped out by the smooth working of the Balmain Ladies’
Club, which went with the easy motion of one of Pinero s.
plays. Afternoon tea was handed out to the visitors, and
the Rechabite Band (!) discoursed sweet music. The girls,
swam well, and some of them showed a good turn of speed.
* * * *
Although the N.S.W. Lawn Tennis players
scored heavily against the Victorians on Friday and
Saturday last, winning 263 to Victoria’s 165 g'ames, it was
not due to any combination in their play. Fortunately
for the Cornstalks, the Victorians were even more dis-
jointed, and the contests were pure tests of individual
prowess wherein the Walers were, man for man, far
superior. Heath and Howitt were the only players Vic-
toria had. Heath is a player whom it is a pleasure to
watch, and Howitt has a greased lightning serve. It
consists of a drive of high velocity, in the making of
which the racquet is drawn across the ball in a manner that
makes it slither along the surface of the court without
rising. The receiver is driven right back to the edge of
the horizon to take it, and then the annoying Howitt
just screws it over the top of the net. In any case it is a
heart-breaker, for if the receiver does catch it just
as it strikes the ground he only manages to knock it
half-way between glory and Kingdom Come.
» * * *
Both Manly and St. George are wildly anxious
to have first grade Rugby football clubs of their own, but
the Union is not at all enthusiastic on the matter. Re-
membering what happened to the Eastern Suburbs when
Sydney District Club was created, many members are
advocating a reduction of the number to eight instead of
an increase to ten as proposed. However, a committee is
to be appointed to thresh the whole subject out and
report to the council, and general opinion has it that
there is a slight majority in favor of the increase.
\ * * *
The paltering of Attorney-General Wade over
the bowlers’ final drink exasperates some of the clubs.
His latest ukase is that, as bowling is a daylight business,
it will break the law for a club to serve drinks after dark.
“ Bosh 1 ” exclaimed one club man, “ we’ll lay on the
electric light.”
* * *
Maorilander Jim Griffin, ex-amateur champion
of the continent, made his second appearance in the pro-
fessional ring last Tuesday week night at Sydney
National Sporting Club. He’s a hard hitter and a pretty
fighter—as long as he is allowed to stand off and come in
and back out as he likes, but when the other fellow
stands in close and bangs heavily about the body, Griffin
loses his head and wastes his strength in wild swipes that
would seriously damage an elephant—and break Griffin s
arm and fist—if they landed. Another weak point in his
fighting is his tendency to chop dow n on to his man, and
some of Australia’s professional pugs have frightfully
thick skulls, whereby James Griffin stands to get badly
damaged about his smiting machinery. On the Tuesday
night in question he found himself up against Sydney
Russell, and although the husky young blacksmith
doesn’t pretend to be in the front rank, he
had the ex-amateur champion perilously near being
beaten. In fact, had Russell stood in close right
through, instead of trying to play the elusive shadow and
always keep just out of reach, the course of history might
have been altered. The first round Griffin attacked, and ,
his blows were clean and crisp, and a pleasant, meaty
sound emanated from Russell when they landed. That
full, rich sound tells that the blows are of the sort that
count. Then Russell, back on the ropes, stood in close
and rained blows about the body, but the Maorilander
stood off and shot a series of pretty lefts in the stomach
and on the neck, varying any' approach to monotony
bv a neat chop on the ear with the right. The
second opened by Russell stopping Griffin’s left
with his nose. With a snort of pain he bored in
close and whacked his right twice on the Maorilander s
body' before the latter could jump away. But like a flash
he came back and raised a lump over Russell’s eye, spoilt
the outline of his mouth, and was inflicting other damage
when Russell fell on his commissariat quarter with both
hands and great fury. Griffin sprang away and whirled
:his fists like a propeller out of water ; in fact, he whirled
them so fast that‘ Russell was temporarily' deprived of
light, and groped about in a species of gloaming, bashing
at his enemy’s stopiach, till the Maorilander went down
crash. The uproar and excitement were tremendous,
ibut the agile Maori lander sprang suddenly' to his feet, and
Russell lumbered about trying to finish him in grampus-
like fashion. Writer doesn’t know what a grampus is,
but belifeves. it looks as Russell did trying to finish
Griffin. The Maorilander was saved by the gong. He
came up wonderfully recuperated, and in the wild work
that followedone blow caughtßussellacrossthe back of the
head, and must have nearly' stove it in. But perhaps
it isn’t hollow ! Still, Griffin’s stock was sinking, and
men who had laid 6 to if against Russell were now offering
even money ,bn him. He had proved that if he kept to
in-fighting he had more than Buckley’s chance. On the
other hand, his condition did not seem so good as
Griffin’s. The fourth round saw Russell suddenly grow
weak. That wild blow across the back of the head was
probably beginning to tell, and in the middle of a strenu-
ous rally Griffin managed to get off at arm’s length and
land a whole shower of blows which sent Russell to the
floor. The fifth found him a beaten man. He was
obviously' afraid of the Maorilander, and apparently ex-
hausted. He hung on at times and tried to keep out of
reach at other times, and when Griffin cornered him and
'let fly a whole book of Euclid about his thinking-box he
lay down —licked. Referee Beckett drew a veil over the
sad scene by declaring Griffin winner. All the same,
Griffin has a lot to learn yet.
* * * *
Sydney Gaiety Athletic Club, which has been
running a 9st. “tournament,” brought the thing to a
lurid finish on Saturday night, when the two survivors,
Murray and Doyle, met in the final. Neither regarded
the other as a sticker, and each waded in to knock his
foe out in short order. Result: Five rounds of sensa-
tional slogging, warm enough to satisfy' the most hardened
bruiser. Then both parties to the disturbance suddenly
developed a sincere respect for the other’s hitting power,
and grew cautious. Both were very tired, and Murray
seemed quite exhausted, so when the ninth round sav
him prone three times, Doyle was declared conqueror.
* * * »
He was littie and shrivelled, old and sardonic,
and he stood on, the platform of the Watson’s Ba,y baths;
watching the Healy brothers plunging through the half-
mile championship. “ Very fast, very fast,” he muttered,
•expectorating with great accuracy* “ but, good Heavens,
there’s no style about this new-fangled way o’ swimmin’.
In my day, s\yimmin’ was a graceful sport, but this here
■dog-paddle sort o’, stroke is the ugliest thing I’ve ever
seen.”. And, without a doubt, the modern stroke is any-
thing but graceful.

Hatting Your Head.
EVERYONE wants their head hatted. If they’ve
the right kind of head they’ll want the right
kind of hat. A hat that will wear well, fit
well, and look well as long as worn, ought to
be good enough.
The Austral Hat Mills
at ABBORTSFORD, VICTORIA,
are turning out just such hats. They’re made of the
best winter rabbit fur—no shoddy about them. This
fur is first sterilized, and then goes through every
process that modern Hat-making knows of. You can
get AUSTRAL HATS from most leading Hatters,
Outfitters, Mercers and Storekeepers throughout the
Commonwealth. Every high-grade hat of their
making has their name branded inside it—look for it.
If you can’t get them in -your district write to the
Austral Hat Mills direct, and they’ll arrange with
your local Storekeeper to stock them. You might as
well mention his name when waiting. Remember,
Austral Hats are Australian all through. You’ll find
them better than the foreign shoddy dumped to the
tune of £200,000 a year on to this tired Continent.
They are obtainable in either stiff or soft Felts—any
style, any shape you like—and very light and com-
fortable to the head. Mind you ask your dealer for
an “ AUSTRAL ” Hat—and get what you ask for.

TO CONNOISSEURS OF ART AND THE
GENERAL PUBLIC.
Attractive and Interesting Sale
by Public Auction, at the
FINE ART GALLERIES
OF
Walter Bradley & Co.,
324 George Street.
The Percy Spence Collection
of Oil Paintings, Water
Colors, and Original
Character Sketches .
The collection embraces some of this accomplished
Artist’s best London work, including' several Royal
Academy Exhibits, Autograph Character Sketches of
Prominent Statesmen, including Lord Selborne and Mr.
Chamberlain, Charming examples of Water Color Paint-
ing, Oil Painting of Bush Life, The Boundary Rider, and
other subjects typical of Australia. Portraits of Promin-
ent Australians, including the late Sir George Dibbs in his
workshop.
WALTER BRADLEY AND CO.
have been favored with instructions from Mr.
PERCY F. S. SPENCE, who is returning to London, to
conduct the above sale.
Catalogues ready early next week. Application for
same will have prompt attention.
Mr. Spence has been in Sydney for the past twelve
months’ recuperating his health after seven years’ active
work in the Great Metropolis, and is now returning to
carry out some most important commissions. Arriving
in England in 1898 he was at once at work illustrating a
story for The Gentlewoman, and then spent two years
working for the Graphic, Harper’s Magazine, Pall Mall,
and other leading journals. Then two years were spent
with The Sphere, during which period Mr. Spence was
entrusted with many important commissions, spending
three weeks on H.M.S. Trafalgar at the Naval Manoeuvres.
He attended the Coronation of King Edward in West-
minster Abbey for The Sphere, and was sent to draw His
Majesty at Homburg. During his engagement with the
Illustrated London News Mr. Spence was commissioned
to draw many great functions, including the Reception of
President Lo'ubet at the Guild Hall, and also the King of
Italy. He was the only artist permitted to sketch at the
War Office Inquiry, and he had the honor of a special
setting from H.R.H. the Prince of Wales at York House.
As a painter, Mr Spence scored a number of successes,
having exhibited four years at the Royal Academy, was
twice placed on the line, and has exhibited in Water
Colors at the Gonnil Gallery.

At Albury (N.S.W.) a man, recently con-
victed of false pretences, had shown against him
an extraordinary list of convictions. The
Victorian police records show that he started
in 1865 at Bendigo, and practically the whole
41 years since then he has spent in gaol. The sen-
tences varied from nineyears down to one year, and
totalled 61 \ years. They were all for forgery and
uttering. A man who forged and uttered so badly
that he was always falling in like this must have
been a very dull person not to realise that
he was a hopelessly poor forger, and an utterer of
no account at all. Most men would have regarded
611s years’ gaol as a hint, and tried some other
walk of crime.
The predicament of a lawyer before
N.S.W. Supreme Court last week was unusual. A
question of liability under the Moore-street
Improvement Act was being heard, when plaintiffs
counsel suddenly realised that the point at issue
had been decided against him by the same court
a year or two ago. He nobly saved the Law from
any chance of making an exhibition of itself, by
asking that his own case should be dismissed, with
costs against his client. The court dismissed it
promptly, but it would have been more to the
client’s satisfaction if the lawyer had made his dis-
covery earlier.
A Miss Sloan, who writes patriotic songs,
sent samples of them to the Duke of Abercorn.
The lady received the following reply from his
Grace :—
Madame, —I am requested by the Duke of Abercorn to
say that he is much obliged to you for sending him copies
of your songs, hut he feels so aghast at the result of the
present election that he is unable to take any interest in
patriotic songs. I therefore beg to return them with
thanks. —Yours sincerely, Hamilton, secretary.
Another case of the blessed country being lost.
George Reid on the proposal bo nationalise
the Tobacco Trust:—
I have just as strong an objection as any Socialist to
monopolies where they are proved to be unfair to the
public. The difference between the Socialist and myself
is this My remedy would be by dealing with the mono-
polist, so as to enable the currents of competition to
resume their normal strength and freedom.
It is a neat little platitude, but there is no infor-
mation in it. Ereetrade wouldn’t “enable the
currents of competition,” &c., for the tobacco
octopus spreads pretty well over the earth ;
besides George never attempted Freetrade in
tobacco —he always went in for heavy Protection
as regards that industry. And what other remedy
would he propose? Apparently, only a faint
wobbling of vague remarks, and a loose wandering
of polysyllables over the face of the earth.

1 Land For Sale.
TASMAKTIA.
North West Coast.
Land to fbo Lot or Sold
THE VAN DIEMEN’S LAND COMPANY are now sub-
dividing their Hampshire Hills and Surrey Hills
Estates into sections of from 100 acres upwards, which
they offer ON LEASE at low rents or FOR SALE on very
advantageous terms extending over 14 years. These
estates, which are well served by -the Emu Bay Railway
from Burnie to Mount Bischoff, Zeehan, and the West
Coast mining field, consist principally of rich basaltic
soil abundantly watered, and are well suited for dairying
and stock raising. The Railway, which passes entirely
through the estates, connects the land to g'ood markets
on thfe North West Coast and Mainland and on the West
Coast mining field. Other portions of the Company’s
estates are also For Sale and To Let on Lease. Full par-
ticulars will be supplied on application to —
A. K. McGAW,
Manager.
Burnie, Tasmania, 23rd February, 1906.

The Company's Consulting Actuary, Mr. R. P. HARDY,
F./.A., F.F.A., in his Report on the 1904 Valua-
tion, says:—
M The Directors are fully entitled to claim that the Security
they offer, both to the present Assured and to the Public
generally, for the redemption of their contracts is of the
highest possible character.
* They should use every means to educate the Public to
appreciate, not merely the great protection afforded by esti-
mating the Liabilities upon the basis of only a 3 per cent,
yield, but, further, that such prudential course has the
additional advantage of providing a Bonus fund that will grow
as the Company grows in age, and will enable compensation
to be made to those longer livers whose contributions will
have helped to build up the Company.”

THE CITIZENS’ EXPENSE RATE is lower than that of any 1 dustrial
Life Assurance Company in the World, save one—The Prudential of
England, which is fifty-seven years old. The total amount of expenses
was £20,948 less in 1905 than in 1902, although the revenue was
£68,443 greater.
THE MORTALITY EXPERIENCE of the Company lias been light
throughout its whole career.
THE RATE OF INTEREST earned by the Funds is steadily increasing
year by year.
A SURPLUS OF £50,645 (exclusive of £20,572 written off securities) in
the Ordinary Branch alone, was revealed at the end of 1905, upon a
valuation basis of Hm 3 per cent., which makes the reserves of the
branch unequalled in Australasia.
OVER ONE THIRD of the Total Assets are held in Government and
Municipal Debentures—Liquid Securities.
ON £1,500,000 OF INVESTMENTS at the close of 1905, there was not
One Penny of Interest outstanding.
OVER 42 PER CENT, of the Total Income last year was added to the
Funds.
FOR EIGHT YEARS IN SUCCESSION the New Assurances in the
Ordinary Branch alone have totalled over One Million Sterling,
some: pre:ss opinions
“Sydney Morning Her aid,” 20 2’06
Altogether the report and balance-sheet are most satisfactory. There
is not only evidence of capable management, but there is a patent desire
to have expenses kept down to a minimum. The cautious character of the
management is clearly indicated by the fact that the company has no out-
standing interest due, no foreclosed properties in its hands, no mortgaged
properties in its possession, and this despite the fact that it has £587,964
loaned out on mortgage. It also has £515,939 invested in Consols, Aus-
tralian Government and municipal securities.
The Melbourne “Age,” 2212/’O6 :
The Company appears to be progressing in the right direction at a
s itisfactory rate. ”
The “Daily Telegraph,” 1/31*06
We have received from the Citizens’ Life Assurance Company its record
of seven years’ progress, which has certainly been highly encouraging.

“ H ” : After tea went on the St. Kilda
beach in the gloaming. An ever-increasing crowd
also gloamed. I became popular at once, ihe
girls were particularly friendly. Several said,
“ Good-night, Jack ! ” quite nicely. Sometimes
they called me Billy. One sweet thing mvited
me to take a stroll. The crimson blushes of my
frank, open countenance were painful when a
fairy beach-comber called me Ducky .
waddled off and sat on a vacant seat to recover.
Three well-dressed, light-hearted virgins sat beside
me. They grew quite at home with me. I cou a
smell violets. It was ravishing. How nice and
friendly the Melbourne girls are ! They were
nice. Suddenly a vision of loveliness threw her-
self into my arms, and then they all rail away
laughing. 1 got quite a thrill. I had no idea the
beach was so friendly. I thrilled home. In my
room I liad more thrills. My watch and chain
and a sovereign had vanished. Thirteen guineas
worth of gloaming ! Blow the gloaming . Blow
afternoons off ! Blow the girls ! Blow every
thing ! !
Hop beer affects some people in curious
wavs. One individual in South Canterbury
(M“L.) has a mania for stopping runaway horses
that aren’t running' away. His first circus hap-
pened without any previous advt., when he rushed
out and yelled “ Whoa, whoa !” in an excited tone
to a moke that was dead asleep, and couldn t be
any more “whoa” than it was alreadythis
didn’t cause much comment, and it wasn t until
he had stopped four other similar runaways that
the passing public manifested any interest in the
proceedings. His next victim was a farmer who
was driving down the street full tilt in a gig. this
time the eccentric person was spilled all over the
road, and there was much crimson language and
orders for a doctor; but the man merely got up,
shook himself, and strolled off, saying, ‘ Well, I
stopped that cove, anyhow.” Within a few
minutes he tackled a perfectly innocent moke that
was discussing a nose-bag, but the owner was
ready for him, and after a violent set-to the
Stopper was secured and tied up m the cowsm'd.
“ Tanjil ” from Mauritius :
Am an Australian. Have spent some time in Mauritius,
also in Kata], also in Madagascar. Have seen much of
colored races in those countries. Feel certain Australia
is doing right to exclude color. Port Louis is now a
stinkpot—too full of plague and Indian filth for Euro-
peans to live in. Not one residential hotel there. Indian
shopkeepers, clerks, etc., have elbowed out the European.
Met one intelligent white youth; he had left school a
year, and could not get office employment of any
kind; pleaded with me to take him off the island. The
cheap-living- Indian youth had supplanted him. iNatal is
drifting into the same state as Mauritius. Durban is
full of Indians. Many own land in the pnucipal streets.
A good many businesses and occupations are monopolised
bv them, indian youths are educated by the State ;
result is many solicitors and others are employing them
as clerks to exclusion of the European. To-day s Mercury
publishes a complaint by “ Tailor ” that the big European
tailoring shops are letting out work on contract to the
cheap coolie. Same rates.to whites would mean starva-
tion. In a few years the probability is that Indians will
very greatly outnumber the whites. Then Mauritius
over again. [“ Tanjil ” makes an error here; Indians
already outnumber whites in Natal.] My experience of
black 'countries is that the white by contact becomes
deteriorated; the energetic become less energetic, the
lazy become lazier, the vicious become more vicious.
Know an Australian in Madagascar who lives, or rather
pigs it, with a Malagasy woman. She supplies the
“shalcoff” that keeps his miserable body and soul
together. Pray God Australia will continue to keep far
from the smellful colored man and his abominable filth,
diseases, and vices. Yet Bruce Smith, Pulsford and Co.-
limited let us hope—want the country opened to the
“gentleman” of color.

‘"Kerani”: Witness the rush of the
squatter to grab roads and reserves before local
government arrives in N.S. Wales. Much of the
(government Gazette at present is occupied by
notices to the effect that “applications have been
received for the closing and purchase of roads
separating portions 2A and 48, and portions 2B
a,K J 4A, parish of Pyaka, county of Timbuctoo,”
and that “unless valid objections are lodged”
within a given date the application will be
granted. This highly illuminating statement in
the widely-read Gazette, with sometimes a notice
to a similar effect in the local paper, is all the
warning the general public gets that its rights and
privileges are being bartered away by a short-
sighted Administration.

LONDON SECRETARY : E. HABBEN, Palmerston House, Broad-street, London, E.C.
This Company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring from THE HAMPDEN COPPER MINES, No Lia-
bility, whose Registered Office is at 31 Queen-street, Melbourne, Mineral Leases Nos. 244 and 245, in the Oloncurry
Mineral Field, North Queensland, comprising 40 acres, and including the whole of the 40 chains along the line of
lode held under the original Reward Claim, and situated about 47 miles from Cloncurry. An additional 200 acres
has been applied for on behalf of this Company, making 240 acres in all.
Herewith is a report on the mine furnished to the shareholders of The Hampden Copper Mines, No Liability, by
Mr. F. Stanley Low, 8.0. E. (now General Manager of the Broken Hill Proprietary Block 10 Company, Limited), when
he was manager of The Hampden Copper Mines, N.L.
It will be observed that Mr. Low estimated the qnantity of ore in the mine (exclusive of pyritic bodies below
water level, and the ore from the No. 1 cut northwards) at 116,000 tons, of an average value of 15 per cent. Copper,
or a total gross value of £1,260,000.
Mr. A. E. Savage, who in September last inspected the Hampden Mine and reported under date February 2, 1906,
says in regard to the estimated quantity of Copper ore disclosed in the mine above water level
“. . . the kaolinised part of the lode will measure about 600 yards long by 40 yards wide, and 17
yards deep, containing a total of 408,000 cubic yards of ore-bearing ground. This at 9cwt. per
cubic yard will yield 183,600 tons of 11 per cent, ore, containing 20,000 tons of copper, allowing for dis-
carding some of the poorer ore to make tip the difference in grade ; this practically confirms Mr. Low’s
estimate of 116,000 tons of 15 per cent, ore, containing 18,560 tons of copper.”
The richness of the higher grade ore is exemplified by the fact that the vendor company despatched 538 tons of
ore to the Wallaroo Smelting-Works, which averaged 36 per cent, of copper, and realised £10,561, after deduction
of smelting charges.
It will also be seen from Mr. Savage’s report that there are 8227 tons of lower grade ore already stacked on the
mine.
The richness and great extent of the copper deposits in the Cloncurry district have been known for many years,
but active development and commercial treatment of the ore was not feasible until direct railway communication
with the seaboard was assured.
The Queensland Government recently authorised an extension of 90 miles of the Townsville railway from Rich-
mond to Eddington, towards Cloncurry, and an assurance has been given that the line will be extended without un-
necessary delay for the remaining 81 miles to Cloncurry.
Connection with Cloncurry, which will be the terminus of the Government railway, can be secured by an exten-
sion of railway south for about 47 miles, over a route of an easy grade, and presenting no engineering difficulties. A
company is now' being formed having as one of its objects the carrying out of this work of extension if the necessary
authority from the Queensland Parliament is obtained, application for which has already been made. With this
accomplished, the hindrance to progressive mining will disappear, and a bright future for this hitherto neglected dis-
trict may be confidently anticipated.
REPORT ON THE HAMPDEN MINE, by Mr. A. E. SAVAGE, formerly Manager of the Sulphide Corporation,
Limited, Smelting Works at Cockle Creek, and now Resident Manager of the Australian Smelting Corporation, Limited.
THE HON. W. L. BAILLIEU, M.L.C., Collins-street, Melbourne. Elsternw'ick, February 2, 1906.
Dear Sir. —I have the honor to present the following report on the Hampden Mine, Cloncurry, Queensland :
SITUATION.—The mine is situated about 47 miles by road, or 38 miles in a straight line nearly due south of Clon-
curry, the nearest town. A town site has been surveyed on Crown lands about half a mile south-east from the mine,
and is now partly occupied.
LEASES.—The property originally consisted of two leases, each 20 chains square, enclosing 80 acres in all, and 40
chains along the lode. In order to reduce rent and number of men required to fulfil labor conditions, these leases
were recently cut down to 20 acres each, by relinquishing a strip on each side, parallel to the lode, thus halving the
area held without sacrificing any ground likely to be of value, as the lode stands almost vertical. Their numbers are
Southern lease, formerly ML37, now ML244 ; Northern lease, formerly ML3B, now ML245. lam advised that additiona
area has been applied for on behalf of the company,
TOPOGRAPHY. —The surrounding country consists of flats or undulating ground, traversed by ranges of low
hills, usually running north and south, though over a small area to the westward they have an east and west trend
The country generally is favorable to the formation of tramways or roads, free from steep grades or long detours for
the carriage of timber, firewood and fluxes, or ore from neighboring mines for treatment at a central smelting works
TIMBER. —The flats and lower hill-slopes are well grassed and lightly timbered with gidgea and several varieties
of small gum suitable for firewood and small mining timbers, while along the rivers and larger creeks gums and ti
tree grow of sufficient size to furnish large timber suitable for structural purposes in any quantity likely to be requires
for the building of works.
WATER SUPPLY. —The average rainfall of the district is from 25 to 30 inches per year, though, in common with
other parts of the interior, droughts are not infrequent. Nearly all this falls during the wet season, from January to
March inclusive, so that it will be necessary to rely on conservation for water supply during- the greater part of the
year. Natural conservation occurs in drift forming alluvial flats near watercourses, from which a supply can be go;
by sinking wells, and in depressions, either open or filled w'ith drift, in the river beds. The former often yield a large
quantity of good quality ; and as a well on the bank of the creek near the mine has afforded an ample supply for loca
domestic and stock purposes throughout some very dry seasons, the practicability of obtaining sufficient water at leas
for domestic and mine steaming purposes by sinking further wells should be tested before undertaking a more expen
sive scheme. With regard to the second form of supply, only the largest holes survive a dry season, but in the drift
water is practically permanent and often in large quantity.
The nearest considerable supply of this sort is at a gorge from 6 to 8 miles distant South-west on the Cion
curry River, at a place where it has cut a narrow channel through a rocky ridge. For some distance above am
below this ridge the river bed is very wide and filled with drift, though formerly a large open hole existed jusi
below the bar. By constructing a concrete or wooden dam on this bar up to the level of the surface of the drift
the natural conservation could be largely increased, while the effect of checking the movement of drift down the
river, combined with the fall of water over the dam, would l;e to more or less completely re-open the large hole
below, and thus further add to the water conserved. This hole is regarded as one of the most reliable sources of
water supply in the district, never having failed in the longest drought, though considerable demands have been
made on it for watering stock. By sinking a well on the bank and driving into the river bed, the water con-
tained in the drift can be brought within reach of pumps for delivery through a pipe line to the Mine or smel-
ters. The track between the two points traverses flats favqrable for a pipe line, with a maximum difference of
level probably not exceeding 100 feet. A further source of supply is the Mine itself, from which a certain quan-
tity of water suitable for furnace purposes, if not boilers, will be obtained. The quantity is at present small,
but with several shafts open and deeper sinking will increase. Generally, the neighborhood of the Mine is not
favorable to artificial conservation in dams, the natural depressions being too shallow to permit of a sufficient
.depth of water being retained to withstand the high evaporation due to the hot, dry climate.
ELEVATIONS. —The Mine lies about 1200 feet above sea level, while, speaking from memory, Cloncurry is;
about (jflb feet. -
GEOLOGY. —Geologically, the country consists of almostVyertical beds of Silurian age, having a general
North and .South strike, and consisting, near Hampden Mine, of slates and quartzites, in which occurrences of
diorite and trtrp rock more or less comformable with their bedding are common. The lode is of the bedded type,,
consisting of mineWJigpd slate up to 200 feet in width, forming the central part of a much wider belt of the
same rock enclosed betVetm diorite on the west and quartzite on the east. It stands almost vertical, and strikes;
about 20 degrees East of North.
OUTCROP. For about half a mile South of the Hampden Leases the position of the lode is marked by a low
ridge consisting.mainly of indurated slate, with occasional patches of quartzose rock, carrying outcrops of jasper
and ironstone, showing more or less copper but no ore bodies of any size. This ridge rises gradually Northw'ard cul-
minatino- m a prominent outcrop of quartz and gossan on ML 15, about 50 yards South of the South boundary of the
Hampden Leases, then falling rather abruptly on to flatter ground, which slopes Northward for about 80 yards within.
Lease 244. Here it joins a low flat, extending a further 120 yards to the foot of a mass of quartzite which rises boldly
to a capping of quartz and gossan about 80 yards further on. From this capping the surface falls rather abruptly to
the West but more gradually to East and North, joining flat ground just beyond the boundary of Lease 244. Within
Lease 245 the surface is low in the Southern part, rising slightly to the North, where the surface is a little more
broken, but on the whole the surface of this lease is devoid of prominent features. Though the richest outcrops have
been removed by open cutting, numerous occurrences of carbonate and oxide of copper can still be seen chiefly
associated with small quartz and gossan outcrops, which occur on all but the lowest parts of the surface, where a.
thin deposit of alluvium hides the original surface. Northward beyond Lease 245, diorite seenis to cut off the
lode formation, though in places small seams of ore can be seen. No signs of faults dislocating the lode were-
observed.
CHARACTER OF ORE CONTENTS.—Both above and below water level the ores are essentially copper ores,,
the small quantity of gold and silver contained being not worth the cost of separation from the copper, while metals-
deleterious to the quality of the copper, such as lead, antimony, and bismuth, seem to be absent, also zinc in any
appreciable quantity.
ORE DEPOSITS. —Above water level the richesst portions of the lode underlie the flats, and consist of a com-
paratively soft kaolinised slate, permeated in all directions by seams of cuprite and glance of great richness, measur-
ing- from a fraction of an inch up to in some oases several feet in thickness. From the central part of these seams,
which is often almost pure cuprite or glance, the percentage of copper falls as the ore merges into the inclosing
kaolin, the centre of the richest seams forming the first grade ore, while the outer parts and the poorer seams form
the lower grades. There seems to be no order or method in the occurrence of these ore bands ; they are not usually
continuous for any great length, and only occasionally outcrop, while they do not seem to be confined to particular
areas or zones, but may be found more or less closely distributed through any part of the kaolinised slate. This has;
been proved by shafts to extend downward from 40 to 60 feet, while on the surface it is in places 200 feet wide, and
seldom less than 100 feet. Excluding the elevated Northern portion of Lease 244, about 600 yards of the lode within
the property consists of this formation. In the elevated silicious portions of the outcrop the ore is comparatively
low grade, consisting of oxide and carbonate of copper, associated with seams of gossan a,nd as impregnations of'
quartzite, but the quantity so far opened up is small and unimportant. In the following estimate this portion of the-
lode is regarded as blank. In the absence of systematic blocking out no ore can be said to be in sight, but the fol-
lowing estimate of probable ore is submitted, based on results from open cutting under Mr. Low’s management. The-
result of later work is not included, because this has been distributed over a number of small openings of which no-
record of yardage excavated is available.
Front Nos. I and 2 open cuts 3500 cubic yards were excavated for the following yield of ore

penlive process such as that used in the Tharsis Mine in Spain for the extraction of copper
Buried under one part of the dump lay a quantity of marcasite in masses which must have come from a
body of some size Superficially these had decomposed and showed copper freely in oxidised forms; internally they
consisted Tmarcasite with more or less chalcopyitte disseminated through it. It was only possible to reach a few
of these lumps, and no representative samples could be got. I have, therefore to rely on past record for an estimate
of its copper contents The Company’s books show a number of assays of this material, ranging from sto 20 per
cent of Copper Discarding the higher results as due to enrichment with secondary ore, I estimate the average con-
tents of theunaltered sulphide at about 7'5 per cent, copper. In view of the nature and great extent oflode
horizontally the uncertainty as to whether this ore occurs in seams or lenses, and the small amount of de\ elopment
work done on it, a forecast of the probable quantity is impossible, but as the rich ore in the upper levels must have
been derived from the decomposition of these sulphides, it is certain that pyntic bodies of some sort underlie^ all the

h Sir —1 have the honor to submit to you the following report on the Hampden Mine. The Hampden Mine, con-
istino-of three blocks of 80 acres each, is situated 47 miles south of Cloncurry, and about 50 miles west of Mackinlay.
inspection of the Mine Plans made by Mr. Thos. Fraser, C.E., will show that the copper-bearing belt is one of slate
vine in diorite country. This slate belt is 550 feet wide at the southern boundary of Block 37, and spreads out to a
vidth of 1000 feet at the north of Block 38. Within the slate area occur well defined outcrops of copper-bearing
Material showing a width between in many places of 200 feet. So far, prospecting work has been confined to ground
ving between these outcrops, and in all calculations these have been taken as the bounds of the ore body. There is,
lowever no reason why the ore should not, on further exploration, be found to extend through the whole slate belt.
The Sections have been prospected and worked by means of 24 main trenches, 12 shafts, and 3 open cuts, as
veil as of many smaller trenches and minor shafts. At No. 6 Shaft, situated at No. 1 Trench in the northern part
>f Block 38, a short grade has been made with a view to future open-cutting. No. 6 Shaft is sunk m ore to a dept i
if 20 feet and the crosscuts in the bottom, as far as they have been driven, show the solid ore body to be 30 feet
vide. The grade to meet this ore body has, so far, been made only for a length of about 56 feet, and has a maximum
lepth of 7 feet; nevertheless, from this small excavation, 7 tons of 40 per cent., _S tons of IS per cent., and 3 tons
if 6i per cent, ore have been taken. High grade ore has been left underfoot for the whole length of the g> ade.
Crenelles Nos. 23, 2, 22 and 21 show ore close to the surface, having a large and varying width. No. 7 Shaft, sunk to
l depth of 544 feet, shows a large body of ore. From this shaft, in the sinking, have been taken 12 tons of 40 per
sent?and 72 tons of 13 per cent. ore. ‘Large bodies of ore have also bee "
shaft. No. 12 Shaft during the past few months has been carried down to a depth of 110 feet bl feet below water
eve]. Very important results have been obtained from the sinking of this shaft. At a depth of 50 feet the slateis
‘ontainino- Oarbonate of Copper gave an assay value of 9-4 per cent, of Copper. At .i 9 feet pyrites associated with
mssan was struck and gave an assay of 6‘5 per cent. This continued down to a depth of 72 feet, where the pyrites
issaved 27’4 per cent. ; two feet lower a vein of copper glance gave the extraordinary high value of /3'7 per cent, of
lopper. At7si feet the pyrites assayed 22-4 per cent., which fell to 21 per cent, at 824 feet. At 97 feet the copper
•ontents were 6’2 per cent., nt 107 feet the value w&s 4.2 per cent. . ~ n
The discovery P f the pyritic body is of great importance to this Mine, and, in fact, to the district generally.
The existence of payable ore bodies well below water level has now been proved, and although of lower value tha,n
;he verv rich deposits of oxide and carbonates near the surface, they may be expected to extend to great deaths. It
nust also be borne in mind that the advanced methods of smelting now in vogue make the treatment of pyntic ores
1111 Proceeding southwards, No. 2 Open Cut is reached. This cut, which had but little outrop to indicate the hidden
veaßh beneath, has now reached a length of over 200 feet and a depth of about IS feet. From this excavation have
ieen taken 20C tons of 40 per cent., 364 tons of 13 per cent., and 11S4 tons of 6J per cent, ore, and throughout the
vhole length of the cut ore of high grade has been left going strongly underfoot. The face of the cut is now within
i few feet of No. 1 Shaft, and the splendid outcrops of high grade ore here displayed. The outcrop of the ore body
.nr. now he traced through No 8 Trench into No. 37 Block till it bursts out into a large and solid outciop at No. 2
fhaft. At the foot and to the skuth of this hold outcrop is No. 1 Cut. No. 12 Trench and No. 3 Shaft, with its under-
ips! hflH shown that a lar°-e bodv of verv high grade ore was situated about this spot. An excavation vai,> mg- from

ffialcocite about a foot in width is now supplying ore in this cut of a value of .5-. per cent of copper. lioni N . 1

Nm 4 Shaft bodiesof Wgh grade ore were encountered, and 13 tons of 40 per cent., 28 tons of 13 per cent., and 197
tons at 6J per cent, ore were classified and stacked. No. 5 Shaft was sunk in ore to a depth of 45 feet. Assays made

From what has been put forth, and with the assistance of the Mine p.ans, it willl be seen that the ore bodies can
be traced from within a chain of the Northern boundary of Block 38, through Block 3/, a distance of about 39 chains
through the Hampden Company’s property. In the absence of railway communication and consequent high rates
of freight—rates which have been more than trebled in many cases on account of the severity of the drought it has
been fmind expedient to work only such parts of the deposit as will give payable quantities of very high
within easy reach of the surface. Only such ore as will bulk 40 per cent, and over has been sought, but in excavating
this high grade material the lower grade ore has often to be taken with it. This, in turn, is classified into second
a-rade of 13 per cent., and third grade of 64 per cent. Very great care is exercised that a proper sample is taken of
every truckwhich leaves the Open Cuts. The marvellous richness of the deposits can best be demonstrated by the
resets gffined in the short timeduring which the Mine has been worked. With only enough men to fulfil the con-
dtuonslf the Mining Act, and notwithstanding the severity of the drought and other deterrent circumstances, ore
to the value of £45,957 wks raised and stacked on the Mine up to 15th October, 1900. Most of this ore was raised

1900, the following additional amounts of ore have been raise d, viz-- 82 tons First, 3t5 tons Second, and 514 tons Third
Grade rnakin" a total of 625 tons First, 2691 tons Second, and 2276 tons Third Giade. . . .

CURED
Said the Horse-Planter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT makes me smile to see all these young
follows running about just now looking for
graft. When they do get it, what is it?
Thirty bob a v/eek and find themselves. Why, I
can make three times that ar.y night when I’m at
work. And no bullocking, either.
Of course, my trade’s not much use except
where there’s plenty of rich people. Farmers are
no good to me; they won’t give any reward for
a missing horse. Ask them a quid to find one,
and they fall down. Besides, their old crocks
aren’t much use to sell again, if they don’t offer a
reward.
Dealers are the same ; all they ever do is to put
the police after me. A fat lot of good that does,
even when I haven’t got them squared. The
average trap knows as much about brands as I do
of running a newspaper.
The flash carriage and sulky horses —that’s where
I make my profit. And family cows. The people
that own these have plenty of cash ; when a 30-
guinea horse or £TS cow gets lost, what’s a couple
of quid for finding them ? Nothing at all; and it
only means me putting them away in a safe place
for a few days. Besides, the spell does them good.
I keep a lot of hands going. Live at Parra-
matta myself, on the Western road. I find it the
most central place for business. I can see what
stock goes up and down nicely from there. It is
handy to all my planting paddocks, too. I have
a ring of these ; the first one is at Bankstown, the
next at Smithfield, the next at St. Mary’s. Then
one at Pitt Town, then at Ryde. Lately, I have
put another on at Hornsby, to cope with the busi-
ness from the Milson’s Point line. A terrible lot
of toffs are going up that way now.
These paddocks take in about all the country
worth working ; they are small and scrubby, and
poked back well out of the way. I keep a man to
look after each. Anything he picks up in his
district he shifts round into the next one. If the
owner makes much of a fuss, that particular
animal is shifted round a couple more paddocks
to be safe. There it stops till I get my reward
for “ finding ” it.
How do I pick them up? Very simple ! Each
man works his own district. Anything likely he
either collects straying about, or else persuades
out of the paddock when no one’s looking. Rides
about every day he’s not busy, looking for busi-
ness ; always carries a couple of halters with him,
besides a good heeler running behind. Some
horses are terrors to pul] back without a good
cattle dog to nip them up. Cows are generally
lifted at night; those fat, old family things travel
so slowly.
I always attend to the bazaars and Camperdown
myself, note the buyer’s address when anything
choice is sold, and follow it up later. Advertise
when things are a bit slack ; find out good sorts
that way. You can quieten any horse in a stable
with a “twitch,” and make him lead anywhere
with a half-hitch under the jaw.
How do I get the reward ? Wait a day or two
after the ad. comes out; then send someone to
them to find out what reward is offered. If suit-
able, send a man in with the lost property. He
pitches a tale that he found them making back to
Bathurst, or Bulb, or somewhere distant. As a
rule they stump up gladly. Some of them grumble
a bit sometimes.
Mostly I get £2 for each horse, £1 for each cow ;
depends on their value. When you reckon the
hands I keep, paddock rent, and my own time, it
doesn’t seem much ; but you must remember I do
a big turnover. Besides, there’s pickings. Very
often it’s safe to resell a £2O horse for £l5 ; and he
only costs me a week or two’s grass and minding.
What happens if they don’t part up ? Well,
they can’t get them, that’s all. I’ve got my living
to make, the same as everybody else. Any of
that sort I send over to a mate of mine who keeps
a pound. He pounds them for me ; as they’re
always pretty good, he puts them up at an off
time, and buys them in for me cheap. Once I’ve
got the pound title for them, the devil himself
can’t take them from me. So I get my profit on
them anyway.
I have one grievance against the pounding sys-
tem. If gazetted in the Police Gazette, any-
thing lost can’t be sold out of a pound in the ordi-
nary way. The pound-keeper must notify the
advertiser, and keep the animal till he comes for
it. So I can’t pound anything gazetted, but have
to keep it paddocked, sometimes for weeks, till
the owner finally offers a reward. This gazetting
is the worst drawback in my business ; I think
the law ought to be amended. If they raised the
gazetting fee to £2 (it is only 3s. at present), it
would suit me nicely.
I find it best to shift stock about between
1 and 5 a.m.; there is never anyone about then,
and they travel better. Anyone constantly at it
develops eyes like a cat; but, of course, sleeping
so much in the daytime you miss the sun glare.
I know of nothing pleasanter than poking along
in the starlight behind a valuable lot of horses
or cows in the warm summer fore-mornings, the
dew fresh on the grass, and the rest of the world
asleep.
Can’t use whips in my business ; always have a
real good, silent biter instead. My old dog Bluey
knows every road and cross-lane this side of the
Mountains. If you skinned him alive you couldn’t
get a whimper out of him. If there is anything
suspicious ahead he runs back, and jumps up
against my stirrup to warn me. The old horse I
ride is just as good and knowing as a Christian.
See him sidle up innocently to a strange horse ;
he will take you alongside so you can whip a
halter on it before it knows what’s coming. And
see him after stock in timber ; you can cut out on
him Without a bridle. I wouldn’t take £lOO for
him old as he is.
Where I made a lot of money was in the Boer
war. Raked the whole State over for horses;
fixed up their brands and marks, gave them a
week’s spell, and put them off on the buyers.
Simply coined money whilst it lasted. And the
best of it was none of them ever came back. There
were a dreadful lot of people looking for lost
horses that time. I slipped some fair outlaws in
amongst them too. Ah, I’d like to see a fresh
war every twelve months !
I am thinking of selling out of this business, as
times aren’t what they were. What with motor-
cars, bikes, and the Labor members, hardly
anyone keeps a horse worth planting nowadays.
I have been trying ringing-in racehorses lately,
and find it a very payable business so far. I have
discovered a permanent dye and bleach at last;
can make piebalds, white stockings, or any color,
to order. So think I will put this business on the
market. Will sell it out in one line for £5OO
cash —plant, goodwill, and all.
FALDER.


DYSPEPTIC DEBILITY
A Harrowing Tale of Suffering and a
Thankful Tribute from Ultimo.
The Case of MR. V. BORWICK.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
Mr. Victor Borwick is a storekeeper who has his business premises and residence at 286 Crown
Road, Ultimo, and although he could have been found at this address at any time during the past
11 years, it was not till lately that a reporter had occasion to pay his establishment a visit, “the
reason for calling,” as the scribe explained, “ being to get some information respecting a widely
known recovery that happened to come within hearing.”
“Ah ! It is six years ago now since I got all right again,” Mr. Borwick remarked, “and I think
it was about up to me to have a change of luck, considering that I had been suffering for fully three
years. The pains that ran along my gums and reached up to the top of my head were something
terrible, and half my time I did not know what I was doing.”
“ Is that the only ailment you have had ?”
“ Indeed, no. All the time the neuralgic tortures were at me I had other troubles to deal with,
for my digestion was just as bad as it could be. Both from want of appetite, and also owing to the
pains about my jaws, eating was almost out of the question ; but what was still more unfortunate
was the fact that the little I did force myself to take to keep body and soul together had the unhappy
habit of disagreeing with me. And you must remember that I was getting no sleep. Neither night
nor day—for weeks at a stretch sometimes —could I get any rest, and, as you may well imagine, the
result was disastrous. I got so terribly weak that ultimately I had to give up my work altogether,
and my body was so denuded of flesh that my clothes hung about me like a sack. It was through
reading somebody’s testimonial that I decided to give Clements’ Tonic a trial, and my earnest hope is
that other sufferers, for their sake, will be so influenced when they read mine, and then I shall
consider I have performed a valuable service to the community. I can give Clements’ Tonic a special
word in respect to its invigorating influence over my nervous system. It cleared every trace of ner-
vousness right way, and as an appetiser it was simply marvellous. Eat ! Why, when once I got
going I hardly knew when to stop, so no wonder I soon filled my clothes out again. But it was all
good, sound flesh I put on, plenty of strength and vigor behind it, and after using several bottles of
Clements’ Tonic there was hardly an ache anywhere to mar the wonderful cure I had made. What
little discomfort there was left after using my first lot of Clements’ Tonic was soon dispersed by my
continuance of the same remedy, and if anybody doubts the completeness of my recovery, all they
have to do is to drop in and see me, and I will give them my experience verbally from end to end.”
“And in the meantime may I circulate your story ?”
“You are at liberty to publish it in any conceivable style you wish.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, VICTOR BORWICK, of 280-288 Crown-road, Ultimo, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have read the annexed
document, and that it contains and is a true and faithful aocount of my illness and cure by Clements’
Tonic, and also contains my full permission to publish in any way my statements—which I give
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Sgd.) VICTOR BORWICK.
Subscribed and declared at Ultimo this twenty-third day of August, one thousand nine hundred
and four, before me, W. J. KYLE, J.P.

The reward of merit. The fishermen of
Gloucester, U.S.A., concerning whom Kipling
wrote in his “Captains Courageous,” look upon
him as a Jonah. Of all the vessels of the fleet of
which he wrote in his book only two remain. The
others have, one by one, gone to the bottom.
The trial, at Adelaide, of Brown, the
alleged criminal lunatic who recently led the
police a wild whirling dance through the hills,
was of an exciting character. Brown had
previously been serving eight years in the
Stockade for house-breaking, but had either
shammed insanity, or acquired the real article,
and been sent to the asylum. Thence
he escaped about Christmas time, and having
stolen some raiment outside, he proceeded to whirl.
When caught, he nearly destroyed a policeman,
and at his trial he wrecked several more police-
men, kicked a door off its hinges, and insisted
upon standing on a chair in the dock. He got 10
years, and promised quite coherently to murder
Justice Homburg when he came out.
“ Nil ” about an episode :
I was sitting with an iced whisky and water late at
night during a heat wave.
She came to the open door and looked at me shyly.
She was dressed in the al—.
I looked at her—but not shyly. Slowly, demurely she
drew nearer, still smiling. I did not know just what to
do, so I went on reading. Nearer. I wondered. Then,
with a swift movement, she was on my knee and making
seductive pats on my shoulders.
Naturally, I was' embarrassed. I said “ Hem ! ” and
looked at my iced whisky and water. Also, I felt for my
pipe. You see, my wife had gone to bed. Daudet is
good at these situations ; I am not.
Her eyes were half shut and gazing languorously into
mine. I could feel her heart beating against my open-
work singlet. What next ? Would she go to sleep ?
It is not easy to go on reading, with someone on your
knee, looking unspeakable things into your eyes, and whis-
pering intoxicatingly into your ear.
“You really shouldn’t,” I murmured. She sighed.
“ What would say if she saw you ? ” I asked, inanely.
It was 2 a.m. The gas was nearly finished (carbide I
burn), and with a supreme effort I took her by the waist,
lifted her off my knee, carried her to the window, and
threw her out.
It was tue cat.


THE ZOBO.
. . . ANYONE CAN PLAY IT.
The Zobo is a new invention, made on the same
principle as the Kazoo. It has a rawhide disc on the
inside, and by singing in and against this disc won-
derful music is produced. Anyone can play any tune
upon it ; imitates fowls, bagpipes, animals, etc. Very
popular with singing clubs and societies.
ZOBO BRASS CORNETTO
A Baby with the Voice of a Giant.
Is. 6d. Each (is. Bd. post free); 2 for 3s.
A more unique merry-making- and fascinating: little novelti
would be exceedingly hard to find. It possesses real merit. It
is an intensely amusing: article for adults as well as for children.
It is not a mere toy, as many might suppose, being: substanti-
ally constructed and easily operated. The *sweet and varied
tones possible to be produced with this little device are incred-
ible. No tune ever composed that cannot be accurately pro-
duced. It is just the thing- for duets, quartettes, chorus,
carnivals, masquerades, serenades, parades, etc., It.may.be
accompanied with various other instruments with very pleasing
effects, and never fails to make a hit. Remember, that if you
do not obtain satisfactory results the first minute, it is no fault
of the instrument. Read over the directions, and persist a
little, when you will be delighted with the results. Best article
to be had for the money.
DIRECTIONS.
Place the stem against the lips, or hold between the teeth as in
the act of smoking. Simply HUM the air of Anv Tune into the
stem. DO NOT BROW. The notes are produced by vibra-
tion, the voice alone causing the vibratory diaphragm to act,
reproducing and modifying any sound emitted against it. The
tones thus produced resemble those of various instruments, such
as the Cornet, Clarionet, Saxophone, Bagpipes, and many others.
Very little practice is required by even a child to master the
instrument; there is a little “knack” to it, that’s all.

“Roma ” : Lately came across a lady who
is growing in an abnormal way. Up to 36 years
of age she was of medium build and figure, took
4’s in boots, and 6’s in gloves. After that age,
she commenced to grow in every way. Now she
can’t get ladies’ boots large enough to fit her—B’s
are nowhere; her hands have lengthened and
broadened to correspond, so that women’s kid
gloves are out of the question. She is several
inches taller than formerly, and very masculine in
appearance. The doctors can do nothing for her
so far. One Melbourne doctor says he heard of
one other similar case. The poor lady
in question is afraid that she will outrival
Abomah. She denies herself all but the barest
necessaries in food, but still she grows in length
and width.
“Old Octopus,” so Melbourne people,
who are not shareholders, name the Tram Com-
pany. “Old Octopus” he is, and with feelers
running into a good many places besides the
public streets. Never a council meeting or con-
ference of councils to consider tram construction
but “Octopus” or his agent is in the midst of
them, glorifying the beneficence of private
enterprise, proclaiming the disaster which
must follow on Government or municipal enter-
prise in those matters. In the newspaper offices,
with one hand ready to clap on any impertinent
fellow’s mouth, “Octopus” holds his own well, and
gives us-to-day just the one penny ride he was
compelled under the Act to give four-and-twentv
years ago. “ I’m poor, nowadays, ’ says Octopus,
‘‘can’t afford to even think of penny sections.”
He stands thus, as a matter of fact96o,ooo
shares £1 each, paid up to 10s., £480,000 ; present
value at 265. 3d. a share —£1,260,000. Profit
annually divided £60,000, equal to 24 per cent,
on the real capital, because of the paid capital
of £480,000 no less than £230,000 has been
provided out of profits. And, in addition,
reserve funds accumulating, which will more than
suffice to pay back all the shareholders’ money
when their time expires. Those same shares in the
land boom time touched over £lO each.
“F.J.L.” : An old fellow at Devil’s Hole,
thinking he was about to die, threw what money
he had into the river, dug his own grave, and
burnt down his humpy, with his belongings. He
then lay down in his grave, and was greatly
annoyed next morning to find himself still alive.
Went olf in a rage to the nearest pub. to get on a
spree, but found he’d burnt his credit. Then
tramped to Glen limes, applied for, and got, the
Old Age Pension.
