BRONCHITIS and ASTHMA.
A COMMERCIAL TRAVELLER SUFFERING IN-
TENSELY CURED BY HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE.
HAS SINCE CAMPED OUT AND TRAVELLED
MUCH WITHOUT CONTRACTING A COLD.
Mr. W. G. Hearne.
Dear Sir, —In 1898, I was for four years previously in
Queensland travelling: representative for a mercantile
house. Having' contracted a heavy cold, I placed myself
under a medical man, and used all kinds of chemists’
prescriptions without avail. I was then staying'at Hayes’
Terminus Hotel, Brisbane. The manager did all possible
for me, for which I shall always feel grateful. Mr. Duncan
Currie, at that time acting manager for the New York
Life Insurance Co., came to see me daily, and he advised
me to try your Bronchitis Cure. I was' about done for,
could not eat, or scarcely swallow ; in fact, used to nearly
suffocate a dozen times a day, suffering' intensely. Guess
of ; my thankfulness, when Mr. Currie brought me a bottle
of your Bronchitis Cure. A few doses actually cured me.
I Stook another bottle away from the city with me, and
part of it to,a young girl at Southport, and it also
saved her life. The balance I gave to Mr. T. McMillan,
Tweed River—three persons cured by your valuable
nfedieine. Although I have camped out and travelled
about 10,000 miles since my illness, I have not contracted
a cold since.—l atn, Sir, your best well-wisher,
THOS. ROSS,
Labasa, via Suva, Fiji.

Posted Missing!
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
HERE’S a black cross a-marking of her
I name upon the sheet,
The underwriters’ features they are any-
thing but sweet.
Their grief is not excited for the safety of the
crew—
They’re adding up the premiums on the barque
that’s overdue;
And a far wind’s blowing,
And a lone sea’s flowing
On a black and barren shore ;
But the sailormen so cheery,
And the captain and his dearie,
They are “ missing ” evermore.
There’h a young gal a-waitin’ in a house beyond
the hill.
There’s aye mg gal a-waitin’ and a-hoping, hoping
still,
They were married as he left her, but afore her
baby’s born,
He’s lyin’ quiet somewhere to the west’-ard of
Cajse Horn.
With a long wash breaking,
When the west wind’s making,
He’ll be restin’ evermore,
By his messmates dreaming,
While the pale light’s streaming
Through green fathom's to the floor.
There’s a gray widow weeping in a house beside
the Quay—
A rover’s chair is empty and for evermore
’twill be—
There’s a gray widow weeping, though she,
struggles hard to pray,
For the lad who made the sunlight in her cold,
winter’s day.
Oh, the sea-birds, flying
From the long Night, crying,
Are the only ones that know
Where a lost ship’s waiting
For her Dead Men’s freighting
To the Port where Lost Ships go.
'I 1 was a mornin’ fair and sunny when we took an’
towed her down
Phst the pretty harbor places to the gates of Syd-
ney town,
With her thousands’ worth of fleeces, a 3 we laid
her on the track—
’l 1 Was “A safe and happy voyage!” and “A
pleasant passage back ! ”
But the Long Night’s found her,
And the sea growth’s round her,
In a silent mourning wave,
And the fish, with eyes of wonder,
In their shining shoals down under
Dart and gleam about her grave.
Did a cry of danger wake them in the watches of
the night ?
Did they see the darkness redden with a great
fire’s growing light ?
Did the white squall strike them blindly with the
quickness of a sword,
Did she lay a-leak and wounded like a poor death-
stricken bird ?
Did a mad surf roaring
O’er-her rent decks pouring
Choke the lost crew’s hopeless cry,
Where the wheeling seabirds only,
On some barren coast and lonely,
Saw the last strong seaman die ?
An old man sadly musing in a house behind the
trees,
A gray wife sitting brooding on such hopeless
thoughts as these,
A childless widow weeping by an ever-waiting
chair,
A young girl playing softly with her orphaned
kiddie’s hair —
When the Night drifts forward,
And the rain sweeps shoreward —
With a salt breath, fresh and free—
Through the raindrops slowly falling,
They will hear the distant calling
Of the Voices of the Sea.
There’s a black cross a-marking of a ship’s name
on the sheet
In the little room at Lloyds, where the shipping
merchants meet;
They’ll reckon up their money on the barque
that’s posted “Lost,”
Regret the lack of salvage, and bewail the sordid
cost —
But a lone wind’s blowing,
And a far sea’s flowing,
And ’tis blithe to be ashore ;
But the long, long Night shall cover
Aye the husband, son and lover,
And their good ship evermore.
N.S.W.
E.J. BRADY.
The Story of Hans.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SVEN owned der schooner, Shling
' Olsen vas mate, und Borka, de Finn,
rushed de cedar aboard out ob de bush.
We vas all partners in de leedle sawmill on the
flat. We tink ob nodin’ but cedar, de corned
beef cask, und rum.
Borka talk a lot about England, und vat a great
island it vas, until we all got sick ob de cedar
trade und de grog shop down de river.
“Let us go to dis England,” says Shling.
Und eferybody says, “Yes, let us go! Let us
go nord vere de geese vas trailin’ up der fiords
und der ice vas boomin’ in der Belt.”
Und so we lay in stores und a new rig-out, und
avay we goes. We make good weather to der
Leu win und Kerguelen. Den we meet de Cape
birds und der green seas, und der vind get hold
ob us like a big Englishman mit wet hands.
Den one day Sven hit de compass a blow mit
his fist. “Vere vas we now, do you tinks?” he
says.
“Someveres bv Finisterre,” I says, for we haf
left der tropics m.der our feet und fetch der grey
skies und Channel rain.
One day we see a big black ship come from de
sky-line. De smoke about her vas like a,town.
“We are in her way,” I says.
“Veil, stop der ondil she speaks.” Und Sven
stood for’rd mit a rusty chronometer in his hand.
De big ship begin to blow und hoot like a howlin’
lion. “Out ob de way! Out ob de way!” she
screams. I read “Campania” on her hull, und I
knew we vas someveres between Liverpool and
New York.
“Cap’n,” says Sven, “oblige us mit de time ob
day, und vich vay vas id to England ? ”
“East, east, you blackguards ! You vas goin’ to
America,” he says. “Kybuclc,” says Sven, und
we go about quick und lively.
We did meet anoder Yankee ship, und de cap’n,
mit a big cigar in his face, poked de borak at us.
“ Ver vas England ?” says Sven.
“Id vasn’t dere ven I coom past,” says de
Yank. “I noticed id vas a bit muddier in de
Nord Sea. Did id haf a gatepost at de corner und
a garden in front ? ” he ask.
"Dot man vas jokin’,” says Sven. “England
vas dere alride.” We keep de schooner east by
east for anoder week ondil we sight de rocks of

“We want England,” savs Sven’ “Vere is

A leedle while und Sheepy come back mit a big

We go nor’-nor’-west until we meet grey skies

Der vas a smell ob fried fish in de fog, und Sven

Borka und Shling vas by de wheel, und dey
looked hard at de sky und de grey water, und
sneezed. “Id vas like de Baffin Sea,” dey said.
“Der vas seals not far avay.”
Sven looked away Nord ver a grey mountain
vas movin’ from the sky-line upon us. He sprang

to de movin’ mass.
“By Shimmy ! ” said Shling. “ Der Nord Pole
haf broke loose ! ” We look troo der mist und see

“We vas a corpse alride,” said Sven to me.
.“ Der schooner vill not answer de wheel.”
De ice-hill came upon us. De sudden cold
pinched de spine und stiffened our hands. I

wall slammed upon her sides.
I vas after de Finn like a shot. A narrow

By crikey, id vas cold. Der vas a black tunnel

De Finn, mit his teeth clackin’, says: “My
fader told me always to keep under de lee of a
berg. Dis vind vas from de Baffin Sea. By
cripes, Australia vas nod too warm after all.”
We crawled around de ice-hill after de Finn
und we had to hold by de frozen drip overhead.
Den we get anoder smell ob fried fish und
gravy. Borka stopped on de ledge of ice holdin’
up his finger.
Round de sharp corner vas a big room worn in

“PEN”:I crave permission to remark
a thing or two in your impartial cuss of a paper
About 12 years ago John Cribble, autnor oi
“Black but Comely,’’founded the
sion settlement near Cairns (Queensland ).
abundantly blessed, dying by inches, as his diary
shows, under the stress of labor there
Then his son took up the work, and, in spite ot
storm and shipwreck and hard times, built schools,
and a church—in fact a unique and complete settle-
ment for the niggers. He fought himself m
nremature old age in the battle for tne
T'erruth. As a special token of appreciation
for these labors in the black Vineyard a special
hurricane came on to him recent y, ,
single night, destroyed the results, so far as pro
oertv is concerned, of 12 years’ devoted labor.
was dying of lung disease when
the hurricane came, and Ins comrades, black and
white, had to hang on to wires and ropgtokeeg
a bit of roof over his dying head. He passed
away in the yell of the storm. Now, I want to
know what these things mean ? I shouldthinkif
they mean anything, they are a mark ofspecial
disapprobation of the whole missionary business.
I appeal to your impartial cuss of a paper for an
opinion.


SUMMER-END FAG.
AT the end of Summer, the body, run down by Summer’s heat, needs to be
toned up and prepared to withstand the trying Autumn and Winter
seasons. The liver and digestive system are deranged by Summer’s
heat, and their imperfect operation gives rise to that weakly “ tired-
out ” feeling, which is now so common, and may be called “Summer-end Fag.”
Bile Beans act directly on the liver and stomach, and by thoroughly stimulating
them, tone up the body and prepare it for the coming season. Thousands of
cases prove this. Here is one : —Miss Cecilia Monaro, formerly of Albury, now
residing at 10 Lackey-street, St. Peter’s, Sydney, says : * ‘ Twelve months ago
I began to suffer from intense depres-
sion, severe headaches and tired feel-
ing. For days I was utterly pros-
trated, hardly able to move, and in
no way capable of attending to my
usual duties. The most racking pains
were in my head, and at times I
thought I would go mad. My joints
ached continually, and I was very
bilious. While living in Albury I
consulted doctors, but their medicines
failed to afford the slightest relief.
For about six months I persevered
with their treatment, but it was of no
avail, and I was, if anything, worse
than ever. I wasted away to a mere
skeleton, and my parents became
alarmed at my condition. Seven
months ago we came to Sydney to live. One day a book bearing upon Bile
Beans was placed under our door, and I glanced through it out of mere curiosity.
I noticed the names of two or three friends of mine who testified to the virtues
of Bile Beans, and I determined to give them a trial, and was overjoyed, after
using nearly a box, to find a decided improvement in my condition. Six boxes
completely restored me to health, and those of my city and country friends who
see me now are astonished at the change. lam at present in the enjoyment of
the best of health, am vigorous in mind and body, can eat heartily, and indulge
in refreshing sleep. I have not the slightest hesitation in declaring that my
present state of health is entirely due to Bile Beans, and I shall have the greatest
pleasure in recommending them to my acquaintances. Every word I say can be
substantiated by.relatives and friends in Albury and Sydney.”

Sea Nymph.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HVT a K&uze o’er her charms was laid,
[X Save the white of the frothing sea,
As she danced, knee-deep, where the
wild surf played,
And laughed in her maiden glee.
And over and over the wide seas rolled
And, holding her fetterless so,
Threw her a kiss for her hair of gold,
And a kiss for her breast of snow.
Yea, morn on morn, with the East afire,
They smiled on her sunlit face
As she sang old songs with the headland choir
And danced with an infinite grace...
A Pagan came in an artist’s guise
And the world has been glad since then—
For the Sea-Nymph lives with her loyal eyes
Deep down in the hearts of men.
And over and over the wide seas rolled
And, holding her fetterless so,
Threw her a kiss for her hair of gold,
And a kiss for her breast of snow.
Then a preacher came, as a lover clad,
And oh ! but his face was fair;
And oh ! but her virgin heart was glad
As she gave him her life to share.
And over and over the wide seas rolled—
Old friends of the dawn they came—
And still with a kiss for her hair of gold,
Dut—she covered her breast in shame.
The night sleeps deep on the ocean ways
A-roek with the restless stars ;
And, ever and ever, a Pagan strays
To the bounds of the headland bars.
“ Sea-nymph ! Sea-nymph ! ” Do'you hear the call
Oh ! ledges so broad and bare ?
“Sea-nymph! Sea-nymph!’’—when the breakers
fall
Is it wbeds that are golden there ?
N.S.W.
E. S. EMERSON.
With Camels.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE took Long Bill under protest.
Wade lost the camels every day for a
week, in the hope of a better man
coming along and taking the job; but the store-
keeper’s patience was exhausted at last, and he
sent the musterers out to bring them in. Had
old Mac., the ; manager, been home at the head
station we wouldn’t have got that week’s grace ;
for Oordillo was nearly out of rations, and in
the middle of that awful drought. But the
camels could face the track. We had six weeks
to go 400 miles in, and were timed to arrive on
Christmas Eve. Our first hundred miles was over
fairly hard tableland country. Wade was inithe
lead, Long Bill in the middle, and I brought up
the tail. The .weather kept moderately cool, so
Bill, with occasional help, bumped them along till
we got to the Lake.
Then troubles commenced. The bore water went
about five pounds of salt to the gallon, and there
was none other for the camels. We knew we
wouldn’t get much pulling out of them next day,
with hot weather and salt water. It was only a
short six miles to our next camp at Box Flat, but
in between lay the dreaded Lake ' Crossing and
the Cobbler Sandhill.
Wade took the crossing first, and went over in
good style. He was a champion with the whip,
and could whale a beast from the wither to the
rump in one cut. He didn’t have much time for
crackers. The bare green-hide was enough for
him. When he lifted his whip his camels would
bellow like Joe Cook and his crowd when the
closure was applied, and get into the collars
without any loss of time. He didn.t liit ’em very
often, but when he did they knew it.
Well, Wade got over and took 'a pull at the
waterbag and a blow on the other side. Then
Bill’s lot got fair into the crossing and stuck.
They turned round and looked at Bill, who had
apparently run amok. He was prancing from
the leaders to the polers and back again, waving
his whip wildly, and hitting himself as often as
the camels. As I came up I could swear old
Hocket, the leader, turned round and winked at
Darky in the pole. He straightened up a bit,
though, when he caught sight of me with my per-
suader. I was not a champion flagellator like
Wade, but I had an idea that a nice length of
trace chain on a 4ft. pole was a good thing in a
tight place. I sort of reckoned that I would have
many a job like the present before I reached Inna-
mincka, and that I’d better make a good impres-
sion so that Hocket and company would know me
when they saw me again.
“ Now, Bill, you reckless long streak, get around
there on the off side and wave your blanky whip
and yell when I’m ready.” I started on my
jingling, and did more hard work in the next
ten minutes than was warranted by my
wages for a whole day. To make matters
equal I started on the polers, and went up
one side and down the other. I let ’em
stand for a, minute, so that the impression
should sink in, like. Then I stood back by the
polers and whistled. They went into it like
horses. We were nearly across when off came
Bill’s whip. Camels stopped, of course, to see
what was the matter. They always stop if any-
thing happens. You’ve only got to start and fill
your pipe, when you’ll see seven or eight blasted
long necks turned in your direction. Bill’s whip
had a bad habit, of coming off at every tight place.
Then he’d get up on the waggon and tie it on,
while Wade or I flogged his team,through for him.
The slavery of that day I won’t forget for a life-
time. The air was fairly sizzling with heat. If
vou belted the camels too much it meant they
would he down and sulk. If you didn’t belt ’em
they wouldn’t gee. The best way was to frighten
them by brandishing the whip. And that’s not
a cooling operation on a hot day. We used
to bump ’em on a bit, and then get in the
shade of the waggon and. pant and drink
water Bill, of course, must drink too much and
get the cramps bad in his tucker box. I was glad
to see the long fool get up on the waggon.
He was out of the road there, at all events.
However, the longest day must close. We got to
Box Flat that night, and half my troubles were
forgotten when Wade said : “Can you do a nip,
Charley?” We had three apiece, and knew no
more till daybreak.’
Next day we spelled. Bill had recovered, and
was starting in to give us some hints on cam el-
driving, when Wade arose, and, tucking up his
sleeves a little higher, squared off and remarked,
“ Come on, you blanky paper-seller, or whatever
you are! Come on!” he repeated; “I’ll teach
you camels.” Bill was six foot two, and big in
proportion, but as soft as the middle of one of his
own dampers. He didn’t come on. The gleam
in Wade’s eye was bad. Bill apologised, and said
he knew nothing about camels. I seconded his
remarks in good sound colonial.
We flogged our weary way along gradually.
Wade got bad with Bill’s cookery, so we put Bill
on camel-hunting with him in the morning, while
I took a spell at the dishes. It didn’t last long,
though. After losing Bill two or three times,
and having to track him up again, we allowed that
our stomachs would have to stand the strain.
Our feet couldn’t.
We pulled in to Innamincka up to time, with
only a hundred miles more of our journey to do.
There we met with “Whistler,” who had left
Cordillo, and was making south with a cheque.
He was a real good fellow was Whistler, and when
we told him our sad case, he agreed to come on in
Bill’s place. The camels were easier after that.
C.X.G.
The Boots of an Actor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS furious with Footlite, and could
not help letting him see it. At the best of
times the man was distasteful to me, but
when he came to me with his infamous proposal,
I could hardly keep from striking him. Fortu-
nately I have a wonderful command of my
temper.
“ How dare you ! ” I cried, as soon as I under-
stood from his hints what he wanted. “ How
dare you make such a cursed suggestion ! ”
“ But I want the engagement,’* he pleaded, and
I saw the tears well up in his foolish, watery <|yes.
“I must have the engagement, and I can’t go in
these ! ” He pointed despairingly at his broken
boots, and pulled out his handkerchief.
It sickens me to see a man weep. “Bah!" I
said, “be a man, my friend—try to be a man ! ”
The creature had no spirit at all. He wrung his
hands and asked me what he was to do.
“Go to him,” I said ; “go to him yourself like
a man and ask him to lend you the boots.”
“No, no,"-he wailed, “it would be useless.
Ever since that wretched dispute he has never
given me a civil word. The man hates me !”
“ Has he ever told you so ? ” '
I saw him wince. “ You heard the fellow
threaten me the other night,” he muttered, and
fell to pulling at one of his frayed heels.
I laughed.
It seemed to madden him. He jumped up, and
strode up 41 id down the room like a lunatic.
“80,” he hissed, “you refuse—you absolutely
refuse- to help me ? ”
“l‘clo.”
“You won’t do ”
“No,” I said. “I won’t do it. You have the
infernal impudence to ask me to steal ”
He raised a protesting hand. “Not steal, my
dear sir. I didn’t ask you to steal the boots. I
merely ” ——
“Enough!” I cried. “Do you think I have
no memory ? You came to me last night and
asked me—as a favor—to obtain the loan of the
boots for you. I tried to do so; but, as I have
already told you, I failed. Kesterton refuses to
lend his boots to anyone.”
“ But,” moaned the creature, “he owes me
fifteen shillings, and he promised me two,weeks
ago—before the disagreement—that he’d leind me
the boots.” ■ '
“ Well,” I said, testily, “ all I know is that he
now objects to doing so.”
1 “ But he promised ’’ r
“ That, my dear fellow, is no concern of mine. ”
.“It is!”'he cried. “It,is a ‘concern ’ of
;yours!”
, I stared at him. “ What do you mean?” I
‘ demanded. “ Explain yourself.”
: He actually shook his fist in my face. “ You
owe me money yourself!” he shouted. “ You
owe me ten shillings. If I had it I could go out
and buy boots.”
“My dear fellow,” I said, “ don’t be alarmed.
You shall be paid immediately I get an engage-
ment—perhaps to-morrow. lam sorry,” I added,
“ very sorry indeed that I ever borrowed money
from you. I Can assure yoh, however, that I will
never do so again.”
“You won’t get the chance ! ” he yelled. “ You
won’t diddle me a second time ! ”
It was all I could do to keep my hands off him.
“ Oh, confound your insolence,” 1 cried, anyone
wpuld think I was a swindler the way you yell at
me. I suppose you think because I owe you a
few paltry shillings I’m going to steal the man’s
boots for you so, that you may go and secure the
very engagement ”
“ It’s a lie ! ” he shouted ; “ a lie ! ”
I managed to keep my temper.
“ That will do," I said coldly, “ I don’t wish to
hear anything further.”
Then I withdrew. I heard him using some
most abominable language as I went out.
For what happened the following morning I
have only my own clumsiness to blame. I knew
of course, when Kesterton refused to lend me the
boots, that he had the Koyal engagement in view,
but as he had owed me half-a-crown for six
months, and having obliged him in a similar
matter of foot-wear some time before—a service
for which he had not made any return—l felt
fully justified, in view of his brutal refusal, in
doing what I did. •
I had secured the boots by crawling across the
floor and wriggling in under the bed, and had just
succeeded in putting on the right one,, when,
turning to pick up the left, I lost my balance and
fell with a thud to the floor.
In a second that ungrateful wretch was out of
bed grappling with me and shouting for help. I
had almost wrenched myself free, when in
bounded Footlite, and the two of them set on to
me like wild beasts. Between them they threw
me to the floor and tore the boots from me, and I
had the mortification of being hustled out on to
the landing attired only in shirt and trousers, and
with my nose bleeding profusely. The scoundrels
had beaten me.
I regret to say that Kesterton got the engage-
ment.
